SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises two agriculture and agro-industries projects
in two of Yugoslavia's less developed republics, Montenegro and Macedonia.
The projects would help achieve the agricultural development objectives of
the two Republics, namely: to increase agricultural production both for the
growing domestic market and for exports, to raise productivity and income,
particularly on private farms, to expand agro-industrial processing capacity,
to improve product quality and to stabilize food supply. They address the
major agricultural development constraints, i.e. the lack of capital for
large investments in land development, irrigation and food processing in the
social sector, and the lack of market outlets and credit for the purchase of
on-farm investments in the private sector; they would also help strengthen
the organization and appraisal/supervision capacity of the two borrowing
banks, Investiciona Banka, Titograd (IBT) and Stopanska Banka, Skopje (SBS).
The project investments have high priority in the 1976-80 Agricultural
Development Plans of the two Republics. They are in line with the Bank's
lending strategy for agriculture in aiming at production growth, expansion of
processing capacities, support for private farmers and institution building.
ii.       The projects would provide credit through IBT in Montenegro and
SBS in Macedonia to eleven social sector enterprises and some 1,000 private
farmers for investments in agricultural processing facilities and primary
production. Processing investments would include wine and wine-by-product
processing, cold storage, vegetable and fruit processing, vegetable collection,
tobacco drying, dairying and slaughtering facilities; in primary production
the project would finance irrigation facilities, vineyard and fruit tree es-
tablishments, livestock and vegetable production, and on-farm mechanization.
iii.      The two borrowers would be IBT for the proposed loan to Montenegro
and SBS for the loan to Macedonia. A project agreement would be concluded
with the "Agrokombinat July 13" (AK) in Titograd, which would implement the
Montenegrin project. SBS would be responsible for implementing the Macedonian
project.
iv.       The Montenegrin project would cost Din 1,001 million (US$55.6 mil-
lion) of which 36% or Din 362 million (US$20.1 million) would be foreign
exchange. The Bank loan of US$26 million to IBT would finance the foreign
exchange cost and interest during construction on the Bank loan of US$5.9
million. The sub-borrower would contribute 5% of the project cost and IBT
would finance the remaining 59% of project cost, of which 20% would be out of
resources from the Federal Fund for the Less Developed Regions (FNP) and 39%
from IBT's own funds. Interest during the grace period on the FNP funds would
be capitalized.
v.        The Macedonian project would cost Din 1,008 million (US$56 million)
of which 37% or Din 378 million (US$21 million) would be the foreign exchange
component. The Bank loan of US$24 million to SBS would finance the foreign
exchange cost and interest during construction on the Bank loan of US$3 mil-
lion. The sub-borrowers would contribute 20% of the project cost. SBS would
finance the remaining 43% of project cost as well as interest during construc-
tion on its loans.



- ii -
vi.       The onlending interest rate to sub-borrowers on IBRD funds would be
11% p.a. in Mlacedonia. It would be 9% p.a. to the AK in Montenegro plus a fee
of 2% p.a. for guarantee and other charges resulting in an effective rate of
11% p.a. The interest rate on local funds would be the local rate for similar
investments prevailing at the time the subloan is made. The maturity for IBRD
funds would be 15 years including a 3-year grace period. The repayment and
grace periods on the local funds in Macedonia would be determined on the basis
of each sub-project's cash flow projection to suit the requirements of the
sub-project. In Montenegro the maturity would be 20 years including a grace
period of 3 years for FNP funds and 8 years including a grace period of 3
years for IBT's own funds. The foreign exchange risk would be borne by the
sub-borrowers for sub-loans to the social sector and by the Government of
Macedonia for sub-loans made to private farmers.
vii.      The processing facilities (US$18.7 million in Montenegro and US$43.7
million in Macedonia) would be procured by the respective sub-borrowers under
international competitive bidding procedures according to IBRD Guidelines,
under supervision of IBT and SBS, respectively. The civil works and equip-
ment for the irrigation facilities, infrastructure investments and collection
center (US$10.7 million) as well as investments for plantation establishment
and on farm-development (US$38.5 million) would be phased over four years and,
due to their relatively small individual size, are not likely to be attractive
to foreign firms. These items if exceeding US$50,000 would be procured under
local bidding procedures, which have proven to be satisfactory to the Bank;
items costing less than US$50,000 would be procured through existing commer-
cial channels.
viii.     The projects would contribute to achieving the objectives outlined
in the Agricultural Development Plans of Montenegro and Macedonia. They
would increase production of mainly fruits and vegetables, wine, beef, lamb
and milk and milk products, improve food product quality and help stabilize
food supply. While the projects' incremental production would largely be
marketed domestically, exports of meat, fruits and vegetables would increase
foreign exchange earnings by about US$100 million over the projects' lifetime.
Total net foreign exchange earnings and savings taking into account the proj-
ects' import components would be about US$60 million (at 1976 prices). The
projects would generate about 1,800 permanent employment opportunities and
reduce underemployment for an additional 23,600 farmers. Almost 55% of the
proposed loans would directly or indirectly benefit an estimated 23,600 small
farmers of which an estimated 12,000 farmers would belong to the target group
with a per capita income of less than US$340 per year. The economic rates of
return for the Montenegrin and Macedonian projects, including administrative
and training expenses would be 17% and 26%, respectively. These rates do not
reflect non-quantifiable benefits including those from improved resource
allocation due to training and institution building.
ix.       Having received satisfactory assurances as summarized in Chapter VII,
the projects would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$26 million, equivalent,
to IBT and US$24 million, equivalent, to SBS, both for a term of 15 years,
including a grace period of 3 years.



APPRAISAL OF
AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MONTENEGRO*
AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES II PROJECT - MACEDONIA*
YUGOSLAVIA
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia and
the Governments of the Socialist Republics of Montenegro and Macedonia have
requested Bank loans for two Agriculture and Agro-Industries projects. They
would provide credit for agricultural processing facilities, including wine
and wine-by-product processing, cold storage, vegetable and fruit processing,
vegetable collection, tobacco drying, dairying and slaughtering facilities;
they would also finance irrigation facilities for the establishment of
vineyard and fruit plantations, as well as investments in livestock, fruit
and vegetable production, and on-farm mechanization. The projects cover both
private farms and social sector enterprises in the less developed Republics
of Montenegro and Macedonia. They aim at expanding agricultural production
and processing capacities in the two Republics; at improving the marketing
of agricultural products, particularly of private farmers' production; at en-
hancing through credit and extension the productivity and income of the rural
population, particularly of private farmers, and at strengthening the appraisal
capacity of the borrowing banks. The projects' investments are of highest
priority in the 1976-80 Agricultural Development Plans for Montenegro and
Macedonia (Annex 1).
1.02      The projects would represent the fourth and fifth Bank loan for
agriculture and agroindustries in Yugoslavia, following the first Agroindus-
tries Project in Macedonia (894-YU) in 1973, the first Agricultural Credit
Project (1129-YU) in 1975 and the Metohija Multipurpose Project (1360-YU) in
1977. Other IBRD loans for multipurpose projects included small agricultural
components. The Montenegrin project was prepared by the FAO/IBRD Cooperative
Programme and the Macedonian by .Stopanska Banka Skopje.
1.03      This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission in
May/June 1976, consisting of Messrs. Heidhues, Bhargava, Wolffelt and Ms.
Hashimoto (Bank) and Messrs. Gioia and Weinschenck (Consultants).
*    In the Annexes of this Report the two projects together are also referred
to as AGRICULTURE AND AGROINDUSTRIES II PROJECT YUGOSLAVIA.



-2-
II. THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
Geography and Climate
2.01      Montenegro and Macedonia are both characterized by extremely moun-
tainous terrain covering 93% of the total area of Montenegro and 75% of
Macedonia. These areas are unsuitable for field crop production, but ex-
tensive upland pastures make possible sheep and some cattle grazing. The
plains and valleys suitable for cultivation allow intensive grain, vegetable
and fruit farming. Two basic climatic zones common to both Republics largely
coincide with the area delineation between mountains and plains - (i) the
Mediterranean (or modified Mediterranean) in the eastern and central parts of
Macedonia and the coastal belt in Montenegro, and (ii) the continental climate
zone prevailing in the mountainous regions of the two Republics.
Agriculture in the Economy
2.02      Agriculture contributes 15% to the total GMP 1/ of Montenegro
and 23% to that of Macedonia, and absorbs 40% and 32% of the active working
population of the two Republics respectively. Though agriculture's role has
been declining relative to the rest of the economy with the rapid progress of
industrialisation, it still remains an important sector in the overall devel-
opment of the two Republics.
2.03      Agricultural production expanded at an average annual growth rate
of 2.8% in Montenegro and 3.6% in Macedonia between 1966 and 1975. In both
Republics, fruit and vegetable production grew most rapidly, encouraged by
the rising relative prices and increased availability of irrigation facili-
ties. The increase in vegetable production was achieved largely by area
extension rather than by yield improvements.  On the other hand, the active
agricultural population declined by 1.5% annually in Macedonia and by 1.7% in
Montenegro between 1961 and 1971. The net result of production growth and
labor input decrease was the growth of labor productivity by an average annual
rate of about 4.5%.
Organization of the Agricultural Sector
2.04      Agriculture of Montenegro and Macedonia, like that of the rest
of Yugoslavia, is characterized by the parallel existence of two sectors -
social and private.
2.05      The social sector consists of agricultural kombinats, state farms
and agricultural cooperatives (Zadruga) and other organizations such as
veterinary and research stations. These organizations operate under the
principles of social ownership of the means of production and workers'
1/   The GMP concept differs from the GNP concept, as the former excludes
certain services, such as public administration, defense, education,
health and social insurance.



-3-
self management. In 1973, the social sector held 5% (Montenegro) and 2:
(Macedonia) of the cultivated land; employed 1.9% (Montenegro) and 8.8%
(Macedonia) of the agricultural labor force; produced 8% (Montenegro) and 30%
(Macedonia) of the agricultural product; and contributed 35% (Montenegro) and
45% (Macedonia) to the market production in the Republics.
2.06      The social sector has been the dynamic force in the development of
modern agriculture in Yugoslavia and, in both Republics, its performance has
been remarkable. In Montenegro production in the social sector rose at a
trend rate of 5.4% between 1962 and 1975, while in Macedonia, the social
sector recorded an average rate above 9% between 1965 and 1975. The main
explanations for the rapid growth of the social sector in agriculture are:
(i) heavy concentration of investments in this sector, absorbing more than
two-thirds of the gross fixed investment in agriculture in the post war
period; (ii) a much greater "intellectual input" of university trained workers
in the social sector; and (iii) much greater emphasis on research in the social
sector.
2.07      The private sector in both Montenegro and Macedonia occupies by far
the larger part of the cultivated land and employs more than 95% of the total
agricultural labor force. It consists of 62,000 holdings with an average size
of 4.9 ha in Montenegro and of about 160,000 farms with an average size of 3.1
ha of cultivated land in Macedonia.
2.08      The production in the private sector increased by 3.2% in Macedonia
and by 2.5%-3% in Montenegro, falling far below the performance of the social
sector. The major causes for the low productivity and yields in the private
sector and the increasing productivity gap between the two sectors are:
(a) a generally lower quality of land; (b) lower share in the irrigated area;
(c) inferior varieties of seeds, plants and animals; (d) low degree of mech-
anization; (e) low fertilizer input; and (f) small farm size and scattered
holdings.
Cooperation between the Private and Social Sectors
2.09      The private and social sectors are for the most part complementary
rather than competitive. Labor-intensive crops, such as vegetables and
tobacco, are almost exclusively produced in the private sector, which also
has 95%-98% of the cattle and 92%-99% of the sheep. Cooperation between the
social and private sectors takes the form of trade in inputs and output,
provision of services by the social sector such as supply of fertilizers,
harvesting, cultivations, marketing and credit and the contracting of joint-
production. While such cooperation brings considerable benefits to the private
farmers, it is fairly limited and declining - limited because of the concen-
tration of social sector organizations in the valleys and high plains and
consequent lack of cooperation possibilities in the mountains; declining
because of the increasing importance of off-farm income of private farmers
and the dissemination of new production techniques in the private sector, both
factors that make contract cooperation far less attractive to private farmers.



-4-
Marketing
2.10      As elsewhere in Yugoslavia, Montenegro and Macedonia have "organized"
markets with "authorized purchases" by social sector enterprises existing side
by side with "informal" markets where the peasants sell directly to consumers.
2.11      The importance of the organized markets is relatively high in
Macedonia because of the orientation of large parts of the private sector's
production for delivery to other parts of Yugoslavia or to exports. In
1974, 42% of wheat, 27% of tomatoes, 63% of grapes, 58% of eggs and 73% of
mutton were sold in the organized market. Private farmers also form groups
to ship significant quantities (especially of early vegetables) directly to
local markets of larger cities in the northern Republics. In Montenegro the
role of the "organized market" has been less significant, accounting for less
than 10% of total production going through the "organized market", reflecting
the predominantly subsistence nature of agriculture in Montenegro.
The Agroindustries Sector
2.12      In Montenegro, due to its mountainous topography, agricultural
production is low and communications are difficult for effective collection
of produce to feed industry. The main agricultural production area is the
Cemovsko Polje plain, which will be developed under the Project. Macedonia,
on the other hand, is a major producing region within Yugoslavia. Never-
theless, the share of Macedonia in Yugoslavia's total processed vegetable
production has declined from about 25% in 1960 to 15-16% in recent years.
This is largely due to the fact that the expansion and replacement in the
processing industry has not kept pace with the expansion of fresh vegetable
production. Both Republics have an undeveloped agro-industrial base.
Development Strategy and Priorities
2.13      Basic Principles.  The basic objective of the Agricultural Develop-
ment Plans for the two Republics is to raise agricultural production to meet
expected domestic needs and to allow for some exports by emphasizing (a) the
further development of the social sector through modernization of production
techniques, expansion of processing facilities, better utilization of avail-
able resources and the adjustment of production to changes in demand; and (b)
the development of private farms primarily through cooperation with the social
sector to ensure further progress in the socialization of agriculture and
rural areas.
2.14      Measures.  To achieve its objectives, the Plans focus on three areas:
(a) price policies, (b) investment policies and (c) support for cooperation
between the social and private sectors.
2.15      Investments.  Annual investments are expected to reach Din 1.5 bil-
lion in Macedonia, more than a 50% increase against the previous plan period,
of which 13% would be in the private sector. In Montenegro, the plan calls



-5-
for Din 1.5 billion investments for the entire period, of which about one-
third is planned in the private sector. Special incentives are foreseen
for encouraging mobilization of enterprises' own resources - income tax
rebates for agricultural investments, turnover tax exemptions, interest rate
subsidies for investment credits, reduction of import duties on imports of
agricultural machinery and increase in the foreign exchange retention quota
from depreciation charges.
2.16      Production Growth.  The Macedonia draft development plan projects
for the period 1976-80 an annual production growth rate of 4.5% for the
agricultural sector as a whole (8.5% in the social sector and 4% in the
private sector). For Montenegro, the growth target is 4.0% for the agricul-
tural sector (7.5% in the social sector and 3.8% in the private sector).
2.17      Evaluation.  The Agricultural Development Plans represent the first
instance in which Yugoslavia's development policy addresses itself specific-
ally to the problems of private farmers. On the other hand, they recognize
the large investment needs in the social sector for irrigation, land devel-
opment and processing facilities and therefore continue to place the major
emphasis on the social sector. The measures outlined for the promotion of
the private sector are: (a) increased cooperation between the two sectors and
establishment or improvement of the extension service in order to achieve a
more rapid transfer of modern technology to the private sector; (b) provision
of credit for on-farm investments.
2.18      These measures address the most serious bottlenecks in the develop-
ment of the social and private sectors. However, the support measures for
the private sector are practically limited to farmers that either form an
association or cooperate with the social sector on a contractual basis. In
this way they will not reach the farmers in the remote mountainous regions,
where the social sector is not represented and where the scope for cooperation
is limited. Rural development investments with emphasis on infrastructure
components would be a pre-condition for improving productivity and marketing
of these farms. In addition, socially oriented programs would have to com-
plement production supporting measures, particularly where the natural con-
ditions are most unfavorable for agricultural production. The five year plan
apparently realizes these difficulties and hence projects low and realistic
growth rates for the private sector. The consequent widening gap in incomes
between the two sectors will reinforce the emerging trend for private farmers
to seek additional sources of income outside the agricultural sector. But
this would present problems for farmers in the mountainous regions who lack
alternative economic activities. To reach these farmers specific measures
would be necessary including improvement of infrastructure facilities and
Government supported credit schemes. The projected growth rates for the
social sector seem realistic considering the relatively large proportion of
investments in irrigation and the corresponding follow-up investments at the
farm level.



-6-
III. FINANCIAL AND INSTITUTIONAL ASPECTS
A.  Banks:  The Proposed Lending Channels
Organization and Ilanagement
3.01      The two banks involved in the projects are:  Investiciona Banka
Titograd (IBT), Republic of Montenegro, and Stopanska Banka, Skopje (SBS),
Republic of Macedonia. 1/ SBS was the borrower under the Agroindustries
Project - Macedonia (Loan 894 YU).  Both banks are participating banks in
the First Agricultural Credit Project (Loan 1129 YU) and are borrowers under
the First and Second Industrial Credit Projects. 2/ In each case the Founders'
Assembly, the apex governing body, establishes the banks' business policy
annually. It also appoints the chief executive, i.e. General Manager, and
guides the banks' operations. Changes in management are infrequent, and
professional competence is the main criterion for selection and appointment
at all levels. The staff of the banks appears generally experienced and
capable.
Financial Structure
3.02      Most short-term resources consist of deposits and borrowings from
the National Bank of Yugoslavia through rediscount facilities. The long-term
resources comprise loans from Fiederal and Republic funds, foreign borrowings,
founders' funds and long-term deposits. The short-term commitments generally
match the short-term resources. The long-term commitments of both SBS and IBT
exceed the long-term resources. However, each bank limits its over-commitments
to the amount of resources it is fairly certain to receive as per the Develop-
ment Plan from the Fund for the Accelerated Development of the Less Developed
Regions (FNP) during the period in which it has to meet these commitments.
Also the banks' level of liquidity is monitored by the National Bank under its
liquidity rules.
3.03      The total assets of IBIT and SBS as of December 31, 1975 were Din
10.4 and Din 34.5 billion respectively, representing a growth of 21.5% and
22.4% over the previous year. I'he long-term loan portfolio accounted for
63.3% (IBT) and 55.8% (SBS) of the total assets. Each bank also manages,
at no risk to itself, substantial funds on behalf of Federal and Republic
Governments and socio-political organizations. The current ratios of the
banks are near 1.1:1 and are satisfactory. The long-term debt/equity ratios
are high; 13.7:1 and 6.7:1 respectively for IBT and SBS. The high debt/equity
1/   The "13 July" Agrokombinat, which is implementing the Montenegrin
project, is discussed in para 3.12 below.
2/   Both the SBS and IBT as recent borrowers under the Second Industrial
Credit Project (Loan 1277 YU) are described in more detail in the
Appraisal Report No. 1058YU.



- 7 -
ratios do not pose a danger to the banks' solvency as most of the long-term
debt is loans from Federal or Republic authorities, repayments of which are
tied to the corresponding repayments by the enterprises to the banks. Finan-
cial structure and performance of the banks is satisfactory, keeping in view
that the banks have close ties with their respective regional economies, both
in funding and lending operations and they merely seek to earn sufficient
revenues to cover all their expenses and financial obligations.
Lending Operations
3.04      Both banks operate as mixed commercial/investment banks and their
loan accounts are fairly evenly distributed among enterprises in all sectors
of the economy, indicative of a reasonably diversified loan portfolio. An
important part of SBS's current loan operations deals with the agricultural
sector (of short-term loans 12.4% to agriculture and of long-term loans 8.5%).
In IBT, lending to agriculture comprised only 7% of total short-term loans
and 3% of total long-term loans. Both banks are the major source of credit
to the agricultural sector in their respective Republics.
Portfolio Evaluation
3.05      As agreed under the Second Industrial Credit Project full audits
of both banks will be available in two or three years from now (see also para
5.12). Until the full audit reports including an analysis of the banks'
portfolios are available, the assessment of the quality of their portfolios
is incomplete. However, according to the banks' unaudited balance sheets the
arrears as percentages of loan portfolio have been small (1% and 1.9% for IBT
and SBS respectively) and are fully covered by each bank's provisions against
bad debts (Reserve Fund). An assessment of the banks' exposure to large
borrowers and borrowers in default could not be made since the banks' records
do not show aggregate loans to an enterprise. This is not a problem for SBS
and IBT since they have access to the borrower's financial statement and the
list of its debt whenever a request for grant or renewal of a loan is made.
However, this information is required by the Bank to evaluate the SBS and
IBT's loan portfolio and would be provided under the financial reporting.
Creditworthiness
3.06      Pending full audits, the creditworthiness of SBS and IBT cannot
be fully established along conventional lines. However, for the following
reasons they are suitable borrowers for the proposed IBRD loan: 1/
(a) The founders have unlimited liability towards the banks'
obligations. Creditors of the banks thus have legal re-
course to all assets of founders, many of which are large
industrial enterprises with substantial assets;
1/   Both banks were accepted as borrowers under the First and Second
Industrial Credit Projects (Loans 1012 YU, 1013 YU and 1277 YU).



-8-
(b) The banks are closely integrated with the economy of the
Republics and their liquidation in case of financial crisis
is inconceivable;
(c) The banks have generally qualified and experienced management
and staff. Experience shows that both SBS and IBT's financial
management has been adequate with no significant losses;
arrears have been manageable, and obligations have been met
satisfactorily.
(d) The bulk of each bank's long-term debt consists of Federal and
Republic funds, which are generally allocated as per regional
development plans with repayments by the bank tied to the
corresponding repayments by the enterprises. 1/ Thus the high
debt/equity ratio is not considered dangerous to the banks'
solvency.
Lending Policies and Procedures
3.07      Investment proposals that enterprises submit to SBS and IBT for
financing are appraised and approved in a branch office or in the head office,
depending on the financing plan and/or complexity of the project. Under
IBRD financed projects both banks have considerably improved their appraisal
methods. However, several weaknesses remain, particularly insufficient tech-
nical appraisal of data and specifications furnished by the loan applicants;
inadequate economic and market analysis, and scanty attention to contingency
allowances on cost estimates. Strengthening of the technical and financial
appraisal capability of both banks for agroindustrial lending would be an
institution building objective under the project. Both banks, with some
strengthening in staff, are considered capable of submitting adequately
appraised projects for financing under the project.
3.08      Systematic supervision is directed by both banks only to problem
projects or projects under construction. Supervision is done mostly by the
branches of the banks. Both banks need to supervise projects more regularly
during and after construction to monitor and analyze the actual results
against appraisal estimates, and to take appropriate action where needed.
The supervision work needs to be centrally coordinated at the head office
to cover various facets of the borrower's dealings with the bank and for
effective feed-back to investment and other operational departments. The
procurement and disbursement methods are sound.
On-lending Interest Rates
3.09      Interest rates on loans and deposits are fixed each year by each
individual bank's Assembly of Founders. The banking structure, where the
borrowers are at the same time the bank's major founders (shareholders), has
1/   In case of genuine difficulty in repayment by enterprises in the past,
such loans were converted into grant reducing liability of the bank.



-9-
led to relatively low interest rates. Furthermore, Federal, Republic and Com-
munal funds are provided to the banks at low cost with predescribed margins
for on-lending or are used to subsidize interest rates. While the Yugoslavs
consider low interest rates on investment credits important for encouraging
investments and use subsidized rates to channel resources into priority
areas, low interest rates, inter alia, may have contributed to excess demand
for investments with in the past inflationary effects, to the choice of highly
capital-intensive production techniques, and to overdesign of investment
projects. They may have also negatively influenced resource mobilization.
These issues are addressed in an Interest Rate Study recently completed by
Vojvodjanska Banka under the First Agricultural Credit Project (1129-YU). The
Study will be the basis for a discussion with the Yugoslav Authorities on the
impact of the existing interest rate regime on resource allocation and mobiliza-
tion in the agricultural sector and on appropriate measures to improve resource
allocation and mobilization in the sector.
3.10      Interest rates for long-term loans to agriculture in the two
Republics vary from 5%-5.5% on loans made out of Federal funds up to 11.5%
on loans from the banks' own resources, with most of the lending done at
7%-8%. Interest rates for investments in priority subsectors may be subsi-
dized to even lower levels such as 3% for irrigation in Macedonia. These
interest rates are reviewed and could be revised every year. The present
interest rates imply negative real rates given the high rate of inflation
(25% in 1974, 18% in 1975 and 11%-12% estimated for 1976).
Forecasts
3.11      The investment planning in SBS and IBT is limited to one year
within the framework of their annual business policy. Both banks prepared
five-year projections of their operations and resources based primarily on
the Plan (1976-80) for their regions. However, these projections are ten-
tative and have high margins of error. For the Plan period 1976-80 the
projected resource gaps of 30% and 35% of the total long-term resource re-
quirements of IBT and SBS respectively are high but expected to be filled
largely with unidentified foreign borrowings. However, considering the
caution exercised in use of long-term resources (para 3.02) no difficulty is
anticipated in both banks being able to discharge their financial obligations
under their respective projects. The funds they need for the projects will
come mainly from Federal funds, which are assured under existing agreements.
Also the amount involved is small relative to overall operations of the banks.
The projected income statements and balance sheets (Annex 3, lables 9 through
12) indicate satisfactory debt-service coverage through 1980 and the current
ratio of a little above 1:1. Both banks are also expected to remain credit-
worthy in future years.
B. "Agrokombinat July 13"
3.12      The "Agrokombinat July 13" (AK) is, as the sole sub-borrower under
the Montenegrin project, responsible for the physical execution of the proj-
ect. It was created in 1963 by amalgamation of small agricultural production



- 10 -
and processing units. With its 2,800 employees it is the largest of four
agrokombinats in Montenegro. It: is an autonomous body structured along the
lines of social sector enterprises and managed by its workers, with the
status of legal person with mortgage and sale rights for the assets. Its
workers' council is the apex bocy which decides the basic policy of AK and
is composed of members elected from amongst the employees. The General
Manager is the chief executive cf AK and apointed by the workers' council.
He is elected for four years with the possibility of re-election. The man-
agement of the AK appears competent. The operations of the AK are carried
out by 26 basic economic units (WB Chart 16334). The AK is engaged in a
broad spectrum of activities covering primary agricultural production (crop
production, milk and livestock production, and plantations), agro-processing
industries (slaughterhouses, bakeries, fisheries, flour mills, winery, dairy
and feed mills), and marketing services (cooperative marketing, supermarkets,
retail and wholesale sales centers). It has substantial experience in irriga-
tion, fruit tree and vineyard operations and wine production; it has also
conducted for about six years pilot operations in the project area in vine
yard and fruit tree production, focussing on experiments with grape and tree
varieties, water and fertilizer applications and cultivation methods. The
financial position of AK, although small, is sound and its financial perform-
ance has been satisfactory. In the context of the self management system
prevailing in Yugoslavia, the financial objective of the AK is to maximize
the average net income per worker. Net income increased from Din 38 million
in 1973 to Din 110 million in 1975. The average net income per worker was
Din 35,500 in 1974 and increased to Din 39,500 in 1975.
C. Performance Under the Macedonia Agroindustries and the
First Agricultural Credit Projects
3.13      The objective of the Macedonia Agroindustries Project, to assist in
financing part of Macedonia's Agricultural Development Program relating to
investments in the social sector (livestock, agroindustry, storage and fish
ponds) and the private sector (farm mechanization, plantations, livestock
and vegetable production) was generally achieved up to the Bank's expecta-
tions and appraisal report estimates. Three studies were undertaken under
the project to provide a clearer understanding of private farm operations
and productivity improvement opportunities, marketing prospects for agricul-
tural products and further needs and benefits of various irrigation schemes.
All loan funds (US$31 million) under the Project have been committed and the
implementation of sub-projects is progressing satisfactorily. Disbursements
have reached US$22.80 million, or 74%. Delays in the implementation of some
sub-projects occurred because of procurement problems, which, for the most
part, have been successfully resolved, and heavy cost overruns (63% over
appraisal estimates) accompanied by difficulties in obtaining additional
financing. However, the rate of disbursements has significantly improved
recently and the actual disbursements as percentage of appraisal estimated
disbursements have improved from a low of 38.8% in early 1975 to 90% in
mid-1976. The loan is expected to be fully disbursed by June 1978 as per
appraisal estimate.



- 11 -
3.14      The physical execution of the project has basically conformed to
the number and purposes envisaged in the Appraisal Report. Of the 43 sub-
projects envisaged and actually approved in the social sector, 21 are com-
pleted, 16 are nearing completion by the end of 1976 and 6 have not started
yet. The funds for sub-loans to the private sector through kombinats and
cooperatives are also fully committed and there is a carry over of loan
applications which will be covered under this project and the Agricultural
Credit Project (Loan 1129-YU). The three studies under the project are com-
pleted and are being reviewed. The marketing and small farm credit studies
have contributed significantly to the development and appraisal of this
project. SBS has fulfilled all loan covenants satisfactorily.
3.15      Under the project the Agriculture and Forestry Loan Department of
SBS was reorganized and strengthened to more effectively handle its lending
operations. The appraisal procedures of SBS were streamlined to overcome
the major weaknesses and the appraisal reports have shown consistent improve-
ment, although some weaknesses still remain. The accounting and disbursement
procedures followed under the project were satisfactory. The progress in
developing appropriate management information systems and regular supervision
has been less than satisfactory. Greater attention will be focused on man-
agement information systems, regular supervision and adequate review and
provision for effects of inflation on costs. SBS's borrowing relationship
with the Bank has been helpful in gaining entry into foreign capital markets;
and arranging foreign co-financing for sub-projects.
3.16      The First Agricultural Credit Project included SBS and IBT as
participating banks. They were responsible for implementing the project
in Macedonia and Montenegro, respectively. Of the amount allocated to
Macedonia (US$5.1 million) and Montenegro (US$0.7 million) 49% and 40%
respectively were committed as of December 1976. Commitments for social
sector subprojects are running ahead of those for the private sector, due
largely to institutional arrangements in Yugoslavia, but a substantial pipe-
line of private sector subprojects should ensure the desired balance.
IV. THE PROJECTS
A. Summary Description and Objectives
4.01      The projects would provide credit to support Montenegro's and
Macedonia's long-term objectives of self-sufficiency in and increased exports
of agricultural production. They would make funds available through IBT and
SBS for credits to eleven social sector enterprises and some 1,000 private
farmers for investments in agricultural processing facilities and primary
production. Processing investments would include wine and wine-by-product
processing, cold storage, vegetable and fruit processing, vegetable collection,
tobacco drying, dairying and slaughtering facilities; in primary production



- 12 -
the project would finance irrigation facilities for and the establishment of
vineyard and fruit plantations, as well as investments in livestock, fruits
and vegetable production, and on-farm mechanization. Detailed project
descriptions and investment models are given in Annexes 4-7.
4.02      The projects would help achieve the Republics' agricultural develop-
ment objectives to increase agricultural production for the domestic market
and exports, to expand agroindustrial processing capacity, to improve product
quality, to stabilize food supply and to raise productivity and income, par-
ticularly on private farms. The investments have priority in the 1976-80
Agricultural Development Plans of Montenegro and Macedonia. Major constraints
to the further development of agriculture in the two Republics would be al-
leviated, i.e. for the social sector, the lack of capital for large invest-
ments in land development, irrigation and processing facilities and, for the
private sector, the lack of market outlets and credit for the purchase of
on-farm investments including better quality breeds, seeds, fertilizers and
agricultural machinery. The investments in processing facilities and in fruit
production, livestock and mechanization are expected to result in increased
production of mainly wine, table grapes, peaches, sour cherries, potatoes,
tomatoes, peppers, cucumbers, milk and meat. The investments in wine pro-
cessing, fruit and vegetable production and processing and livestock produc-
tion and slaughtering facilities would also enhance export possibilities.
Expansion of the social sector's processing and marketing facilities would
create improved marketing outlets for private farmers' production and promote
cooperation between the social and private sectors. The projects would also
aim at strengthening the organization and appraisal and supervision capacity
of IBT and SBS.
B. Detailed Features
Montenegro
4.03      Prolect Components.  This project would provide for (i) the estab-
lishment of a 2,000 ha sprinkler irrigation system in Zone C of the Cemovsko
Polje plain, owned by AK and located south of Titograd, using both groundwater
and Lake Skadar water; (ii) land preparation for and planting of 1,300 ha of
vineyards with wine and 200 ha of vineyards with table grapes, 200 ha of peach
and 300 ha of sour cherry plantations, and 60 ha of windbreaks; (iii) 14 km
farm roads, equipment sheds and supporting facilities and experimental research
on new fruit tree varieties (30 ha); (iv) a winery with an annual capacity of
10 million liters including byproduct processing facilities; (v) a 3,000 m ton
cold store with blast freezing, grading and packing facilities for fruits and
vegetables; and (iv) a collecting center with a total of 500 m2 collecting and
storage space in the neighboring area. Preliminary design work on the irriga-
tion system, plantations and winery and cold stores has been completed. How-
ever, on the appraisal mission's suggestion the irrigation system would be
modified to a simpler and less capital-intensive design: the Lake Skadar
water would be pumped into a reservoir from where it would serve the distri-
bution system by gravity, instead of being pumped directly to the sprinkler



- 13 -
network; and the distribution equipment would consist of portable aluminum
pipes and sprinklers, instead of a heavy "boom o rain" equipment. A detailed
description of the individual components is given in Annex 4, para 17.
Macedonia
4.04      Project Components.  This project would include (i) processing facil-
ities in the social sector including a dairy plant, slaughterhouse, potato
cold store and fruit and vegetable processing facilities; and (ii) investments
for the private sector in grape and fruit production, plastic greenhouses and
mechanization for vegetable production, livestock and tobacco drying facili-
ties. Subloans would be provided for buildings and related infrastructure,
processing equipment, agricultural machinery, plantation establishment and
high quality livestock. Investments would be phased over a four year imple-
mentation period. US$100,000 would be provided to finance a feasibility study
for a wholesale marketing center in Macedonia, proposed in the marketing study
conducted under the Macedonia Agroindustries I Project (894-YU). A detailed
description of individual components is given in Annex 6, Chapter A.
C. Cost Estimates
4.05      Total project cost for the Montenegrin project is estimated at
Din 1,001 million (US$55.6 million) of which Din 361.6 million (US$20.1 mil-
lion) would be the foreign exchange component. Total project cost for the
Macedonian project is estimated at Din 1,008 million (US$ 56 million) of
which Din 378.4 million (US$21 million) would be the foreign exchange compo-
nent. Details of investment costs are shown in Annex 5 for Montenegro and in
Annex 7 for Macedonia and are summarized on page 14.
4.06      Cost estimates are based on May 1976 costs.  A physical contingency
of 15% for the irrigation and 5% for the other components (averaging 5.4%) and
a 49.2% price contingency have been added to the Montenegrin project; and an
average 5% physical contingency and an average 30.4% price contingency have
been included in the Macedonian project costs to meet expected inflation in
foreign and local costs during the implementation period.

The Bank loans would be in amounts of US$26 million for the Montenegrin proj-
ect and US$24 million for the Macedonian project (in each instance the loan
is expected to finance 100% of foreign exchange cost and interest during con-
struction on the Bank loan). The loan for Montenegro would be made to IBT,
which is familiar with administrative and disbursement procedures for IBRD
loans, for 15 years including three years of grace for on-lending to AK. The
loan for Macedonia would be made to SBS also for 15 years including three
years of grace for on-lending to sub-borrowers. The local financing for the
Montenegrin project would be provided by IBT (US$34.3 million, equivalent) and
AK (US$3 million, equivalent). IBT's loan would be funded from the FNP fund
(US$12.9) and from its own resources (US$21.4). The FNP funds would be onlent



- 16 -
to AK with a 20 year maturity including a three year grace period on a
"staggered basis", i.e. counting the full grace and maturity period from the
time of disbursement; interest during the "staggered" grace period would be
capitalized. IBT's own funds would have an eight year maturity with three
years of grace, again applying the "staggered" system; interest on these
funds would not be capitalized. The Government of Montenegro would cover any
deficit that may occur in AK's cash flow during the lifetime of the loan. 1/
The local financing for the Macedonian project amounting to 43% of individual
sub-project cost would be provided by SBS; the grace and maturity periods
would be adjusted to the needs of individual subprojects. Subborrowers would
contribute a minimum of 20% of subproject costs. IBT and SBS would also
finance any cost overruns that may occur in their respective projects.
Assurances on these financing terms were obtained during negotiations.
4.08      Both projects require financing of interest during construction.
The Montenegrin project investments in vineyards and fruit tree production have
a long gestation period and, therefore, a slow build up of cash flow as shown
in the project's cash flow projections (Annex 5, Table 4). The individual
processing investments in Macedonia also will not produce revenues for the
first three years and, taking into account their phasing over the project's
implementation period, would require financing of interest during construction
for four years. The subproject cash flows are given in Annex 7, Tables 4, 7,
10, 13, 16 and 19, and Annex 10, Table 2 for the total project. Without the
inclusion of interest during construction financing of US$5.9 million and US$3
million in the Bank's loans to IBT and SBS respectively, the cash flow posi-
tion of the beneficiaries and of the borrowers would be imprudently stretched
in meeting their obligations including the servicing of the proposed Bank
loans. With the inclusion of interest during construction on the proposed
Bank loans and the financing arrangements discussed in para 4.07, the subbor-
rowers' repayments under the two projects would be sufficient to enable IBT
and SBS to repay the respective IBRD loans within 15 years with three years
of grace and level payments of principal.
E. Disbursements
4.09      Bank disbursements would extend over about 6 years in Montenegro
and 5 years in Macedonia. An estimated disbursement schedule is given in
Annex 9. Disbursement of the IBRD loan in Montenegro would be made against
100% of foreign expenditures or 36% of local expenditures, up to a maximum
of 36% of total project cost. In Macedonia the IBRD loan would be disbursed
against 100% of foreign expenditures or 37% of local expenditures, up to a
maximum of 37% of the total cost of subprojects, as well as 100% of foreign
expenditures for the market center study. Disbursement requests would be
supported by full documentation including procurement contracts in both
I/   Such deficit is projected to amount to about US$18 million, equivalent,
during 1982 to 1987 and would require terms similar to those on which the
FNP funds are lent to AK.



- 17 -
Montenegro and Macedonia except for SBS subloans to private farmers for which
IBRD would disburse against certificates of expenditure. For the latter,
documents and contracts would be available for review by IBRD during super-
vision. Assurances on these disbursement procedures were obtained during
negotiations.
F. Procurement
Processing Facilities
4.10      The winery and cold store in Montenegro (US$18.7 million) would be
procured by AK and the processing facilities in Macedonia (US$43.7 million)
by the respective sub-borrowers under international competitive bidding
procedures according to IBRD Guidelines, under the supervision of IBT and SBS,
respectively. These facilities, except the tobacco drying sheds, would be
procured under a turnkey contract since the civil works need to be closely
integrated with the electrical-mechanical equipment. Domestic manufacturers
would receive a 15% margin of preference or the actual level of import duty,
whichever is less, in bid comparisons.
Irrigation Facilities, Collection Centers and Infrastructure in Montenegro
4.11      The civil works and equipment for the irrigation facilities (US$8.7
million) would be grouped into five contracts. The contracts would be phased
over four years and too small to attract foreign firms. The infrastructure
investments and collection center (US$2.0 million) are simple structures
mainly of civil works which due to their relatively small size are not likely
to be attractive to foreign contractors. These items would be procured, by AK
under IBT supervision, under local bidding procedures, which apply nationwide
and which have proven to be satisfactory to the Bank under ongoing projects.
The Bank would receive copies of draft designs for review and all contracts
as well as the bid evaluation reports.
Items for Plantation Establishment and On-Farm Development
4.12      Items for plantation establishment and on-farm development (US$38.5
million) would not be suitable for bulk procurement and international competi-
tive bidding because investments would be phased over 4 years and, in Macedonia,
dispersed throughout the Republic. Subborrowers would, under the supervision
of IBT and SBS, purchase equipment, fertilizer, seeds, planting material,
livestock and construction materials costing less than US$50,000 directly
through existing commercial channels, which include both domestic and inter-
national suppliers. The total of such direct procurement would be about
US$8.5 million. Such items exceeding US$50,000 (totalling about US$30 mil-
lion) would be procured by the sub-borrowers under IBT's and SBS's supervision
through local bidding procedures. The Bank would receive contracts and bid
evaluation reports on all items except those for on-farm investments in the
private sector (see para 4.09). Machinery and equipment dealers maintain
adequate supplies of spare parts, provide services and repair facilities



- 18 -
and are suitably competitive. During negotiations assurances were obtained
that the procurement procedures outlined in paras 4.10 through 4.12 would be
followed.
G. Environmental Impact
4.13      The project is not expected to have significant adverse environ-
mental impact on the project area. All processing facilities would have
adequate effluent treatment facilities.
V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Organization
5.01      The proposed loans would be channelled through IBT for the
Montenegrin and SBS for the Macedonian project. The IBT loan would finance
investments of AK, which would be responsible for the implementation of the
Montenegrin project.  A project agreement with AK would detail its responsi-
bilities. Accordingly IBT's role would be limited, apart form providing part
of the financing, to loan administration and financial coordination and
supervision. On the other hand, the loan to SBS would finance investments of
numerous social and private sector subborrowers and, therefore, SBS would be
responsible for implementing the Macedonian project.
5.02      IBT's organization (with some staff strengthening as discussed
below), financial condition and operating policies are adequate for its role
under the project. IBT's IBRD Operations Unit, which handles all work re-
lating to lending under IBRD financed projects, would also be responsible
for this project. The unit consists of five experienced economists and, for
agricultural work, it draws on the expertise of an agroindustries engineer
and an expert in agronomy/forestry from its Technical Group. Short-term
consultants are hired when needed. To handle the increased work load under
this project, the Unit's staff would need to be increased, to administer
efficiently the proposed loan. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
from IBT that it would appoint to the Unit a qualified and experienced agri-
culturist/agro-industries expert and a senior credit officer by September 1,
1977.
5.03      The Agricultural Operations Department in SBS is responsible for
implementing the first Agroindustries Project. It has sufficient expertise
in agriculture, livestock, irrigation and economic and financial analysis
areas. However, experience under the first Agroindustries Project indicates
continued weaknesses in appraising agroindustry subprojects, particularly
their technical and marketing aspects and in international procurement.
The SBS needs to strengthen the staff in its Agricultural Department by
appointing an agroindustries specialist and a marketing specialist both



- 19 -
to improve the appraisal analysis and to handle the increased volume of
business under the project. It also needs outside technical assistance in
international procurement. Furthermore, the coordination between different
departments of SBS under IBRD financed projects needs to be improved to avoid
delays in project implementation. To carry out effectively the supervisory
and administrative functions with respect to the project, the SBS would form
and maintain a unit staffed by five senior staff members. The unit, to be
headed by an Assistant General Manager of the SBS, would also include the
Director and Assistant Director of the Agriculture Loan Department and a
representative each from the Disbursement and Collection and the Foreign
Exchange Department. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
SBS that it would by September 1, 1977, appoint a qualified and experienced
agroindustries and a marketing specialist, employ consultants acceptable to
the Bank to assist in international procurement and form a coordination unit
as described above. With these proposed additions in staff and organization,
together with its satisfactory financial condition and operating policies
(Chapter III), SBS would be adequately equipped to implement the project
efficiently.
5.04      A Project Unit, organized as a Basic Organization of Associated
Labor (BOAL), would be created within AK for the implementation of the project
in Montenegro. The Unit would include the Project Unit Director and qualified
irrigation engineers, agronomists and procurement specialists and employ con-
sultants for specific technical and international procurement matters. The
Unit would have an executive committee consisting of: General Manager of
the Agrokombinat, who will also be the President of the executive committee;
Director and Division Chiefs of the Project Unit; Director of the internal
bank of AK; and the Director of the Agro-Economic Institute of AK. The
executive committee would be responsible for the Unit's operating policies
and procedures and supervise the project implementation. It would also play
a coordinating role between AK, the Secretariat of Agriculture and IBT. The
detailed organization of the Unit is described in Annex 3. Assurances were
obtained that AK would establish the Project Unit as described above prior to
effectiveness of the proposed loan, and continue to maintain it until comple-
tion of the project. The Director of the Project Unit has been designated
and participated in the negotiations. Assurances were obtained at the time
of negotiations that by September 1, 1977, the AK (i) would appoint other key
staff, appropriately qualified and experienced, and (ii) would hire consul-
tants, acceptable to the Bank, for irrigation work and procurement matters.
After the project's implementation, its facilities would be transferred to
the Agrokombinat's respective operating units. These units are adequately
organized and staffed, and follow satisfactory policies to warrant efficient
operation of the project facilities.
Training and Technical Assistance
5.05      A course in appraisal criteria and techniques used in Agro-
industries and Agriculture credit projects would be organized by SBS. IBT
would be invited among other banks to participate in the seminar. SBS would
also organize a procurement course with participants including staff of the
AK and other social sector enterprises likely to be sub-borrowers under the
project as well as IBT and other interested banks. The courses would be



- 20 -
held prior to March 1, 1978, and their contents would be acceptable to the
Bank. Assucances to this effect were obtained during negotiations. Efforts
would also be made to train SBS and IBT staff in agro-industrial lending
through participation in EDI courses.
B. Policies and Procedures of Projects Sub-lending
Appraisal Criteria
5.06      The IBT and the SBS would appraise the sub-projects and make the
sub-loans following the on-lending policies, procedures, terms and conditions
described in Annex 12. In summary, IBT would review AK's loan applications
taking into account particularly possible changes in the economic and market
environment, and the project's and AK's financial situation. SBS would
appraise each subproject paying particular attention to their technical,
financial and economic viability, to the marketing and supply aspects, the
inclusion of adequate contingency allowances and the creditworthiness of the
subborrowers. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
5.07      Assurances were obtained that IBT and SBS would ensure that the
seasonal credit requirements of each sub-borrower during the implementation
of the sub-project would be met.
On-lending Interest Rates
5.08      The interest rate for on-lending the IBRD funds under the Montenegrin
project would be 9% p.a. plus a fee of 2% p.a. for guarantee and other charges
resulting in an effective rate of 11% p.a. It would be 11% p.a. in Macedonia.
The interest rate on local funds would be the local rate for similar invest-
ments prevailing at the time the subloan is made. The foreign exchange risk
would be borne by the sub-borrowers in the social sector and by the Government
of Macedonia for sub-loans made to private farmers. The banks would take
adequate steps to protect themselves against the exchange risk of the foreign
exchange surplus accruing to them from sub-loan repayments occurring earlier
than amortization payments of the Bank loan. Assurances on these arrangements
were obtained during negotiations.
Free Limit
5.09      The IBT would grant the sub-loans to AK after reviewing the updated
loan applications. The sub-project has been fully appraised by IBRD and
normal control on implementation and design will be exercised through Bank
approval of tender documents and in terms of the Project Agreement with AK.
On these premises no prior approval by the Bank for the granting of sub-loans
to AK by IBT would be necessary, unless a major change in project design
would occur.
5.10      In view of SBS's continued weaknesses in sub-project appraisal
(para. 5.03), it would forward to IBRD its appraisal reports for social
sector sub-projects whose project costs exceed US$1.5 million, for review



- 21 -
and approval. In addition, the SBS would forward to IBRD for approval
also subprojects in the private sector having an aggregated investment cost
above US$1.5 million. This would result in the Bank reviewing about 6-7
subprojects for a total amount of US$ 45.0 million, or about 80% of the
Macedonian project. Assurances to this effect were obtained during nego-
tiations.
C. Project Monitoring
Audit
5.11      The IBT and SBS each would maintain an account for its respective
project. This would include disbursements made under the projects for all
sub-loans.
5.12      As required by Yugoslav law all banks and enterprises are audited
annually by the Social Accounting Service (SDK). Normal audit by SDK, which
is limited to verification of cash balances and checking of financial state-
ments for mathematical accuracy and comparisons with previous years, does not
conform to internationally accepted standards.  SDK has implemented a training
program for its staff, with the Bank's support, to undertake internationally
accepted audits of Yugoslav banks including SBS and IBT.  A reclassification
of the accounts of SBS and IBT was done by SDK for the year 1974 to eliminate
double counting. Interim audits of the 1975 accounts of SBS and IBT have
been done. Full audits with audit opinion on both banks are expected in
about 2 to 3 years as per schedule agreed under the Second Industrial Line of
Credit (Loan 1277-YU). Assurances were obtained from both SBS and IBT that
they would submit audit reports satisfactoty to the Bank, within four months
from the end of each fiscal year. Assurances were also obtained that the
banks will compile and furnish information on their loan portfolios in such
a fashion as to enable analysis of the quality of the loan portfolio.
5.13      The AK would maintain separate accounts for transactions under the
project and submit annual audit reports acceptable to the Bank within four
months of the end of each fiscal year. Assurances to this effect were ob-
tained during negotiations.
Supervision
5.14      SBS and IBT need to strengthen their supervision system to ensure
adequate technical and financial supervision of sub-projects during and after
construction. SBS would also require the social sector sub-borrowers and
the social sector enterprises borrowing on behalf of private farmers, to sub-
mi  SDK certif-` A  inancial statements within six months of the end of each
fiscal year. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.



- 22 -
Reporting Requirements
5.15      The SBS and the IBT would provide the Bank within 3 months after the
end of each quarter and year, respectively, with quarterly and annual reports
covering the progress of the projects, sublending operations under the proj-
ects, the financial statement, including all necessary information required
to assess the quality of their loan portfolio and general data on agricul-
tural and economic policy and market and financial environment affecting
the implementation of the projects. AK would submit through the IBT to the
Bank quarterly progress report on the physical execution of the project.
The SBS, IBT and the AK would set up project monitoring systems by March 1,
1978, to be designed on the basis of the study on project impact undertaken
under the First Agroindutries Project Macedonia and acceptable to the Bank and
would, on completion of the projects, evaluate the project benefits and assess
the economic impact thereof. Assurances to these effects were obtained.
D. Retroactive Financing
5.16      To start implementation expeditiously and to avoid delays AK intends
to appoint consultants for the detailed irrigation design work, order root-
stocks for the first 200 ha of vineyards and purchase machinery for land
development prior to the expected signature date of the proposed loan to IBT.
The appointment of the consultants and procurement of the goods would be done
in accordance with Bank guidelines. Retroactive financing for these expendi-
tures up to an amount of US$300,000 is recommended under the IBT loan.
VI. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
A. Production, Markets and Prices
6.01      Production.  The incremental annual production generated by the
projects would be:

The total value of annual incremental production at full development is es-
timated at Din 650 million (US$36 million) at 1976 prices. The incremental
primary production in the private sector would result from expanded area,
increased number of animals and increased yields ranging from 21% for beef
to 59% for milk (Annex 7).
6.02      Raw Material.  The winery and cold store in Montenegro would de-
pend for their raw material supplies on AK's production of wine grapes, table
grapes, peaches and sour cherries; and on the private farmers' vegetable pro-
duction from the neighboring areas. At full development, 14,700 m tons of
wine grapes would be converted into 9.56 million litres of wine; and 100 m
tons of table grapes, 2,000 m tons of peaches; and 200 m tons of sour cherries
would pass through the cold store. In addition, 3,400 m tons of vegetables
from private farmers would be collected and stored in the cold store.
6.03      The social sector processing units in Macedonia would receive their
raw material from social sector enterprises and private farmers, including
those financed under the project. Processing facilities have been a major
constraint on increased production and marketing, particularly for the private
sector; adequate supplies for the processing plants would be secured.
6.04      Markets.  Most of the incremental production of commodities financed
under the projects would be marketed domestically. With Yugoslavia's GNP
expected to rise substantially and further increases in tourism, the market
prospects for the projects' production are good. Some early vegetables and
fruits, particularly peppers, tomatoes, table grapes, peaches and cherries



- 24 -
as well as a substantial amount of the processed products, notably apple
juice concentrate, apple puree, frozen cherries and processed tomatoes,
would be exported, both to Western European and COMECON markets. The in-
cremental sheep production and the slaughterhouse investments in Macedonia
would assure continuity and expansion of exports to Italy and Greece and
enable Macedonia to exploit more fully new export markets in Arab countries
in North Africa and the Middle East. Expanded tobacco production would
largely supply the domestic market. A detailed discussion of market aspects
of the projects' commodities is given in Annex 2.
6.05      Wine to be produced under the Montenegrin project, equivalent to
about 2% of Yugoslavia's present production, would be marketed mainly domes-
tically. Demand for wine is projected to increase at about 6% p.a. 1/ and
somewhat higher for the project's high quality "Vranac" wine. The strong
demand for Vranac, as indicated by prices considerably above those for other
wines, is expected to continue. While export markets for ordinary wines are
stagnating or even decreasing, about 15% of the project's wine production,
which would qualify for the "appellation controlee" label of the Yugoslav Wine
Fund 2/, would have good market prospects in Western Europe and the United
States.
6.06      Prices.  Farmgate prices as well as farm input prices with few ex-
ceptions have, in practice, been determined by demand and supply. The Gov-
ernment's efforts to contain increasing prices have failed, largely because
of the inadequate price control mechanisms (Annex 1, paras 32 and 33). The
incremental production from the projects, which would result in substituting
for or reducing the growth of imports, or supplying otherwise not satisfied
needs, would not induce a reduction in producer prices since the expected
demand increase is likely to exceed the additional production. Thus rising
farmgate prices due to excess demand are expected, but they would increase
less than without the projects.
B. Financial Benefits
6.07      Under the Montenegrin project, the winery sub-component is projected
to generate a financial rate of return of 13% and the fruit orchards and cold
store component 17.7%. The sensitivity analysis shows that the financial
rates of return would range from 10.1% to 16.2% if the investment, or operat-
ing costs were to increase or the benefits to decrease by 10% (Annex 5). In
Macedonia the financial rates of return of the investments vary between 13%
1/   Demand on organized markets, which are supplied by social sector
enterprises.
2/   The Yugoslav Wine Fund awards the appellation controlee grade in
accordance with the methodology prescribed by the Office International
du Vin, of which Yugoslavia is a member.



- 25 -
and 28% (Annex 7). The sensitivity analysis yields the following: if invest-
ment costs would increase by 10% the financial rates of return would vary
between 11% and 24%; an increase in operating expenses by 10% would result in
financial rates of return between 11% and 25%; and if revenues were to drop by
10% the rates would range from 8% to 21%. All calculations are based on April
1976 Yugoslav prices for both inputs and outputs.
6.08      In Macedonia about 10 social sector enterprises and 960 private
farmers would receive credit under the project. The annual incremental
income of social sector enterprises at full development would be US$28
million, raising the income of the workers' families by an average of about
63%. In the private sector, the farm families' total annual income would
increase by US$4.6 million due to loans under the project. In Montenegro the
annual incremental income of the Agrokombinat is estimated at US$7.5 million.
The average workers' family income is projected to increase by about 55%.
The investment cost per beneficiary family for the two projects would amount
to about US$2,300.
C. Economic Justification
6.09      The Montenegrin project would focus on increasing production in
the social sector. AK would develop 2000 ha of land which so far has been
unsuitable for any agricultural production. During more than five years of
research and trial production AK established the project area's suitability
for grape and certain fruit tree production, which would be undertaken under
the project; it also gained experience in the most appropriate methods of
land development, irrigation, cultivation and fertilizer and pesticide use.
With no alternative agricultural use of the land, the project's economic
benefit to the Montenegrin economy would be substantial as reflected in the
economic rate of return of more than 17%. The project would complement the
Bank's lending under past and forthcoming agricultural credit operations in
Montenegro, which provide credit to private farmers, as well as the Govern-
ment's own allocation of credit to the private sector in the 1976-80 Agricul-
tural Development Plan. With the amounts foreseen for private farmers under
both the Second Agricultural Credit Project, which is planned to be presented
for consideration by the Board later this year, and Montenegro's Agricultural
Development Plan 1976-80, the needs of the private sector would be adequately
covered.
6.10      The Macedonian project would emphasize increasing the agricultural
processing and marketing capacity in the social and production in the private
sector. Apart from credit channelled directly to private farmers, the pro-
cessing facilities in the social sector would indirectly benefit private
farmers by providing marketing outlets and thus alleviating one of the main
constraints to increased production in the private sector.



- 26 -
6.11      Foreign Exchange Earnings.  The projects in concentrating on the
domestic market would, over their 30 years lifetime, generate about US$100
million in foreign exchange through increased exports. Taking into account
foreign exchange savings through import substitution and direct and indirect
import components of investments and operating costs, the total net foreign
exchange earnings or savings are estimated to amount to the order of US$60
million (at 1976 prices). This has been taken into account in the economic
rate of return calculation.
6.12      Employment.  The projects would generate an estimated 1200 man
years permanent jobs in Macedonia and 630 in Montenegro. For an additional
23,600 farmers (those receiving credit plus indirect beneficiaries), the
projects would reduce underemployment and provide means to use more fully
their free capacities in buildings. The additional investments and consump-
tion of inputs, such as fertilizer, pesticides, fuel, via their backward
linkages, and the increased production via forward linkages, would generate
sizeable indirect employment opportunities which were not quantified. The
productivity aspects of employment have been taken into account in the eco-
nomic rate of return.
6.13      Income Distribution.  The projects would finance investments in two
less developed regions in Yugoslavia with a per capita income of about 30%
below the Yugoslav average. Moreover, about US$4.4 million or 18.5% of the
proposed Bank loan to SBS would directly benefit an estimated 2,100 small
farmers in Macedonia 1/, of which about 1,350 farmers would belong to the
target groups with annual per .zapita incomes below US$340. Their incomes are
estimated to increase by about 40% for mechanization investments up to around
130% for livestock farmers.  The processing investments would bestow indirect
benefits by providing marketing outlets for their incremental production to
some 21,300 farmers in MacedonLa and about 170 farmers in Montenegro (Annex
11, Table 4), of which more than half are estimated to belong to the target
groups. The Macedonian project would also assist the efforts to reduce the
income gap between the social and private sectors.
6.14      Economic Rate of Return.  Based on the assumptions discussed in
detail in Annex 11 the economic rates of return were calculated for the
respective projects (see Tables 2 and 3 of Annex 11). The Montenegrin project
would generate a 17.3% rate of return; and the Macedonian project 25.6%. These
rates of return do not take account of the non-quantifiable benefits resulting
from the contribution of the project to improved resource allocation in the
agricultural and agroindustries sectors through training and institution
building. Sensitivity tests were conducted to assess the stability of the
respective rates of return to variations in project cost and benefit streams.
The variations are expected nol: to exceed 10% of the base estimate. The
results are:
1/   Farmers receiving credit, farmers benefitting from the tobacco drying
sheds which are processing tobacco solely supplied by private farmers,
and farmers sharing in mechanization investments.



- 27 -

Investment Costs    +10%               16.0%                23.2%

Benefits             -10%              14.4%                17.0%
Both projects' rates of return are most sensitive to variations of benefits.
However, even under a pessimistic assumption of 10% lower benefits, the rates
of return remain satisfactory.
VII. RECOMMENDATIONS AND AGREEMENTS REACHED
7.01      During negotiations, assurances were obtained from IBT and AK as well
as from SBS that they would implement the respective projects and follow the
financing, disbursement, procurement, lending and reporting procedures discussed
above as well as implement the staffing and organizational requirements
outlined above.
7.02      The conclusion of a loan agreement between the IBT and the AK
securing the local financing and an agreement on on-lending of the Bank loan
to the AK, both acceptable to the Bank, would be conditions for making the
proposed loan to IBT effective. The establishment of the Project Unit within
AK would also be a condition of the loan's effectiveness.
7.03      Having received adequate assurances from the respective borrowers
and AK, the Montenegrin project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$26
million, equivalent, to IBT for a term of 15 years including a three year
grace period; the Macedonian project would be suitable for a Bank loan of
US$24 million, equivalent, to SBS for a term of 15 years including a three
year grace period.

A. The Agricultural Sector
1.        The role of the agricultural sector in the economy of Montenegro and
Macedonia is outlined by the following indicators:

Agriculture contributed 14.9% to the total GMP of Montenegro, and 23% of the
total GMP of Macedonia, and absorbs 40% and 32% of the active working popula-
tion of the two republics respectively. Though its role has been declining
relative to the rest of the economy with the rapid progress of industrializa-
tion, it still remains an important sector in the overall development of
the two republics.
Production
2.        Total agricultural production grew between 1966 and 1975 at an
average annual growth rate of 3.6% in Macedonia, 2.8% in Montenegro. Fruits
and vegetable production increased most rapidly, encouraged by the rising

relative prices and expanded irrigation availability, mainly at the expense
of extensive grain production. Macedonia in addition increased significantly
the production of rice and sunflowers.

3.        In the meat sector, the production of broilers increased most sig-

yields per cow (75% of the incremental production). In Macedonia, milk
production stagnated until 1971 and began to rise since then with increasing
growth rates of 5-6%, based almost exclusively on an increase in the number
of cows rather than the higher yields per cow.

Agricultural Inputs
5.        Land Use.  Agricultural land use has changed in both republics
since 1963 in favor of high value fruit production.  Both republics reduced
their arable land (mostly meadows and pastures) by 7% and increased the area
under orchards and vineyards; newly irrigated land was predominantly planted
with vineyards and orchards. In Macedonia, 4% of total agricultural land
in 1974 was under irrigation and about 2% in Montenegro. The irrigated area
has increased by about 57% in Macedonia between 1965 and 1974 while it stag-
nated in Montenegro.

8.        Power.  The number of horses has remained practically constant in
both Republics since 1960, while the number of tractors increased. Most of
the tractors and agricultural machinery was purchased after 1970, particularly
by the private sector. The pace of mechanization was, however, remarkably slow
compared with other regions in Yugoslavia. While the number of tractors in-
creased by 10.6% per year during 1961-1973 in Yugoslavia, the rate of increase
is only 5.4% and 3.9% for Macedonia and Montenegro, respectively. Accordingly,
the hectarage per tractor is much larger in Macedonia and even more so in
