9.        Productivity.  Productivity in the agricultural sector is lowest
in Montenegro both because of the low level of modern input use as well as
extremely unfavorable natural conditions. Macedonia's agricultural sector,
on the other hand, is much more advanced and enjoys more favorable conditions.
The growth rate of production and the decrease in labor input amounted to an
average annual growth of labor productivity by 4%-5% in both Republics. The
labor productivity in agriculture in 1974 in Macedonia was about 7% higher
than the average for Yugoslavia; in Montenegro it was 11% lower in 1974.

Natural Conditions
10.       Both republics are characterized by extremely mountainous terrain,
with peaks rising well over 2,500 m. Plains and valleys suitable for culti-
vation make up hardly more than one quarter of the total surface of Macedonia,
while in Montenegro, which has the lowest ratio of arable land to total sur-
face area, a mere 6.8% of the land surface is in the plains, and only 15% of
the plains has favorable agronomical characteristics. The rest are either
sandy and unsuited to crop production, suffer from drainage problems, or are
liable to flooding. The mountains, with steep slopes, heavy soils and karst,
are generally unfavorable for crop production, but extensive upland pastures
make possible sheep and some cattle growing. The agricultural sector of
Montenegro and Macedonia has, accordingly, a "dual" structure, characterized
by extensive mountain farming oriented towards livestock and intensive lowland
crop farming.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
11.       The pattern of farming is also determined by the climatic conditions
which divide both Republics into two distinct zones - the Mediterranean (or
modified Mediterranean) climatic zone in the eastern and central parts of
Macedonia and the coastal belt in Montenegro, and the continental climate
zones prevailing in the mountainous regions of the two republics.
Organization of the Agricultural Sector
12.       Agriculture of Montenegro and Macedonia, like that of the rest of
Yugoslavia, is characterized by the parallel existence of two sectors - social
and private. Their relative importance is shown in the following table.
Respective Roles of the Private and Social Sectors, 1973
(relative shares, total agriculture = 100)
Montenegro           Macedonia           Yugoslavia
Private   Social    Private   Social   Private    Social
Cultivated area        95         5         80        20         86         14
GMP                    92         8          70       30         77         23
Market Production      65        35          55       45         55         45
Labor Force            98.1       1.9        91.2      8.8       95.2        4.8
Source: Statisticki Godisnjak Yugoslavije
13.       The Social Sector.  The social sector has its origin in the post
war collectivization policy and received further impetus from the Agrarian
Reform which limited the size oE private holdings to 10 ha of cultivable land
and 25 ha of forest land. These limitations still apply. During the past ten
years the production capacity of the social sector has been increased, mainly
through capital investments, which increased by 163% in Macedonia and 72% in
Montenegro between 1965 and 1974 (Annex 1 Table 2). The increase of land
resources was limited to reclamation and purchase from the private sector
against lifetime pension payments to the private holders. It did not play
an important role during the last decade.
14.       The number of employees in the social sector (including processing
industries) in both Republics has remained at almost the same level since 1965,
fluctuating around 33,000 persons in Macedonia and between 3-4,000 persons in
Montenegro. However, the employment structure changed significantly especial-
ly in Macedonia, where the number of employees in primary production declined
by 7% between 1963 and 1974, from 26,900 persons to 24,900 persons.
15.       The social sector covers a variety of organizations, which include
agricultural kombinats, state farms and agricultural cooperatives (Zadruga)
and other organizations such as veterinary and research stations. There is a
tendency for social sector organizations in both Republics, as elsewhere in



ANNEX 1
Page 7
the country, to merge with the aim of achieving a more efficient use of
resources, especially of marketing and processing facilities and research and
planning services. In 1974, there existed only 48 autonomous kombinats and
93 autonomous cooperatives in Macedonia, against 80 kombinats and 521 coopera-
tives in 1970. In Montenegro there were only five kombinats and 9 cooperati-
ves in 1974, compared with 77 independent organizations in 1962. In Macedonia
the 12 most important social sector enterprises have formed the ZIKM (Central
Industrial Kombinat, Macedonia). The ZIKM is an umbrella organization which
supplies certain services to the enterprises of the organization such as
planning or assistance in planning, marketing and market research, and training
of staff. The social sector organizations which have formed the ZIKM remain
independent, autonomous organizations which pay the umbrella organizations for
their services and provide a financial guarantee for their existence.
16.       The social sector has been a dynamic force in the development of
modern agriculture in Yugoslavia and, in both Republics, its performance has
been remarkable. In Montenegro the volume of physical production in the social
sector increased at a trend rate of 5.1% between 1962-73, while in Macedonia,
the social sector recorded an average rate above 9% between 1965-73. The main
explanations for the rapid growth of the social sector in agriculture were:
(i) heavy concentration of investments in this sector; (ii) difference in the
"intellectual input" high-lighted by a 5.3% increase of university trained
workers in the social sector in Montenegro and 20% in Macedonia between 1963-
73, while the total employment in the social sector in the two Republics
remained at the same levels during this period-(para 14); and (iii) much
greater emphasis placed on research in the social sector as illustrated by the
existence of three major specialized research institutions in Macedonia that
do 65%-80% of their work for social sector enterprises, and an independent
research institute in Montenegro that derives some 40% of its resources under-
taking contract research for social sector enterprises.
17.       Growth rates of production within the social sector varied widely
between different subsectors, indicating the priority given to capital intensive
production.

19.       The share of the processing industry in the social sector's GMP in
the two Republics is relatively small in comparison with the rest of Yugoslavia.
About 20%-25% of the social product of the social sector of agriculture in
Macedonia is produced by the processing industry, while its share in Montenegro
is about 35%. The relatively small capacity is partly due to the orientation of
fruit and vegetable production towards early varieties for consumption in fresh
form. However, an expansion of processing facilities is justified considering
the planned expansion of vegetable production and the fact that both Republics
send part of their peak season production for processing to other republics.
Macedonia's relatively high share of sheep slaughtered in abattoirs shown
in the table below is due to strong export orientation of lamb production in
Macedonia.

20.       The social sector supplies more than 40% of the production sold on
organized markets in both Republics. This underlines its role in supplying
the cities. As elsewhere in Yugoslavia, the social sector is concentrating
on the labor extensive and capital intensive production lines, but it also
plays an important role in marketing labor intensive products of the private
sector, such as milk. The social sector markets 54% and 95% of milk marketed
in Montenegro and Macedonia respectively but shares only 55% and 24% of the
total production.

Private Sector
21.       The private sector in both Montenegro and Macedonia dominates in
terms of land area, labor force and production. It consists of 62,000 hold-
ings with an average size of 4.9 ha in Montenegro and of about 160,000 farms
with an average size of 3.1 ha of cultivable land in Macedonia.
Size Distribution of Individual Farms in Macedonia and Montenegro
Size of                      Macedonia                       Montenegro
Holdings              % of holdin    % of area        % of holding   % of area
ha
0 - 2                    57.7           17.9              46             23
2 - 3                    13.8           14.1              14             11
3 - 5                    15.0           23.7              15             17
5 - 10                   13.5           44.3              25             49
An average of 4.1 persons live on a farm in Montenegro and about 3.6 persons
in Macedonia.  One active person worked on 2.6 ha of cultivable land in
Montenegro and 2 ha in Macedonia.
22.       The production in the private sector increased by 3.2% in
Macedonia and by 2.5% - 3% in Montenegro between 1970 and 1975.  Produc-
tivity and yields remained relatively low and the productivity gap between
both sectors continued to increase during the past plan period. The major
reasons are:
(a) a generally lower quality of land;
(b) lower share in the irrigated area;
(c) inferior varieties of seeds, plant and animals;
(d) low degree of mechanization;
(e) low fertilizer intput; and
(f) small farm size and scattered holdings.
23.       The income situation of the private farmers depends largely upon
the opportunities for off farm income, on the one hand, and on the possi-
bilities to intensify land use, on the other. The share of off-farm income
has increased considerably in both Republics. On the average off-farm in-
come contributes roughly half of the individual sector's total income in both
Republics. However, large differences exist with respect to:



ANNEX 1
Page 11
(a) the employment opportunities in the vicinity of the farms;
(b) the size of the farm;
(c) the natural condition of the farm location.
If unfavorable natural conditions coincide with a lack of opportunities
for off-farm work, as is the case in most parts of the mountains in
Montenegro as well as in Macedonia, the income of the farmers is extremely
low.
Cooperation between the Private and Social Sectors
24.       Cooperation between the social and private sectors takes the form
of trade in inputs and outputs, provision of services by the social sector,
such as supply of fertilizers, cultivation, harvesting, marketing and credit
and contract production. While such cooperation brings considerable benefits
to the private farmers, it is fairly limited - less than 5% of the farmers
in Macedonia, and 2% of farmers in Montenegro, are involved in contract
cooperation. Moreover, in both Republics, this form of cooperation shows
a declining trend. One explanation for the limited size of contract coopera-
tion may be that these two southern Republics, long under Turkish rule, have
not evolved a strong tradition in agricultural cooperatives as the northern
republics had done under the Austrian influence. Another reason is the
concentration of the major activities of the social sector in the valleys
and high plains and, therefore, a lack of adequate market outlets in the
mountains. The declining trend in contract cooperation is partly due to
the increasing importance of off-farm income, and the corresponding decreas-
ing interest in agricultural activities partly owing to the fact that
farmers are willing to enter into contract cooperation only so long as
they benefit in terms of learning new techniques and achieving higher
productivity.
25.       A more loose form of coooperation in the marketing of products
has gained importance in recent years in Macedonia. As Macedonia is a food
surplus region, the individual sector had to rely increasingly on the
social sector marketing its produce, particularly vegetables and fruits,
in other parts of Yugoslavia or abroad. The volume of the private sector's
production marketed via the social sector increased by 210% in Macedonia.
In Montenegro marketing through the social sector declined because as a
food importing Republic, private farmers had increasing opportunities to
sell their production directly on the local markets.

Extension Services
26.       Traditionally, prior to the setting up of independent extension serv-
ices, the provision of services to the private farmer, such as the introduction
of new varieties and improved breeds, and dissemination of new techniques was
made by social sector organizations. However, this arrangement had its limit-
ations. Unless private farmers had joined production agreements with social
sector organizations, they could not benefit from these services. Moreover,
social sector organizations have tended to concentrate in a small number of
comiiunes which are fairly well developed, and private farmers in underdeveloped
mountainous regions could not be reached by them. Hence, the distribution of
services has become uneven.
27.       In 1971, Macedonia established an independent extension service for
the development of private sector agriculture. In each of the thirty communes,
there is an independent extension station employing 2-4 university graduates
as experts. The services are fully financed from the republic and commune
budgets, the two sides sharing the financing in equal parts. The major objec-
tives of the extension services is the improvement of the production techniques
on private farms, the introduction of new varieties and the improvement of
breeds (in cooperation with the Veterinary Institute and the Institute for
Livestock Breeding). On the commune level, the extension service tries to
reach the farmers by carrying out production demonstrations and experiments
in higher yields varieties and breeds, offering technical advice and helping
farmers in preparing credit applications to be submitted to kombinats or banks.
In addition, the republic operates a central extension service that supports
the commune stations by supplying information materials, providing technical
assistance, facilitating exchange of knowledge and preparing local working
programs. In the periods 1974-75, the achievements of Macedonia's extension
service were as follows:
-    Artificial insemination of 71,000 cows (54% of total number
of cows in Republic).
-    Provision of 2,000 rams of good quality (0.2% of rams available
for service) and subsidies for 4,500 heifers (5.1% of Republic
total).



ANNEX 1
Page 13
Provision of 5,000 tons of seeds.
Provision of 541,000 fruit root stocks.
-    Carrying out of some 1,000 demonstration tests.
During this same period, it also assisted in providing credit for investments
in 1,300 tractors (23% of Republic total), 34,000 sheep (1.8% of Republic
total), 623 cows (0.5% of Republic total) and 236 holdings of orchards and
vineyard plantations. For the 1976 program, the Republic and the Communes
have set aside 20 million dinars and 10 million dinars respectively for
the extension service.
28.       In Montenegro, the extension service was officially initiated in
February 1976. Though it has not fully commenced its activities, the pro-
posal is to set up extension centers throughout the Republic. Each commune
is expected to have a center funded in part by the commune, and in part
through a central fund. A total of thirty-five persons is expected to be
working for the extension service on the Republic and Commune level.
Marketing and Prices
29.       As elsewhere in Yugoslavia, Montenegro and Macedonia have "organized"
markets with "authorized purchases" by social sector enterprises existing side
by side with "informal" markets where the peasants sell directly to consumers.

30.       The importance of the organized markets is relatively high in
Macedonia because of the orientation of large parts of the private sector's
production to delivery to other parts of Yugoslavia, or to exports. In 1974,
for instance, 42% of wheat, 27% of tomatoes, 63% of grapes, 58% of eggs and
73% of mutton were sold in the organized market (see Table above). However,
for sales to other republics, the private sector does not rely exclusively
on the social sector. Private farmers or informal groups of private farmers
send significant quantities (especially of early vegetables) directly to local
markets of larger cities in the northern republics.
31.       In Montenegro the role of the "organized market" has been less sig-
nificant. The Table in para 29 indicates that for all commodities other than
peppers, wool and mutton, less than 10% of total production goes through the
"organized market", reflecting the predominantly subsistence nature of agri-
culture in Montenegro.



ANNEX 1
Page 15
32.       Agricultural price policy rests on agreement reached by all the
members of the Federation, and currently three types of pricing arrangements
are used:
(i) Guaranteed minimum prices: applied to all important
agricultural goods (cereals, meat, wool) for which the
implementation of a price guarantee is technically possible;
(ii)  Minimum prices without guarantee of the Government:  Pre-
scribed mainly for those agricultural goods which need pro-
cessing, such as milk, sugar beet and other industrial
crops; and
(iii)  Prices free from intervention:  for goods which are marketed
in peasant markets or are highly perishable, so that effective
control is practically impossible.
33.       Guaranteed minimum prices are intended to be adjusted according
to changes in production costs. However, in practice the agricultural price
policy has not been able to prevent the actual prices from increasing above
the tolerance limit of 10% above the minimum over the past five years. The
Government lacks the necessary funds and storage capacity for maintaining a
buffer stock to smooth seasonal price fluctuations of basic staple products
like cereals and maize. The inefficiency of the price policy particularly for
grain has resulted in a similarly ineffective price control for those products
that are closely related to grain prices, such as pigs and broilers.

Taxation
34.       Social sector agricultural enterprises pay income and turnover
taxes and various compulsory and statutory contributions, in much the same
way as industrial enterprises. However, agricultural investments and most
inputs are exempted from turnover taxation. The income tax of kombinats
and cooperatives in Montenegro and Macedonia is proportional to their income.
The tax rate varies by region, ranging from 3.4% in Montenegro to 3% in
Macedonia. Compulsory and statutory contributions include a surcharge for
social insurance, allocations to the collective consumption fund, business
and reserve funds and payments to the Skopje Earthquake Reconstruction Fund
and the Fund for the Accelerated Development of the Less Developed Regions.
Private farmers pay a tax according to the cadastral value of their agri-
cultural land. The valuation is based on the estimated productivity of the
land with each republic applying its own rules in establishing yields, pro-
duction costs and prices to be used in arriving at cadastral income. The
valuation is updated every five years.
Agro-industries Sector
35.       Both Republics have a comparatively undeveloped agroindustrial base,
in Montenegro, due to its mountainous topography, agricultural production is
low and communications are difficult for effective collection of produce to
feed an industry. Consequently incentives are lacking to increase production
and volumes are generally insufficient to establish profitable agroindustries.
The main agricultural producing area is the Cemovsko Polje plain, whose poten-
tial will be partly developed under the Project.
36.       Macedonia is a major vegetable producing region within Yugoslavia
and there are substantial quantities of vegetables available for local pro-
cessing industries and for marketing in other Republics. The share of
Macedonia in the total Yugoslavia processed vegetable production has been
around 15-16% in recent years, having declined from about 25% in 1960. This
decline is due to the growing population of fresh vegetables in Macedonia
and the fact that the processing industry has not kept pace with the expan-
sion and, by having remained rather stationary, has become both insufficient
in capacity and to some extent obsolescent.
37.       In 1972, the industry processed 28,700 tons of vegetables represent-
ing a mere 6% of the total 483,300 tons produced.

38.       According to the above projections, the total output of vegetable
products in Yugoslavia will more than double the 1972 level and almost 50% of
the 1980 production will come from Macedonia. The greatest total growth will
be in canned products, although a considerable increase in frozen vegetables
production is also anticipated. Dehydrated vegetable output, which was neg-
ligible up to 1972 is expected to reach over 1,000 tons/year in Macedonia
and substantial increases are foreseen in the production of tomato juice and
other vegetable products.
D. Agricultural Development Policy and Priorities
39.       The disappointing performance of the agricultural sector in the
second half of the 1960s, with annual growth rates of production below 2%, and
awareness of the need to use more efficiently the resources of the private
sector, initiated discussions on an integrated policy approach for the agri-
cultural sector in the early 1970s. In July 1973 the Republics and Auto-
nomous provinces agreed on a common agricultural policy for the period up
to 1985. This agreement, called the Interrepublican Agreement on the Long-
term Development Policy for Agriculture, reflected the growing concern among
political and economic authorities that the country's needs for food can
only be met with the cooperation of the private farmers.
40.       Basic Principles.  The plan emphasizes as the basic principles
of the long-term agricultural policy:
(a) the further development of the social sector through
modernization of production techniques, expansion of pro-
cessing facilities, better utilization of available re-
sources and the adjustment of production to changes in
demand;



ANNEX 1
Page 18
(b) the development of private farms either through cooperation
with the social sector or through new forms of association
among private farmers to ensure further progress in the
socialization of agriculture and rural areas.
These principles have been adopted by all republics and provinces.
The operational part of the plan focuses on three areas:
(a) price policies;
(b) investment policies;
(c) private sector development policies through cooperation
and new forms of enterprises, the so-called associations.
The present price system as discussed above will be maintained in principle.
Market prices are intended to be kept within the range of + 15% of minimum
prices through interventionist purchases or sales. Minimum prices for live-
stock will be fixed in relation to the average price for maize. The ratios
aimed at are: for pigs 1:8.5; for heifers 1:10; for fattened sheep 1:6.9;
and for broilers 1:8.9.
41.       Investment Targets.   The draft development plans 1976-80 for the
two Republics envisage the following investment targets. Annual investments
are projected to reach 1.5 billion Dinars in Macedonia, more than 50% increase
against the previous plan period. In Montenegro, the plan calls for 1.5
billion Dinars investments for the whole period, of which about one-third is
planned for investment in the private sector. The figures include the
Cemovsko Polje project.
42.       Macedonia plans to spend the 7.5 billion Dinars foreseen for in-
vestments in agriculture as follows:
Irrigation                       Din 2 billion
Processing Industries            Din 1.5 billion
Primary Production               Din 4 billion of which about
1 billion is planned for the
private sector.
Montenegro plans to spend about 30% of the projected investments in the
northern regions for the promotion of livestock. The rest is to be spent in
plant and fruit production and irrigation.
43.       Financing.  The sources of financing will remain basically the same
as in the past. Investors' own Funds, including the contribution from the basic
turnover tax, will contribute about 30% of investments. The Federal Fund for



ANNEX 1
Page 19
the Less Developed Regions will contribute about 40% and the rest is expected
from international sources.
44.       To mobilize enterprises' own resource, the following measures al-
ready listed in the previous plan will be continued:
(a) income tax exemption for investments in agriculture;
(b) turnover tax exemption for investment credits in agriculture;
(c) interest rate subsidies for investment credits in agriculture;
(d) reduction of import duties on agroindustrial equipment not
produced in Yugoslavia;
(e) elimination of the Dinar deposit requirements with the National
Bank for foreign credits channelled into agricultural investments;
(f) increase in the foreign exchange retention quota from deprecia-
tion charges.
45.       Support will be continued to all social enterprises that establish
new or extend existing units for the cooperation with the private sector.
Moreover, new associations within the private sector will be promoted. These
associations may take various organizational structures, from loose coopera-
tion in machine services to full integration of individual farms in collec-
tive units, similar to the collective farms established in the early 1950s.
Farmers may leave the collective at any time with all their property, if
they wish to do so.
46.       Credits to private farmers will continue to be almost exclusively
channelled through cooperation arrangements with the social sector. Private
farmers' associations will also be directly eligible for bank credits.
47.       Growth Targets.  The Macedonia draft development plan projects the
same growth rates for 1976-80 as in the past plan period, i.e. an annual
production growth rate of 4.5% for the agricultural sector as a whole (8.5%
in the social sector and 3% in the private sector). Macedonia intends to
make full use of its competitive advantages in the markets for vegetables,
fruits, table grapes, rice and tobacco. It expects to increase is share
in the domestic markets for these products. For meat and milk, it intends
to raise the level of self-sufficiency of the Republic.
48.       Montenegro expects especially high growth rates in the production
of wine and fruit, taking into account the production of the Cemovsko Polje
project, which is expected to be in full production at the end of the plan
period. In the other areas slightly higher growth rates than in the past
are expected.

Evaluation
49.       The Agricultural Development Plan for the first time in Yugoslavia's
post-war development addresses particularly the private farmer besides pro-
viding comprehensive guidelines for further development of the social sector.
The major specific measures for the promotion of the development of the
private sector are:
(a) Promotion of cooperation with the social sector and estab-
lishment or improvement of the extension service in order
to achieve a more rapid transfer of modern technology
already available in the social sector.
(b) Provision of credit for small farm investments to comple-
ment private farmers' own financial resources and to increase
the incentive to use them for production purposes.
The measures address the most serious bottlenecks in the development of the
private sector. However, the support measures for the private sector are
practically limited to farmers that either form an association or cooperate
with the social sector on a contractual basis. In this way they will not
reach the farmers in the remote mountainous regions, where the social sector
is not represented and where the scope for cooperation is limited. Rural
development investments with emphasis on infrastructure components would be
a pre-condition for improving productivity and marketing of these farms.
In addition, socially oriented programs would have to complement production
supporting measures, particularly where the natural conditions are most un-
favorable for agricultural production. The five year plan apparently realizes
these difficulties in projecting low (but realistic) growth rates of private
sector production as compared with the projected growth rates for the social
sector. The resulting widening gap of incomes between both sectors will
reinforce the recent trend for private farmers to look for additional income
sources outside the sector. Since these opportunities are mainly available
around the cities and, frequently, in the more fertile plains the income
difference within the private sector will increase even further and may cause
serious development problems in the mountainous regions.



ANNEX I
Page 21
50.       The projected growth rates for the social sector have been achieved
in the past. Though it becomes more difficult to repeat them at the higher
level, it seems possible to realize them at least approximately, considering
the relatively large proportion of investments in irrigation and the corres-
ponding follow-up investments at the farm level.
January 1977

ANNEX 2
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES II PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Production and Marketing
Production
1.        Agricultural production in the Cemovsko Polje plain in Montenegro
and Macedonia depends largely on the availability of water. With irrigation
the project regions enjoy considerable comparative production and marketing
advantages for grapes, fruit, vegetables, rice, sunflower and tobacco, both
on the Yugoslav market and, for most products, on the European markets as
well. Production of these crops has expanded with the increase of the irrig-
ated area mainly at the expense of extensive grain production or grassland as
may be seen from the following data:
Index of Acreage and Production in Macedonia /1
1974
(1965-1967 = 100)
Acreage     Production
Wheat                                    91             95
Maize                                    95            118
Grapes                                  112            145
Apples                                  147 /2         205
Tomatoes                                155            158
Peppers                                 162            192
Sunflower                               196            249
/1   Source:  Statistical Yearbook of Macedonia.
/2   No. of trees.
2.        The project would raise production by expanding vineyards, fruit
orchards, vegetable growing and livestock as well as through improving cul-
tivation methods and increasing the use of fertilizer and better seeds. It
would also influence the production and marketing structure by increasing
the production of early vegetables in plastic houses and by expanding the
capacity of the storage and processing industry.
3.        Vegetable Production.  Vegetable production covers about 10% of the
total arable land in Macedonia and about 40% of the cultivated land in Zone B



ANNEX 2
Page 2
of the Cemovsko Polje plain, the project region in Montenegro 1/. The private
sector produces practically the entire labor-intensive fresh vegetable crop,
which is largely sold on local peasant markets. Surpluses during the peak
season are also delivered to the processing facilities or trade organizations
of the social sector. The social sector farms, especially in Macedonia con-
centrate on capital-intensive production lines such as beans, peas and toma-
toes for their own processing facilities. The project would finance plastic
houses for the production of early vegetables, mainly tomatoes, peppers and
cucumbers. Plastic houses were also financed under the first agricultural
credit project and have found a very satisfactory response from private
farmers; production increased by more than 60% since 1973. Increased annual
production in plastic houses from the project at full development is proj-
ected to amount to 570 m tons. In addition mechanization will have an in-
direct impact on vegetable production which is estimated at 7,000 to 7,500 m
tons at full development.
4.        Fruit Production.  In 1974, Macedonia supplied 12% of the total
Yugoslav production of apricots, 11% of the apple production, 8% of the pro-
duction of pears, 4% of the production of peaches and 75% of the market pro-
duction of table grapes. Montenegro's share in total fruit production is
below 2%, except for peaches which amounted to 3.3% of the entire Yugoslav
production.
5.        Fruit production has been fluctuating with weather conditions.
However, yearly variations tend to be smaller in these two Republics than
in the rest of Yugoslavia. The production of apples, pears, sour cherries
and grapes shows a strongly upward moving trend since 1965. The area of
sour cherry and peach plantations has increased rapidly in recent years,
after a long period of stagnation (see Tables 4 and 5). Most of the new
plantations have not yet reached full production. In Macedonia the proj-
ect will result in an increase of orchard plantations in the private
sector of 110 ha and of table grapes of about 55 ha. In Montenegro, the
production of peaches, which amounted to 2,286 m tons in 1974 would be
raised by 3,200 m tons at full development of the project. The production
of sour cherries amounted to 143 m ton in 1974, 0.3% of Yugoslavia's total
production. The project would raise sour cherry production by 3,900 m tons
at full development.
6.        Tobacco Production.  Macedonia's annual tobacco production of 27,800
m ton in 1974 accounted for 47% of Yugoslavia's total production of 59,000 m
ton. Area and production have increased by 2-3% annually since 1971, after a
period of stagnation and decrease. tMacedonia and the rest of Yugoslavia grow
1/ For production data on vegetable, see Annex 2, Tables 2 and 3.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
mainly Oriental and semi-Oriental types of tobacco. Virginia tobacco ac-
counts for 15% of the total production; most of it is produced in eastern
Croatia. Production of Virginia tobacco was introduced in Macedonia in 1967
after a period of testing by the Prilep Tobacco Institute.  It is mainly
grown in irrigated areas and exclusively on private farms. Virginia tobacco
growing was supported by the tobacco drying sheds financed under the First
Agroindustries Project Macedonia.  It amounts, at present, to about 1,400 m
ton per year. Since 1971, Yugoslavia has exported about 18,000-20,000 m ton
of Oriental and semi-Oriental tobacco per year. Imports, mostly of Virginia
tobacco, declined from 5,500 m ton in 1971 to 1,800 m ton in 1974. Macedonia
enjoys very favorable climatic and soil conditions for growing Virginia tobac-
co. Substitution of Virginia tobacco for Oriental varieties as well as the
increase in area devoted to Virginia tobacco has been held back by the lack
of drying facilities. The total available drying facilities suitable for
Virginia tobaccos amount at present to 2,700 m ton (dried leaves).
7.        The project will increase the drying capacity by 800 m ton dried
leaves at full development, thus permitting an increase of the area urder
Virginia tobacco by 400 ha. The incremental production would mainly supply
the domestic market as the local demand for Virginia tobacco is expected to
increase with consumption of cigarettes, at a rate of 4-5% per year.
8.        Sheep Production.  About 25% of the total sheep herd is kept in
Macedonia. The number of sheep reached a peak of 2.2 million in 1968 and
subsequently has declined until 1972. Since 1972 it has increased again
with the improvement of the economic conditions and reached 1.9 million in
1975. 1.75 million sheep or 92% were kept in the private sector. The pre-
dominant race is the local Pramenka breed (in Macedonia subdivided into
two subraces: Ovcepola, which is kept in the east, and Shara, which is kept
in the west), with a low productivity (1-1.2 kg low quality wool per animal,
25-30 kg milk per lactation, and an average slaughtering weight of below 20
kg). The fertility rate is low with about 90-95 lambs per 100 ewes. The
average size of the herd in the private sector varies between 80 and 150
ewes; but herds of 500 sheep and more also exist.
9.        Macedonia's production of lamb exceeds its consumption.  Surplus
production is either exported or sold to other Republics. The production
of the low quality wool is processed locally. Market prices are low and
farmers frequently face difficulties in marketing the wool. High quality
wool from Merino sheep has a good market. Wool is sold at prices between 20-
25 Din per kg. The project would contribute to improved quality of both wool
and meat in the private sector. It would supply 20,000 sheep and produce at
full development 33,000 lambs, 1.8 million kg milk and 155,000 kg wool. In
addition, the new breeds would gradually upgrade existing herds and thus
further raise the production of quality lamb and wool.
10.       Milk Production.  Macedonia produced only 3.2% of the total milk
in Yugoslavia (2.9% of the cow milk and 25% of the sheep milk). Its share



ANNEX 2
Page 4
in the production sold on authorized markets is remarkably higher with 6.5%.
Macedonia is a milk deficit Republic. The number of cows fluctuated con-
siderably between 1961 and 1971. Since 1971 it has continuously increased
at rates between 5% and 6% annually, reaching 147,000 cows in 1975, of which
138,000 are kept in the individual sector. About 50% of these cows are the
Busha type of native race with low milk yields (usually around 800 kg per
cow and low meat producing pot:ential). About 20% are pure breeds (Friesians,
Montafons or Simmenthals) or F3/F4 cross breeds between Bushas and pure
breeds. About 30% of the cows are cross breeds at the F1/F2 level.
11.       The Government supports the improving of cattle breeds mainly in
the following ways:
(a) by artificial insemination. At present about 25% of the
cows in the individual sector are thus inseminated.
(b) by supplying and subsidizing selected "community bulls".
About 25% of the cows are inseminated at present in this way.
To avoid quality heifers being sold for slaughtering, the extension service
supports and subsidizes a heifer raising program for selected areas. The
project would assist the Government in these efforts by financing the pur-
chase of high quality breeds and investments in buildings and fodder pro-
duction on private farms. Its direct impact on the production of milk is
estimated at 2,700 m tons. The supply of high quality heifers to other
farmers will raise milk production indirectly.
Marketing
12.       The increase in production resulting from the project would be
absorbed predominantly by the domestic market. Exports would include some
of the early vegetables and fruit, wine and sheep production.
13.       Vegetable Marketing.  Most of the project's vegetable production
is consumed domestically, both within Montenegro and Macedonia as well as
in other regions. Most of the production "exported" to Yugoslavia's other
Republics is the surplus during the peak season for both processing and
direct consumption.
14.       The project region in Montenegro produces most of the vegetables
of the Republic (except potatoes). Its contribution to the Yugoslav market
is, however, marginal and restricted to the sale of some early vegetables
and some sales to processing industries. Macedonia's share in the total
Yugoslav production of tomatoes and peppers amounted to 28% and 35% respec-
tively in 1973/75. It contributed considerably to Yugoslavia's exports of
tomatoes and peppers.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
15.       The main buyer of Yugoslavia's peppers is the Federal Republic
of Germany, which absorbed 63% of Yugoslavia's pepper exports in 1974 (see
Table 10). Germany's pepper imports since 1967 increased by more than 30%
and are likely to continue to rise, although probably with slightly de-
creasing growth rates. Yugoslavia has maintained successfullly its compet-
itive position in West European markets against increasing competition,
mainly from Bulgaria, Romania and Spain. It can be expected that with their
closeness to the main market and long experience in marketing in this field,
Yugoslav exporters will continue to be competitive. Tomatoes were largely
exported to the German Democratic Republic (66% of total exports in 1973,
as shown in Table 10). In Eastern Europe, Yugoslavia meets heavy competition
of Comecon members, notably Bulgaria and Romania whose total 1972 exports
about equalled Yugoslavia's entire production. Because of poor market data
and the presence of state trading, Yugoslavia's prospects in the Eastern
European markets are uncertain and difficult to evaluate. Western Europe,
which absorbed about 10% of Yugoslavia's tomato exports, may become more
interesting in the future. Total demand in Western Europe is likely to
rise by approximately 2% per year. Macedonia's competitive position in
these markets would be strengthened by expanding the early production.
16.       Domestic vegetable consumption, excluding beans, has been growing
since 1966 at 3.5% to 4% per year, with slightly increasing growth rates
after 1969 (Table 13). Per capita consumption, approximately 84 kg in 1975,
is comparatively high relative to the country's per capita income level.
Production and consumption since 1965 developed more or less parallel, al-
though price development suggests that there might have been a slight
tendency of production to lag behind consumption in recent years except in
the main season (Table 12). The increase in consumption is likely to con-
tinue except for potatoes and beans. Yugoslav sources estimate the annual
increase of the future demand at 3-4% based on an income elasticity for
demand of 0.6 thus projecting a per capita consumption of 115-120 kg
(excluding beans and potatoes) in 1985. FAO projections, based on an
income elasticity of 0.5 arrive at consumption growth rates of about 3%.
17.       Seasonal prices have fluctuated in wide ranges during 1972-74,
(Table 12), indicating the uneven supply distribution during the year.
While there is some tendency towards oversupply during the main season,
the supply of fresh vegetables is low or non-existent between November and
May. In summary, it can be concluded that there is only limited room for
the expansion of consumption during the main season at prices that cover
production costs. There is, however, a large and growing need for vege-
tables outside the major season. This off-season demand can only be
satisfied if the supply is adjusted to avail of it. The project takes
this situation into account by providing for investments in plastic houses
to extend the growing season and for investments to enlarge the storage and
cooling capacity.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
18.       Fruit Marketing.  The project region contributes considerably
to the supply of apples, table grapes and early peaches on the Yugoslav
market and to the export of table grapes and of early peaches. Fruit
consumption in Yugoslavia follows more or less the development of supply.
Imports are insignificant. Exports play a role only for soft fruits,
early peaches and table grapes; in 1974, 17% of their total market produc-
tion was exported.
19.       Exports of grapes have tripled in value since 1970.  The export
by volume increased by 115% between 1970 and 1974. East Germany has become
the major importer, with 1974 imports amounting to 43.6% of total Yugoslav
exports in 1974, followed by Czechoslovakia and Austria (see Table 11).
Yugoslavia's competitive position in the East European markets is good
and further export increases to these areas can be expected. In the West
European markets it meets heavy competition from other Mediterranean coun-
tries and there is probably only limited room for further expansion.
20.       Peaches are marketed predominantly in Yugoslavia.  Only less than
1% of the peach production is exported. The United Kingdom absorbed 44% of
total Yugoslav exports in 1974, 24% were exported by Austria and the rest
went to Comecon countries, notably to Czechoslovakia, the predominant buyer
of Yugoslavian peaches in the Eastern Bloc. Export prospects to Eastern
countries may improve somewhat. Demand for peaches in Western Europe is
likely to continue its steady growth since 1960. Yugoslavia has succeeded
to export against heavy competition from Italy and Greece, mainly the early
production from the project regions. It is likely to defend or even improve
its position successfully. Domestic market prospects continue to be good,
particularly as Montenegro and Macedonia enjoy the advantages of early produc-
tion. A consumption growth of 3% to 4% per year has been projected with the
demand for early production increasing even faster.
21.       The market for fresh apples is limited to domestic consumption.
Only concentrated apple juice is exported, mainly to the Federal Republic of
Germany (67%) and the United States (31% of total exports). Exports of con-
centrated juice increased 120% between 1970 and 1974. The demand has in-
creased rapidly as reflected by a price increase of more than 400% during
the same period. The project's production is expected to be marketed mainly
in the Federal Republic of Germany, where market prospects appear good.
22.       Yugoslavia's production of sour cherries is sold about evenly do-
mestically and abroad. Exports are well diversified with Eastern European
markets absorbing about 80-85% of total exports. The main exporters are the
Federal Republic of Germany, Italy, Austria, the Netherlands, France and
Great Britain. The market prospects for sour cherries are excellent, both
in Yugoslavia as well as in Eastern Europe. Production has lagged behind
demand, and the situation is likely to remain unchanged for the foreseeable
future.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
23.       Market for Sheep Production.  Domestic consumption of mutton and
wool increased modestly since 1965 with average annual growth rates belo- 2',,
with a tendency to stagnate during recent years. The stagnating consumption
is mainly due to insufficient supply at prices that would prevent consumers
shifting to the consumption of pork and poultry meat.
24.       Exports decreased by almost 50% between 1967 and 1974 from 4,600
m ton to 2,370 m ton, although export prices increased drastically from
16 Din per kg (carcass weight) in 1968 to 43 Din per kg in 1974. About 99%
of all exports went to Italy in 1974. The EEC's import restrictions on meat
drastically reduced exports to Italy and prospects that they would return
to the previous levels are uncertain. Macedonia, which has a large share
in Yugoslavia's sheep exports, is particularly hard-hit. However, it should
be possible to market the relatively small portion of the production so far
exported (5% of the total production has been exported) partly on the do-
mestic market and partly on the new export markets. Domestic sales would
require a reduction in prices by about 15-20%. New export market prospects
appear in some Arab countries in the Middle East and North Africa. The
slaughterhouse investments under the project, designed to allow slaughter-
ing according to international hygienic standards and to Moslem regulations,
would be essential in opening up such export markets. In 1975, Macedonia
exported live sheep to Tunisia as it lacked proper slaugthering facilities.
With suitable facilities and a determined export effort, Macedonia should be
able to gain ample access to these markets and to compete successfully
against other suppliers, such as Romania and Bulgaria.
25.       Milk and milk products are entirely consumed in Macedonia.  Per
capita consumption of milk increased from about 29 kg in 1965 to about 65 kg
in 1975, with annual growth rates slightly above 8%. The consumption level
is still about 20% below the average consumption of Yugoslavia, mainly be-
cause of the insufficient supply. Consumption of milk products increased an-
nually by 3-4% since 1965 and corresponds at present to the average per cap-
ita consumption in Yugoslavia of about 7 kg. Milk production in Macedonia
is not likely to meet the future demand. The investments in livestock and
dairy facilities under the project would help to close the gap.
The Wine Market
Past Development
26.       The area under vineyards in Yugoslavia declined slightly from
261,000 ha in 1965 to 248,000 ha in 1975. Production of grapes and wine
during the last 10 years showed a slightly upward moving tendency, with
an average annual growth rate of about 1-1.5% between 1965/67 and 1973/75.
However, growth varied significantly annually and by region: production
increased strongly in Montenegro and Macedonia, while it stagnated in Serbia
proper and Vojvodina. The latter shared 37% of the total production in 1974.

27.       Domestic consumption has increased continuously since 1965 with an
average annual growth rate of about 2%. With total production and consumption
moving parallel, the wine market appeared relatively stable during the last 10
year period. However, behind the overall wine market development there oc-
curred fundamental structural changes that may have significant repercussions
for its future development.
28.       Market Organization.  The organization of the wine market follows
in principle the general structure of agricultural markets. Like the market
of other products it consists of two parts:
(a)  the "unorganized (peasant) markets", where private farmers sell
their home-made wine directly. This market includes family
consumption of home-made wine in rural households;
(b) the "organized market" with authorized purchases, where
social sector enterprises sell commercially processed wine.
29.       The wine sold on the unorganized market is processed on private
farms using the farmer's own grapes. The share of the unorganized market
in total production is steadily declining, although it still amounts to
about 75%; the share varies widely between the Republics ranging from 0%
in Kosovo to 80% in Slovenia.

30.       The organized market consists of:
(a) wine from grapes of the social sector and processed in social
sector wineries;
(b)  wine from grapes produced in the private sector and processed
in social sector enterprises; and
(c)  wine from grapes produced and processed in the private sector
sold through the trade channels of the social sector.
The importance of these categories varies widely between the Republics.
While in the project regions almost all wine sold on organized markets is
processed in wineries of the social sector, the share amounts to only 40%
in Serbia and Croatia.

In Montenegro the social sector is practically the sole producer of grapes.
The soil and climatic conditions of the individual sector are not suitable
for wine growing and other crops are more competitive.
31.       Organized market demand grew at a rate of 7-7.5% per year since
1970, while production increased steadily between 1965 and 1974 with average
annual growth rates of 8-9%. The low grape harvest in 1975 (30% below the
peak production in 1973 and 10% below the already subnormal production in
1974) caused a sharp drop of the total wine production, which probably has
interrupted the upward trend of market production. However, available pre-
liminary figures do not permit a definite statement.

32.       Exports.  The surplus of the organized market production was absorbed
by increased exports. About 42% of the total market production was exported
in 1974. Since 1970 exports increased by 218% equivalent to an average annual
growth rate of 22%. The low 1975 production and the surplus conditions on the
West European markets caused a decline of Yugoslavia's exports by 23%, from
830,000 hl to 640,000 hl, the level of exports in 1973. Imports of wine in-
creased from 6,000 hl in 1970 to more than 200,000 hl in 1973 and about
160,000 hl in 1975. Imports are mainly low quality wines from Morocco and
Algeria used largely for blending and processing.
33.       Bottled Versus Bulk Exports.  About 87% of the wine was exported in
barrels. Exports of quality wine in bottles gained importance during recent
years but amounted only to 13% of exports in 1974.
Share of Different Categories of Wine in Total Wine Exports
Total Exports = 100

ANNEX 2
Page 12
The structure of exports is more determined by the market organization in the
receiving countries than by an active Yugoslav marketing policy. The export
of wine in bottles is of major importance only in Eastern countries which lack
the necessary storage and bottling capacities for the import of wine in bulk.
Also the USA and Canada import largely bottled wine as small trading firms
without bottling facilities handle most of the imports. Countries like the
Federal Republic of Germany, where about 80% of the total imports is handled
by one large firm, prefer to use their own storage and bottling facilities to
enlarge their profit margin.
34.       Regional Export Structure.  About 54% of the total exports in 1974
went to the EEC countries, one third to the Comecon and about 6% to Japan,
which has become the most important customer outside the EEC and the Comecon.
Japan increased its imports from Yugoslavia from 4,000 hl in 1970 to 50,000
hl in 1974. The major part of the large increase in exports between 1970
and 1974 was, however, absorbed by EEC countries, especially by the Federal
Republic of Germany, who increased imports of Yugoslav wine by 2.45 million
hl or more than 300%, thereby absorbing 54% of the total increase in exports
between 1970 and 1974. Comecon countries increased their exports by only
97,000 hl accounting for 21% of the total increase of exports between 1970
and 1974.

35.       Prices.  Export and domestic prices increased continuously since
1970 indicating the stable market conditions Yugoslav producers have enjoyed
so far.

Domestic Markets

Within individual years prices varied considerably between countries and, on
the domestic market, between regions, indicating in both cases mainly quality
differences (see Table 14). Quality wines achieved from 20% to 40% higher
prices in general. Market differentiation according to quality, particularly
creating a separate market for high quality wines, is in the initial stage.
Montenegro is leading in these efforts as it produces relatively high quality
wines.

Source: Vino Macedonia
Prospects
36.       General.  Basic trends of production and consumption on the domestic
markets, as discussed above, will continue in the next years. However, growth
rates are likely to change and endanger the stable development of the past.
Projections of Yugoslav Wine Fund imply the following growth rates:



ANNEX 2
Page 14
Projected Annual Growth Rates 1975 - 1985
Area under vineyards                                0.5
Yields                                              4-5
Production of grapes                                4-5
Total wine production                               4-5
Organized market production                          9
Demand on organized markets                         3-4
These projections show that a tendency towards surplus production is built
into the present development, unless the rapid increase of exports continues.
37.       Exports.  The prospects for Yugoslav wine exports depend on the de-
velopment of the wine markets in the following countries or country groups:
(a) the EEC
(b) the Comecon
(c) Japan
(d) United States and Canada
38.       The EEC is the most important market for Yugoslav-exports absorbing
more than half of the total wine exports and two-thirds of the increase of
exports between 1970-1974. The short-term outlook for imports into the EEC
has improved slightly with the "normal" EEC harvest in 1975/76, after several
surplus years caused by the good harvests in 1973/74 and 1974/75.
Production and Imports of Wine in the EEC
Average for:  1968/72    1973/74    1974/75    1975/76
_______------- 1,000 hl----------------
Production                        135,700    170,050    160,617    142,339
Imports                             4,481/1    7,226        5,264       5,361
Source: Kom. report (Kom(75)) 416 Brussel, 1975.
Statistisches Jahrbuch fur Ernahrung, Landwirtschaft und
Forsten Hamburg/Berlin in 1975 and Vorbilanz fur das
Weinwirtschaftsjahr 1975/76, Brussel.
Medium and long-term outlooks are less favorable. Direct wine consumption
stagnated practically since 1968/69. The increase of total consumption
was mainly due to increased use of wine for processing to alcohol, partly
supported by Government subsidies.



ANNEX 2
Page 1,
Development of Consumption of Wine in the EEC
1969/70    1973/74    1974/75    1975/76
---------------million hl--------
Total Consumption                       141.1       189.9      162.2       150.1
-Direct Consumption                     127.6      124.6       128.4       132.1
-Processing & distillation               11.4        23.2       32.4        16.9
39.       Per capita consumption in the EEC varied between 48 and 51 kg since
1970, varying from 2 kg per capita in Ireland to more than 100 kg in Italy and
France. Differences are explained by consumption preferences rather than
by differences in per capita incomes. This suggests that future consumption
is likely to stagnate or to increase only modestly. The stagnating direct
consumption is likely to continue with small variation between countries.
Additional expenses for wine are likely to be spent mostly for higher quality.
Production is likely to continue to increase as replanting of old vineyards,
especially in Italy and France, will increase yields. There is also an
increase in the area under vineyards expected.
40.       The new regulations for the wine market in the EEC implemented
in May 1976 try to tackle the surplus problem by preventing new planting of
vineyards until 1978 (except vineyards for the production of quality wine in
certain regions) and by granting premiums to farmers who give up the produc-
tion of low quality wines without establishing new vineyards within the next
six years. It is, however, doubtful that these measures will achieve their
intended objective, since the EEC has at the same time raised the guaranteed
minimum price for low quality wine, thus providing a strong production in-
centive. Therefore, the tendency of supply to grow faster than demand is
likely to continue.
41.       Yugoslavia shares only 4% of the total wine imports of the EEC and
has so far succeeded in defending and even increasing its market share. Recent
changes in the foreign trade policy of the EEC have worsened the competitive
position of Yugoslav wine producers considerably:
(a)  by granting duty free import quotas of yearly 550,000 hl
(about 10% of present total imports) to the Magreb States;
(b)  by increasing the degree of protection for domestic pro-
ducers and by determining a minimum import price for im-
ported wine (reference price) which is enforced by a
variable duty, if necessary.
The pressure from third countries to receive preferred access to the Common
European Market will continue. Greece, Portugal and Spain are probably the



ANNEX 2
Page 16
next candidates. Granting preference to these countries would have a major
impact on Yugoslavia's market position, particularly in West Germany and the
United Kingdom. The EEC's concession to the Magreb States would affect
Yugoslavia only indirectly since most of the Magreb wine is imported to
France, to where Yugoslavia is not exporting.
42.       Yugoslavia is likely to be able to defend its present position suc-
cessfully since the major part of its imports to the United Kingdom and to
West Germany is in the hands of a very few effective and powerful importers.
Nevertheless, expansion of exports will become increasingly difficult; ex-
ports are likely to stagnate or at best may increase by 1 - 2% annually.
Moreover, the possibility that the Common Market will impose a ban on wine
imports from non-preference countries in years of great surplus cannot be
excluded entirely in the light of past experience on other markets.
43.       Export prospects for high quality wines (appellation controlee) are
more favorable. The market for well-established, high-quality wine, of which
only limited quantities are available, has hardly been affected by the general
surplus situation in the EEC. The prices remained high. On the other hand
less known quality wines experienced a considerable price decrease close to
the price level of ordinary wines. The conclusion is that wines of selected
qualiity will have good chances, regardless of whether they are produced
inside or outside the Common Market area. However, to exploit these chances
by introducing wines unknown to consumers, such as the Vranac wine to be pro-
duced under the project, requires not only high-quality production but also
skillful marketing efforts. Yugoslavia and particularly the Cemovsko Polje
plain have the potential for producing excellent wine.
44.       Limited information exists about the market of the Comecon Member
Counties. Yugoslav exports increased moderately with annual growth rates
of 3 - 4% during the last years. Considering the present level of wine con-
sumption in Czechoslovakia, Eastern Germany and the USSR and their prospects
for general economic development, one can expect a continuation of the in-
crease of exports to these countries with similar orders of magnitude as in
the past.
45.       The rapid increase of wine exports to Japan has induced ambitious
hopes in Yugoslavia. An increase of exports from 50,000 hl in 1974 to more
than 150,000 hl is planned for 1976. Japan is a new-comer on the interna-
tional wine market; it increased its imports from 13,710 hl in 1969 to almost
400,000 hl in 1974, of which Yugoslavia imported 13%. Sharp competition is
likely to develop among wine exporting countries and the increase of consump-
tion in Japan has to be projected with great caution, since the general public
has not yet developed a wine-drinking habit. Yugoslavia's export prospects
should not be overestimated therefore. The growth rates of its exports since
1970 are not likely to continue.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
Conclusion
46.       Wine Exports.  The growth rate of Yugoslavia's wine exports since
1970 will decrease considerably. Exports are likely to increase at growth
rates around 3% and possible somewhat faster if Yugoslavia succeeds in using
its potential of producing high quality wine.
47.       Domestic market.  The main market for Vranac wine will remain the
domestic market. The projections of the Wine Fund for domestic markets (see
para 35) may be slightly too pessimistic, especially if prices in real terms
were to fall under the pressure of increased supply, demand on the organized
market is likely to increase faster, and one might expect growth rates of
5 - 7% assuming an income elasticity of demand of 0.7 and a price elasticity
of demand around 1.0.
48.       Vranac is a high quality red wine produced mainly in Montenegro and
Macedonia, which resembles famous French wines like Chateauneuf-du-Pape or
Paul Masons Pinot Noire from California. So far it is used for direct con-
sumption almost only in Montenegro and Macedonia. Outside the project regions
it is mainly used fore blending because of its dark red color and its special
flavor. In 1975 it was sold in Mlontenegro at 13.75 Din/I in bulk and at 18 -
22 Din/i in bottles. These prices are considerably above the average (see
para 34) indicating that the demand for Vranac wines is high.
49.       The increasing pressure of supply in Yugoslavia may lead to a more
distinct separation of the markets of high-quality and ordinary wines, similar
to the development in Western Europe. Experiences there shc-w that fluctua-
tions of prices and quantities in the former markets are considerably smaller
than on the ordinary wine markets. To create a stable long-term market for
Vranac, efforts need to be continued to establish Vranac as a high quality
well-known wine outside Montenegro. A promising start has been made. In
early 1976 the wine received the appellation controlee label from the Yugoslav
Wine Fund in accordance with the methodology prescribed by the Office Inter-
national du Vin, of which Yugoslavia is a member.
