ANNEX 3
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES II PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Financial and Institutional Aspects
A. The Banks' Legal Framework and Accounting System 1/
I. LEGAL FRAMEWORK
1.        The basic legal framework under which Yugoslav banks operate is
provided by the Federal Banking Law of December 1971 and each bank's statutes.
Major provisions of this law and of the statutes of the proposed IBRD bor-
rowers - SBS and IBT - are the following:
(a) A bank cannot be founded by fewer than 25 founders; the
minimum conditions of joining and withdrawals of founders
are prescribed by the statutes.
(b) A bank must have a minimum of Din 50 million as founders
fund (capital). Prior to 1975, a founder could have up to
10% of the total voting rights depending on his share in the
founders fund, but now each founder has one vote.
(c) The founders of a bank have an unlimited liability in respect
of the bank.
(d) The bank's statutes prescribe the powers of the various managing
organs. The bank Assembly consisting of representatives of the
founders is the highest governing body of the bank.
(e) The Assembly appoints the bank's chief executive and members
of the Executive Board and Credit Board.
(f) The Assembly approves the bank's annual business policy and
the policy on interest rates and decides on the allocation.
(g) Banks are required to make a yearly appropriation to a Reserve
Fund out of income, of at least 0.5% of loans outstanding at
the year-end. This fund can be used only for writing-off bad
1/   This section is adapted from the Appraisal Report for Industrial Credit
Project II, November 1975, as there have been no changes in the inter-
vening period up to the time of this Appraisal.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
debts and, temporarily, for meeting the bank's obligations in
case of tight liquidity. The resources of this fund have to
be generally maintained in liquid or semi-liquid funds.
(h) Banks are required to write-off bad debt first against income.
Write-offs against the Reserve Fund are only possible to the
extent that there is insufficient income.
(i)  A bank will be liquidated if it is unable to meet its obligations
for more than six months.
(j) In case of liquidation, the deposits of individuals with the
bank have priority over all other creditors of the bank. (In
addition, such deposits of individuals are also guaranteed by
the Federal Government under a separate law).
2.        In addition to the Banking Law, the banks have to comply with
various regulations and directives issued by the National Bank of Yugoslavia
and the Federal Government. The main directives which have a direct implica-
tion on the banks' financial operations are:
(a) Liquidity Requirements. The banks have to maintain a certain
proportion of their demand deposits (currently ranging from 2%
of domestic savings accounts to 24% of enterprises' giro
deposits) as deposits with the National Bank. The primary
purpose is to ensure adequate liquidity of banks but the
measure is also used as a tool for implementing monetary policy.
(b) Foreign Exchange Regime. Only authorized banks can undertake
foreign exchange transactions under supervision of the National
Bank. SBS and IBT are so authorized. When making a foreign
exchange loan to an enterprise, a bank has to ensure that the
borrower will be in a position to generate sufficient foreign
exchange funds (through retention quotas against depreciation
and exports, and special quotas) to repay the loan. The banks
are also authorized to buy and sell foreign exchange at the
Belgrade foreign exchange market. All foreign borrowings are
regulated by the National Bank and have to be registered with
it prior to and after the borrowing is contracted.
(c) Limitations Regarding Banks' Overall Commitments. Under a
Federal Law, passed in the second half of 1975, a bank is
required to keep the level of commitments of guarantees and
investment loans within the limits of its liquid investment
potential. Under the law, the Federal Executive Council shall
specify which resources shall constitute a bank's liquid in-
vestment potential and the conditions under which guarantees
can be made. It is intended that banks shall report periodic-
ally to SDK the status of their liquid investment potential



ANNEX 3
Page 3
and obligations incurred, while SDK, in turn, shall inform the
Federal Executive Council. The law seeks to tighten the
financial management of investments and affects, aside from
banks, enterprises, socio-political organizations, communities
of common interest, agricultural cooperatives, etc.
II. ACCOUNTING SYSTEM
3.        The accounting systems in Yugoslavia are substantially different
from the systems of western countries. In preparing periodic balance sheets,
statements of income and expenditures, and other financial reports, the two
banks do not adhere strictly to generally accepted accounting principles.
The major differences in the system and the difficulties in reconciling these
differences are summarized below:
(a) The branches of the banks being autonomous units in themselves,
prepare their own balance sheets and income statements. In
consolidating statements for a bank as a whole, the individual
balance sheet items of its branches are often added up without
eliminating inter-branch entries.
(b) The banks' accounts of resources and operations include funds
managed by them on behalf of local authorities (socio-political
organizations) at no risk to the banks. Such operations on
commission basis have to be eliminated from the main body of
the banks' balance sheets.
(c) Short-term loans have maturities of up to two years and data
on loans with less than one year maturity (current assets) is
not kept separately. It is therefore not possible to separate
current and long-term positions strictly according to normal
accounting definitions.
(d) Accounts are maintained for each loan made by a bank differ-
entiated by purpose and/or source of funds (e.g. loan for fixed
assets or for working capital and loan from Federal funds,
Republic funds, etc.). A borrower may have as many as 100 such
loans outstanding, maintained in the branches or the head office
of the bank. No routine record is presently kept of total ex-
posure to any one bororwer and, because of the large number of
accounts involved, compilation of such information is a
formidable job.
(e) The banks, operating as commercial and investment banks to their
clients, observe strict confidentiality as regards financial
information received from their clients. They cannot disclose
the annual income and balance sheets of the clients to any
outsider (including the IBRD) without the consent of the
clients themselves.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
(f) The banks do not attempt generally to make a periodic assessment
of potential losses within their loan portfolios. Annual appro-
priations to the Reserve Fund can be considered as provisions
against losses but are not the result of a case-by-case review
of their loans outstanding (para 1 (g)).
(g) The appropriation for reserves is not treated as an expense
item in determining a bank's net income. The banks, however,
are required by law to write-off, against income, 25% (current
rate) of their total uncollected interest overdue by 45 days
or more at the end of a year.
III. AUDITS
4.        The Social Accounting Service (SDK) is responsible for the audit of
all enterprises, including the Banks, in terms of Yugoslav law. The audit
is mostly in the form of a certification that the banks' and enterprises'
operations are within the various legal requirements, that the statements are
in agreement with the records kept by the banks and correct charges of in-
terest, allocation of net income, etc. have been made. There is no verifica-
tion of the existence of the various assets and liabilities to the banks and
no opinion on whether they are fairly treated. In addition to the SDK, the
banks are subject to inspections by the National Bank of Yugoslavia, which
ensures, from time to time, that the banks comply with the regulations on
foreign-exchange transactions and special deposit requirements.
5.        Normal audit by the SDK does not meet the standards usually re-
quired by the Bank, but arrangements have been made for proper audits of
the Bank Group projects in Yugoslavia, including audits of the proposed
borrowing banks under the Project. SDK has implemented a training program
for its staff, with the Bank's support, to undertake such audits. As per
the audit program envisaged under the First and Second Industrial line of
credit full audit of the SBS and IBT was done by SDK for the year 1974.
Interim audits of the 1975 accounts of SBS and IBT are being done. These
audit arrangements are considered satisfactory.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
B. The Banks 1/
I. GENERAL
6.        The Bank loan is proposed to be made to the Investiciona Banka
Titograd (IBT) in Montenegro and Stopanska Banka Skopje (SBS) in Macedonia.
Both banks are the leading bank in their respective republics and have been
associated with the Bank financed First Agricultural Credit Project, and
First and Second Industrial Credit Projects. SBS was the borrower under the
Macedonia Agroindustries Project. IBT was founded in 1964 and SBS was formed
in 1973 by merging three banks: Stopanska Banka, Commercial and Investment
Bank, Skopje, and Commercial and Investment Bank, Bitola. The ownership,
organization and operations of each bank, as well as the functions and powers
of its main management organs and branches are governed by its statutes and
the self-management agreements.
II. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Ownership
7.        Both banks are owned and controlled by the large enrerprises
(founders) of their respective Republics. The founders are akin to share-
holders and the founders fund is the equivalent of share-capital. As of
December 31, 1975 the number of founders was 131 in IBT and 342 in SBS.
The founders fund was 362 and 1,822 million Dinars for IBT and SBS respec-
tively.
Organization Structure
8.        The organization charts of both banks are placed at Appendices 1
(WB 15722 R) and 2 (WB 15721 R). The Banks are organized as follows:
(a) Founders Assembly is the highest governing body of a bank,
in which every founder has the right to one vote. The Assembly
approves the bank's policies, appoints the chief executive and
elects the members of the Executive Board and Credit Board.
It also decides on the distribution of the bank's income to
employees and founders as well as appropriations to the
bank's reserves.
1/   Both the SBS and IBT, as borrowers under the Second Industrial Credit
Project (Loan No. 1277-YU) are described in more detail in the Report,
Appraisal of Second Industrial Credit Project, No. 1058-YU.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
(b) Executive Board is the chief management body of the banks re-
sponsible for executing their policies approved by the Founders'
Assembly and other statutory functions, including approval of
loans exceeding authority of credit boards (above 5 million Din
in case of IBT and 30 million Din in case of SBS). Members of
the executive board (19 for IBT and 35 in SBS) are elected
from among the founders.
(c) Credit Board consists of members elected from among the
founders. Its function is loan approval.
(d) Internal Control Committee is elected by the Founders' Assembly
from among the founders. It ensures that the provisions of the
law and of the statutes of the bank are correctly enforced, that
funds are not misused and that the functions are adequately
performed.
(e) Workers' Council consists of members elected from and by em-
ployees and represents the employees in the administration and
operations of the bank. In consultation with bank's management
it decides on individual salaries and fringe benefits for
workers within the aggregate amounts approved by the founders.
(f) Head Office of each bank is organized along functional activi-
ties (sectors), such as Domestic Resources Sector, Sector for
Investment, Foreign Business Sector and Legal and Administration
Sector. Accounting activity is attached to the sector for Domestic
Resources in case of IBT and to the Legal and Administrative
Sector in the case of SBS. The Investment Sector is subdivided
into departments for Agriculture, Basic Industries, Processing
and other sectors, and Disbursement and Collection in the case
of SBS. In IBT the Investment sector comprises Basic Industries,
Processing Industries, other sectors, short-term loans and
Engineering and Technical Departments. The Engineering group
provides common services to other departments. The organization
structure of the head office is satisfactory.
(g)  Branches, SBS has 22 branches and IBT has 8 branches.  Each
branch has its own Founders Assembly, Credit Board and
Executive Board. They have substantial autonomy in allocat-
ing funds mobilized by them and in day to day operations.
However, the statutes of the banks limit the maximum amount
of loan which the branch may approve.
Management and Staff
9.        The General Manager of the bank is the Chief Executive.  He is
appointed for a four-year term by the bank's Assembly on the basis of recom-
mendations of a selection committee. The General Manager is assisted by
Assistant General Managers who in turn supervise two or three Department
Directors. Junior Managers are typically university graduates with many



ANNEX 3
Page 7
years of experience. Changes in top managers are infrequent, and profes-
sional competence is the main criterion for selection and appointment at all
levels. The total staff of the banks is 477 in IBT and 1,565 in SBS. About
35% of the staff in IBT and 22% in SBS are in the head office. The propor-
tion of professional staff in IBT is about 25% and 26% in SBS. The pro-
fessional staff consists mostly of economists, accountants, engineers and
lawyers.
Agricultural Staff in IBT
10.       Agricultural staff in IBT forms part of the Engineering and Technical
Group. IBT has one Agroindustries Engineer and an expert in Agronomy/Forestry.
IBT also has several economists experienced in economic analysis and appraisal
of agricultural projects. IBT has no separate agricultural department, as its
loans to the agricultural sector form only a small portion of the total loan
portfolio. At present there is an IBRD operations unit, consisting of 5
experienced economists as staff members, which is handling all work relating
to lending and IBRD financed projects. For the Agricultural Credit project,
the Unit is assisted in its work by the two agricultural experts, mentioned
above, and hires consultants if needed. In view of the Unit's involvement
in agricultural lending and the additional work load expected under this
Project, it will be necessary to expand the Unit.
Agricultural Staff in SBS
11.       Agricultural staff in SBS is part of the Agricultural Operations
Department. The Department has a total of 29 staff members, encompassing
expertise in agriculture, livestock, agro-processing, marketing, irrigations
and economic and financial analysis areas. Experience with the Department's
work under the Macedonia Agroindustries Project indicates two areas of weak-
ness, (a) agroindustries expertise, and (b) marketing analysis. Structurally,
there is a weakness in coordination of the work done in different departments
of SBS and IBRD financed projects.
III. FINANCIAL STRUCTURE
12.       The analysis of financial statements of the banks has been res-
tricted to 1974 and 1975 as these have been reclassified by SDK from the
Yugoslav system to a format generally acceptable to the Bank (paras 3 to 5).
Certain adjustments in the accounts have been made to remove inter-branch
accounts and separate the commission business (managed funds) from other
businesses of the banks. The reclassified and adjusted statements are
not strictly comparable to financial statements of the banks prior to 1974.
Resource Position
13.       Most short-term resources consist of deposits and borrowings from
the National Bank through rediscount facilities. As a general policy the



ANNEX 3
Page 8
short-term resources are used almost exclusively for short-term investments
and long-term resources for long-term investments. National bank may allow
prudent usage of short-term resources for long-term resources. The long-term
commitments of both SBS and IBT exceed the long-term resources. This situa-
tion is customary for these banks as they plan their operations on a disburse-
ment basis rather than on a commitment basis. Each bank limits its over-com-
mitments to the amount of resources it is fairly certain to receive from the
Federal Fund during the period in which it has to meet these commitments.
The resources position is therefore satisfactory.
Financial Performance (Table 2)
14.       It is the stated policy of each bank that its income should cover
all expenses with an adequate amount of transfer to: (a) the Reserve Fund
(substitute for provision against losses), and (b) the "funds of the bank
as a working community", which are used to acquire and maintain working
facilities and to provide some fringe benefits to the workers. Profitability
is not considered an essential measure of the bank's efficiency and financial
performance. Income surpluses, if any, are returned to the enterprises as
dividends or transferred to the founders' fund of the bank. Income state-
ments of the SBS and IBT reflect the break-even nature of operations: gross
income, less direct financial expenses in 1975, was 1.6% and 2.0% of average
total assets for IBT and SBS respectively. These are low and reflect both
the mixed nature and the non-profit orientation of the banks. The Adminis-
trative expenses in 1975 were also on the low side, about 0.5% of the average
total assets for both IBT and SBS, showing strict control over the adminis-
trative budget.
Financial Position (Table 1)
15.       The total assets of IB, and SBS, as of December 31, 1975, were 10.4
and 34.5 billion Dinars respectively, representing a growth of 21.5% and 22.4%
over the previous year. The long-term loan portfolio accounted for 63.3%
(IBT) and 55.8% (SBS) of the total assets. Each bank also manages, at no risk
to itself, substantial funds on behalf of Federal and Republic Governments
and socio-political organizations. The current ratios of the banks are near
1.1:1 and are satisfactory. The long term debt/equity ratios are high; 13.7:1
and 6.7:1 respectively for IBT ard SBS. The high debt/equity ratios do not
pose a danger to the banks' solvency as most of the long-term debts are loans
from federal or republic authorities, repayments of which are more or less
tied to the corresponding repayments by the enterprises to the bank.
Lending Operations (Table 3)
16.       The short-term loan commitments amounted to Din 3.2 billion in
IBT and Din 18.4 billion in SBS in 1975, representing an increase of 9.1%
and 14.3% respectively over the previous years. Short-term loan disburse-
ments are substantially the same as commitments. The percentage of short-
term loans to agriculture was 7.1% in IBT and 12.4% in SBS (Table 3).



ANNEX 3
Page 9
17.       The long-term loans commitments by both banks in 1975 increased
significantly by 128% and 46% over 1974. Sector-wise the increase was
mainly in Industry and Agriculture sectors in IBT and in Industry sector
in SBS (Table 4). Increase in Industrial Sector was due in part to a few
large energy projects as well as to a significant rise in project costs due
to international and domestic inflation.
Lending to Agricultural Sector
18.       The percentage of short-term loans to agriculture was 7.1% in
IBT and 12.4% in SBS. The corresponding percentages for the long-term
loans to agriculture were 3% and 8.5% for IBT and SBS respectively. The
lending to agriculture sector by IBT doubled in 1975 compared to 1974,
reflecting an increased emphasis on agriculture in the republic's plan
and expansion of IBT's lending to financing of the private sector agricul-
ture. Nearly 30% of the IBT long-term loans for agriculture went to the
private sector, benefitting 700 private sector farm families, and helped
finance 221 tractors, 7,000 m2 of plastic houses, 23 ha vineyards, 1 ha
citrus plantation, 40 pumpsets, 80 animal feed mills, 180 ha olive planta-
tions, 100 cows and 30 sheep.
19.      Agricultural lending has been relatively more prominent in SBS loan
portfolio (14.6%) compared to IBT (3%). The average size of agricultural
loans in both banks is small compared to the other two important sectors in
lending, i.e. industry and transport, and public utilities.
Quality of the Loan Portfolio
20.       Analysis of loan arrears in IBT as of December 31, 1975 and in SBS
as of June 30, 1975, given in Tables 5 and 6, shows that the loans affected
by arrears related to the total amounts outstanding were 6.5% in IBT and 6.1%
in SBS. Most of the arrears was aged less than six months. The most frequent
causes for arrears were the depressed markets at home and abroad and, in new
projects, delays in project implementation, often resulting from difficulties
in securing resources to finance cost over-runs. The arrears position both
in amount and age deteriorated in the SBS in 1975 compared to 1974 and will
need to be watched carefully. However, the overall situation is manageable
considering (a) the actual arrears of principal and interest as percentages
of the banks' portfolios are small, 1% and 1.9% for IBT and SBS respectively;
(b) each bank's provisions (Reserve Fund) against bad debts exceeded arrears
of over 3 months; (c) loans of the banks are adequately secured by acceptance
orders from borrowers. These acceptance orders can be exercised through
intervention of SDK, which handles all receipts and payments of enterprises.
Through this legal instrument a bank can halt an enterprise's flow of payments
until its obligations towards the bank have been met. Such a drastic step
is taken only as a last resort and with due consideration for the implications
of such action.
21.       The number of loans granted is fairly evenly distributed in enter-
prises in all sectors of the economy indicative of a reasonably diversified
loan portfolio (Table 4). However, the wide disparity of loan amounts per



ANNEX 3
Page 10
borrower in different sectors resulted in an uneven distribution of loan
amounts between sectors. However, an assessment of the extent of the bank's
records do not show aggregate loans to an enterprise. This is not a problem
for SBS and IBT since they have access to the borrower's financial statement
and the list of its debt everytime a request for granting or renewing a loan
is made. However, this information is required by the Bank to evaluate the
SBS and IBT's loan portfolio. Until the full audit reports including an
analysis of the banks' portfolios are available, the assessment of quality
of the loan portfolio is incomplete.
Creditworthiness
22.       In the Yugoslav banking system and financial environment the banks
are in a unique position, which makes it possible to consider both IBT and
SBS creditworthy even though neither the full audit of the banks is available
nor detailed records are kept of banks' exposure to a single borrower. This
assessment of creditworthiness is based on the same arguments that were
accepted for Industrial Credits I and II and Agricultural Credit I, which
are the following:
(a)  The Founders have unlimited liability towards the bank's
obligations. Creditors of the bank thus have legal recourse
to all assets of founders, many of which are large industrial
enterprises with substantial assets.
(b)  The Banks are closely integrated with the economy of the
Republics and their liquidation in case of financial crisis
is inconceivable.
(c)  The banks have qualified and experienced management and staff.
Experience shows that both SBS and IBT's financial management
has been adequate with no significant losses, arrears have
been manageable, ancl obligations have been met satisfactorily.
Forecasts
23.       At present investment planning in SBS and IBT is limited to one
year within the framework of their annual business policy. Both banks pre-
pared five-year projections of their operations and resources based pri-
marily on the Plan (1976-80) for their regions. These projections are given
in Tables 8 and 9 and are tentative with high margins of error. Medium-term
planning is being attempted by both banks under the Second Industrial Credit
Project and the quality of forecasts will improve as the banks acquire more
experience. For the plan period of 1976-80, resource gaps projected for each
bank, pertaining to domestic and foreign borrowings are high, in absolute
terms (Din 5.0 billion for IBT and Din 14.8 billion for SBS) and as a portion
of each bank's total long-term resource requirements (30X for IBT and 35% for
SBS). The gaps are expected to be filled largely with foreign borrowings by
own efforts or through the National Bank. The proposed loan is small in rela-
tion to the bank's total resource needs. No difficulty is anticipated in



ANNEX 3
Page 11
both banks being able to discharge their financial obligations under the
Project, as the amounts of funds needed will come mainly from Federal funds
and are assured, as also the amounts involved are small relative to overall
operations of the bank.
24.       The projected income statements and balance sheets of the banks for
1976-80 are given in Tables 10 through 13. The break-even orientation of the
banks is expected to continue; satisfactory debt service coverage through
1980 is indicated and the current ratios are projected to remain at a little
above 1:1. The financial strength of the banks will depend not on the extent
of equity but on the financial strength of its founders and the continuation
of the past practice of transferring resources from time to time from the
bank's accounts of federal and republic funds to managed funds or to enter-
prises which would have the effect of keeping debt/equity ratio low.   Under
these premises both banks are expected to remain creditworthy in future years.
IV. LENDING POLICIES AND PROCEDURES
Business and Financial Policy
25.       Both SBS and IBT have a Business Policy which specifies the bank's
main objectives, magnitude of operations and interest rate policies for cus-
tomer deposits and lending operations for a year. The IBT has a policy of
limiting its financial exposure in a single enterprise to a maximum of 10%
of its portfolio (exceptions can be approved by its Assembly). SBS is
limited by the Banking Law in its lending to a single client to 30% of its
total investment portfolio. Both banks generally match the maturities of
their loans and their resources. A wide distribution of loans amongst the
founders ensured by the large number of founders (each with one vote in the
Assembly) thus almost eliminating the risk of excessive lending. (This in-
formal financial policy arrangement has worked satisfactorily in the past).
Project Appraisals
26.       In both banks the projects financed entirely out of the resources
of the branch are appraised and approved at the branch. However, since most
of the bank's resources, incuding federal and republic funds and foreign ex-
change funds, are channelled through the head office, most projects are ap-
praised and approved at the Head Office. The credit application consists of
an investment program supported by technical, marketing and financial data.
Appraisal reports are prepared by the appropriate department with the help of
advisors/consultants in the case of complex projects. Under IBRD financed
projects both banks have considerably improved their appraisal methods; how-
ever, the following weaknesses still exist:
(a) insufficient critical technical appraisal of the technical data
and specifications provided by the borrower;



ANNEX 3
Page 12
(b) no comparative evaluation of labor versus capital intensive
alternatives;
(c) inadequate marketing analysis of future sales;
(d) the evidence supporting availability of raw materials is
neither questioned ncr verified;
(e) inadequate financial and economic analysis; and
(f) scanty attention to contingency allowances.
Strengthening of the technical appraisal capability of both banks for agro-
industrial lending would be an important institution building objective under
the Project. Further strengthening and application of financial and eco-
nomic analysis in the project appraisals will continue to be one of the
institution building objectives of the IBRD loan.
Supervision of Projects
27.       Systematic supervision is directed by both banks only to projects
under construction or to problem projects. Although the banks maintain
close contact with their clients' operations and financial position in con-
nection with short-term lending yet the operating projects of borrowers who
repay their loans on schedules are not normally supervised. The supervision
responsibility is mostly delegated to the branches of the banks. Both banks
used to supervise projects more regularly during construction and after they
become operations, to monitor and analyze the actual results against ap-
praisal estimates and to take appropriate action where needed. The super-
vision work needs to be centrally coordinated at the head office to cover
various facets of the borrower's dealings with the bank, and for effective
feedback to investment and other operational departments. Supervision sys-
tems for projects will need to De streamlined and strengthened.
Procurement and Disbursement
28.       The banks ensure that their clients observe the laws and regula-
tions on procurement (advertising, bid opening, awards, etc.). When the total
value of equipment for an investment is over Din 500,000 (about US$28,000),
the selection of supplier has to be based on a minimum of three bids obtained
through public tender published in the official Gazette of Yugoslavia. Ex-
ceptions are allowed for specific cases where this procedure would not be
suitable. Disbursements are made on contractor notes, bills and other satis-
factory evidence of serivces rendered or goods supplied. Overall disburse-
ment methods appear to be sound,



ANNEX 3
Page 13
V. INTEREST RATES AND RESOURCE ALLOCATION
29.       Interest rates on loans and deposits are fixed each year by the
Founders Assembly. The present interest rate structure in the two republic
is as follows:
IBT           SBS
Deposits (depending on maturity)            3   - 10%       4 - 10%
Short-term Loans                           10.5 - 11%       8 - 11.5%
Long-term Loans
from own resources                      up to 11%        up to 11.5%
from Federal Funds                          5%             5.5%
30.       The banking structure, where the borrowers are at the same time
the bank's major founders (shareholders), has led to relatively low interest
rates, Furthermore, Federal, Republic and communal funds are provided to
the banks at low cost with predescribed margins for on-lending or are used
to subsidize interest rates. While the Yugoslavs consider low interest rates
on in.restment credits important for encouraging investments and use subsi-
dized rates to channel resources into priority areas, low interest rates,
inter alia, may have contributed to excess demand for investments with in the
past inflationary effects, to the choice of highly capital-intensive produc-
tion techniques, and to overdesign of investment projects. They may have also
negatively influenced resource mobilization. These issues are addressed in an
Interest Rate Study recently completed by Vojvodjanska Banka under the First
Agricultural Credit Project (1129-YU). The Study will be the basis for a dis-
cussion with the Yugoslav Authorities on the impact of the existing interest
rate regime on resource allocation and mobilization in the agricultural sector
and oa, appropriate measures to improve resource allocation and mobilization of
the sector.
31.       Interest rates for long-term loans to agriculture in the two
Republics vary from 5%-5.5% on loans made out of Federal funds up to 11.5% on
loans from the banks' own resources, with most of the lending done at 7%-8%.
Interest rates for investments in priority subsectors may be subsidized to
even lower levels such as 3% for irrigation in Macedonia. These interest
rates are received and could be revised every year. The present interest
rates imply negative real rates given the high rate of inflation (25% in
1974, 18% in 1975 and 11%-12% estimated for 1976).



ANNEX 3
Page 14
B. "13 July" Agrokombinat
General
32.       The "13 July" Agrokombinat (AK) was created in 1963 by amalgamation
of small agricultural and processing units, for development of general agri-
cultural production in Montenegro. It is governed under the self-management
system. Like all kombinats it is an autonomous body with the status of a
legal person and freedom to contract for capital, raw materials and workers.
Its assets are social property, but it has mortgage and sale rights.
Organization and Management
33.       The organization and structure of the AK in presented in WB Chart
No. 16334. The Workers' Council is the apex body which decides the basic pol-
icy for AK. The members of the Workers' Council are elected for a two-year
term from amongst the employees, who are also the collective owners of the AK.
Half the Council members are replaced each year. The Workers' Council elects
three executive boards to deal with different aspects of policy making. The
General Manager is the chief executive of AK and is assisted by a Deputy
General Manager and an Assistant General Manager. All the senior management
is appointed by the Workers' Council. The General Manager is responsible for
day-to-day operations and implements the policies laid down by the Workers'
Council and executive boards. The General Manager is assisted in his work by
a Committee of Managers, composed of Managers of each basic unit of AK. The
General Manager is elected for four years with the possibility of reelection.
34.       The operations of the AK are carried out by 26 economic units called
"Basic Units of Associated Labor" (BOAL). Each BOAL is responsible for a spe-
cific activity, such as plantations, processing, etc. Each BOAL elects repre-
sentatives to the Workers' Council and appoints the Manager for the BOAL. In
addition to the 26 BOALS (see WB Chart 16334), there are three common services
groups serving all the BOALS and the General Manager: Personnel Services,
Administrative Services and Financial Services.
35.       The total number of employees of the AK was 2,792 as at the end of
January, 1976. In terms of qualifications, the distribution was as follows:
Qualification                        Number            % of total
University graduates                    180                 6
Technicians and secondary school        578                 21
Skilled workers                      1,232                 44
Semi-skilled workers                   421                 15
Non-skilled workers                     381                 14
Total                           2,792                100
The number of employees has increased 60% in 1975 as a result of the mills
and bakeries economic units moving into the AK during the year. The AK also
employs seasonal workers for the picking of grapes and fruits.



ANNEX 3
Page 15
Operations
36.       The AK is engaged in a broad spectrum of activities covering primary
agricultural production (crop production, milk and livestock production, and
plantations), agro-processing industries (slaughterhouses, bakeries, fisheries,
flour mills, winery and animal feed unit), and marketing services (coopera-
tive marketing, supermarkets, retail and wholesale sales centers). AK also
has an Agro-Economic Institute which is planning the new developments of the
AK. Besides the Cemovsko Polje sub-project, the AK also plans the following
investments: expansion of existing dairy farm by 400 cows; ham production
factory (1,500 tons per year); carp ponds (140 ha); grapes and fruit nursery
(40 ha); poultry farm (80,000 chickens), and apple orchards (60 ha).
Financial Structure and Performance
37.       The aggregate balance sheet (Table 14) of AK amounted to Din 892.5
million in 1975 up from Din 461.9 million in 1973. The near double increase
was mainly due to new investments and to amalgamation of wheat and corn pro-
cessing units, and flour mills and bakeries, into the AK. Total equity funds
of the AK amounted to Din 329.6 million in 1975 as against Din 110.5 million
in 1972. The fixed assets existing, as well as in progress, increased from
Din 261.47 million in 1973 to Din 490.2 million in 1975. The increase in
fixed assets was financed mainly from long-term loans from banks and the
increase in business funds for the working capital needs depended heavily on
suppliers' credit, and supplemented them with short-term loans from banks.
However, the current assets of the AK exceeded its current liabilities. The
current ratio was 1.1:1.0 in 1975. The equity funds of AK exceed the long-
term borrowings. The long-term debt/equity ratio was 0.7:1.0 in 1975. Both
ratios were satisfactory. AK's financial position although small is sound and
no difficulty is anticipated in meeting its share of 5.4% in project costs.
38.       The financial performance of the AK should be viewed in the context
of the self management system prevailing in Yugoslavia in which the financial
objective of the investor is to maximize the average surplus per worker.
The Net Surplus is arrived at after subtracting from gross earnings: the
operating expenses (minus the salaries), depreciation, legal and contractual
obligations. This Net Surplus is then allocated to pay salaries and to busi-
ness, reserve and housing funds. The Net Surplus of AK increased from Din 45
million in 1973 to Din 119 million in 1975 (Table 15). The average surplus
per worker was Din 39,291 in 1974 and increased to Din 42,651 in 1975. 1975
was an exceptional year since the bakery and flour mill, economic units, were
merged into the AK affecting the financial results of the year. The net
surplus as a percentage of equity was 41% in 1973, rose to 58% in 1974 but
declined to 36% in 1975. The financial performance of AK has been satisfactory.

practices in Yugoslavia. Short-term loans refer to laons with a maturity of up

possible for this exercise but would be attempted by SDK in future. It should
also be noted that the 'Reserve fund', shown in conformity with Yugoslav
practice under equity, has the character of provisions against bad loans.
