APPRAISAL OF
AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES II PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
MONTENEGRO-CEMOVSKO POLJE PROJECT
Project Description
A. THE PROJECT AREA
General Features
1.        Cemovsko Polje is the largest flat area (19,000 ha approx.) within
the Republic of Montenegro (Map No. IBRD 12346). The plain, bordered by hilly
and mountainous limestone formations slopes gently from Titograd toward Lake
Skadar in north-south direction. The Moraca river flows southward along the
western boundaries of the area and its tributary, the Cijevna river traverses
the upper part of the plain. The main railway from Titograd to Bar runs
along the western edge of the area and Titograd airport is located in the
plain.
2.        The Cemovsko Polje is divided into three zones:
(a) Zone A (10,000 ha), the lower part of the plain, extends
over the area periodically flooded by Lake Skadar. At
present Zone A is mainly used for seasonal grazing;
(b) Zone B (4,500 ha) covers the middle part of the plain.
This zone is owned by small holders practising mixed
agriculture on fragmented plots.
(c) Zone C (4,200 ha) lies on the upper part of the plain and is
at present not farmed; recently land rights over about 3,000
ha were transferred from the Titograd municipality to the 13
July Agrokombinat. The proposed project area falls into this
zone.
TOPOGRAPHY, SOILS AND DRAINAGE
3.        Zone C is a flat area with little micro relief and only small
undulations, degrading from the eastern foothills of Cemovsko Polje toward
the central part of the plain with an average slope of about 0.7%. The River
Cijevna crosses Zone C in the direction of maximum slope and cuts the area
into two unequal parts. The area is covered by a gravelly fluvio-glacial
layer. Gravels, which consist of dolomite and limestone, are assorted in
size up to a maximum of 12-15 cm in general.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
4.        Two types of soils are mostly represented in the project area:
(i) The higher Cijevna terrace soils, covering about 72% of
the area, are gravelly brown loams. At about 20 cm, the
gravel content rises to 50-80%. Drainage is good to
excessive.
(ii)  Lower Cijevna soils, covering about 28% of the area, are
similar to the higher terrace soils but their gravel con-
tent is more variable lecreasing with distance from the
river.
5.        The carbon percentages of the top soil vary from 1-2%.  The organic
matter content is not given, but on the basis of the carbon content it appears
to range between 2-3%. The phosphoric acid content has a range from 130 to
460 parts per million and suggests that the level of available phosphorus is
reasonable. Exchangeable potassium in the top soil varies generally from 80
to 160 p.p.m. The total potassiim content is also low and does not exceed
0.240%, indicating that potassiun rich minerals, which may act as a reserve
for replenishing available potas3ium, are absent. The salt content is neg-
ligible.
6.        Based on a soil survey the land has been classified as follows:
Class      Soil description                                           Area in %
I      Limitations seriously -:estricting choice of crops
and requiring measures for improvement                        34.0
II      Very serious limitatioAis severely restricting
choice of crops and reqluiring special cultural
practices                                                     12.0
III      Similar to II but more extreme                                54.0
Total                                            100.0
7.        There are several adverse factors and limitations which make the
project area soils unsuitable for- diversified cropping.  However, based on
the following considerations it :,s concluded that the proposed fruit orchards
and vineyards can be successfull- planted:
(i) Drainage is satisfactory and therefore no problems are
likely to arise in estciblishing deciduous orchards,
vineyards, and peach trees.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
(ii) The areas with cemented subsoil, developed directly on
fluvio-glacial deposits, could be substantially improved
by deep ploughing in order to loosen the cemented layer.
(iii) High gravel content, which severely interfers with annual
crop growing, is not a limiting factor for fruit trees and
vines which have a deep root system.
(iv) The reduced water holding capacity, due to the high skeletal
percentage in the soil, could be overcome by more frequent
irrigation water applications.
(v) The good drainage conditions favor the establishment of
vines and peach trees.
(vi) The low level of available phosphorus and probably avail-
able potassium could be corrected by means of fertilizers
which also contain nitrogen.
(vii) Possible deficiencies of micro-elements such as zinc and
manganese could be easily amended by foliar applications.
8.        Suitability of Zone C for the proposed plantations is amply sub-
stantiated by the results obtained by the Agrokombinat over the last 5 to 6
years in its trial plot (30 ha) representing the average conditions within
the project area.
CLIMATE
9.        The climate in general may be described as Mediterranean with a
strong continental influence as shown by the marked variation between summer
and winter temperatures. The records of Titograd meteorological station,
which cover a period of 35 years, indicate the following (for details see
Annex 4, Tables 3, 4, and 5):
(i: The combined influence of high temperatures, strong
winds and low relative humidity results in high evapo-
transpiration in summer making irrigation essential.
(ii) In order to protect the crop, especially the fruit trees,
from the strong dry winds during summer and to reduce
evapotranspiration and ensure efficient sprinkler opera-
tion, adequate protection should be provided by wind-
breaks.
(iii) Winter frosts present no risk to the deciduous fruit
trees and vines since they flower and come into leaf
well after the middle of March when the frost hazard
is negligible.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
(iv) Hail, causing heavy damage to the production, is frequent.
Therefore crops should be insured against this.
WATER RESOURCES
10.       Surface Water is available from three sources.  First, river Cijevna
which does not ensure enough water for the whole project area. Second, the
river Moraca which has more than adequate discharge for total project purposes.
Third, Lake Skadar has nearly limitless water; its nearest point to the proj-
ect area is about 4 km. The water of both river Moraca and Lake Skadar is
equally suitable for irrigation. The use of either source of water for irri-
gation presents no salinity or alkali hazards. The use of lake water rather
than river water in early summer may prove more beneficial to crops because of
its higher temperature.
11.       Groundwater.  The hydrogeological studies carried out in the past 1/
identified in the Cemovsko Polje two aquifers: (i) an upper aquifer corres-
ponding to the alluvial quaternary fan of the River Cijevna and flowing toward
Lake Skadar with a gradient of I m - 2 m per km; (ii) a lower aquifer in the
underlying bedrock which consists of mesozoic limestone and dolomites highly
karstified and fractured.  This aquifer also flows toward Lake Skadar.  The
aquifer in the quaternary alluvium is recharged mostly by the flow of River
Cijevna, while the water circulating in the mesozoic formation originates
mainly in the mountain area bordering the Cemovsko plain. However, water
interchanges between the two aquifers seem very probable.
12.       The watertable is widespread under the whole plain at a depth
ranging from 40 m to 20 m below the ground level in the upper parts of Zone C,
and from 2 m to 3 m in the lower areas of Zone B where water is drawn by farm-
ers from shallow open wells. The level variation from winter to summer is
about 3 m. Several tube-wells have been drilled into the aquifer, both for
water supply (Titograd and minor towns) and irrigation (Agrokombinat trial
plot and new vineyard plantations): the well discharge varies from 80 to
166 1/sec for drawdown less than 1.50 m.
13.       The available results of laboratory analyses confirm the good water
quality for all uses. The referred hydrogeological studies assessed the safe
yield from the upper aquifer nearly 80 million m3 per year. These figures
are probably on the low side, but the only way to ascertain the actual
potential would be to carry out test and observation for several years.
1/   Namely:  Mise en Valeur de la Plaine de Cemovsko, Avant-projet d'irriga-
tion a partir des eaux souterraines - Tome II. Les Resources Naturelles,
by SATEC AGRAR & HYDROTECNIC, Mars 1971 - OECD Project.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
14.       Should the whole 2,000 ha project area be irrigated by groundwater,
a peak flow of 1.460 m3/sec would have to be abstracted, i.e. about 90% of the
overall groundwater potential assessed so far. This could imply a substantial
lowering of the watertable in Zone B and raise problems with the present water
users, making difficult the further irrigation development by small land
holders in the area. Therefore the proposed limit the use of groundwater
for irrigation in the project area at about 0.600 m3/sec, in order to leave a
sufficient safety margin against possible conflicts with the existing water
uses.
15.       Water requirements.  The water requirements have been calculated by
means of the Blaney-Criddle methods as modified in the FAO Irrigation and
Drainage Paper No. 24 "Crop Water Requirements," and are shown in Table 1.
Based on the analysis of crop water requirements, irrigation would be
necessary in general from June to August. However, it must be expected that
in particularly dry years water applications would start in May. The peak
requirement occurs in July with a demand of 0.61 1/sec x ha. However, since
the system would operate 20 hours per day, it should be sized for a capacity
of 0.73 1/sec x ha.
16.       Total Water Demand and Supply.  According to the gross crop water
requirements shown in Table 1 at full development the average annual water
demand in a year of average distribution of precipitations would be 4230
m3/ha. The overall supply of water from the project works at full develop-
ment would be 2.961 million m3 per year from wells and 5.499 million m3 per
year from Lake Skader.
B. CEMOVSKO POLJE PROJECT - DETAILED FEATURES
17.       The project would provide for:
(i) Irrigation System
A fixed irrigation system (buried asbestos-cement pipes)
would provide water down to 6 ha plots, which would be
served by movable equipment. Ilater for 1,300 ha at an
elevation of 87 m and less would be pumped from the Lake
Skadar into an adjacent reservoir basin of 110 m elevation
from where it would serve the irrigation system by gravity.
The remaining 700 ha of higher than 87 m altitude would be
served by groundwater. Portable equipment would consist of
aluminum pipes,and sprinklers.
(ii) Pumping Equipment and Power Supply
The pumping station at the Lake Skadar would consist of 5
units with a total installed capacity of 950 1/sec. Six wells



ANNEX 4
Page 6
with average discharge of 85 I/sec each would serve an
average area of 116 ha each. Power would be supplied by
installing a 110/10 kv substation and 16.5 km of 10 kv
pnm,cr 1 ines.
(iii) Vineyards and Fruit Trees Establishment
The project would include agricultural equipment for deep
ploughing to a depth of 80 cm and for land preparation and
plantation establishment. The typical layout of the planta-
tions would be a series of rectangular blocks of 200 m x 300 m
(6 ha). Vines would be planted in rows, spaced 2.60 m, with
individual plants 80 cm apart; they would be trained in cordons.
Vines would be of the Richter 99 variety (for wine grapes) and
Cardinal (for table grapes), both grafted on American rootstocks.
Peach trees would be spaced 5 m x 5 m; early varieties that are
currently grown in the Cemovsko Polje plain would be planted.
Fertilizer and manure applications would be adopted to the soil
conditions, requiring high dressings of phosphoric acid and potash
during the establishment period. About eight sprayings against
insect pests and diseases would be necessary. The plantation
establishment would be coordinated with the construction of the
irrigation facilities and would require five years. The annual
schedule is given in Annex 5, Table 14. Windbreaks would be neces-
sary to protect the plantations and to ensure uniformity of water
distribution.
(iv) Infrastructure and Research
Three farm centers for storing equipment and inputs and pro-
viding facilities for field staff would be constructed. About
14 km unpaved service roads alongside the plantation blocks and
a bridge across the Cijevna river would secure easy transportation
and communication. A program of experiments on new fruit tree
varieties as well as on fertilizer application, water require-
ments and pest protection would be implemented.
(v)  Winery
A new winery would be built in Titograd for an annual crushing
capacity of about 15,000 tons of wine grapes and a wine holding
capacity of about 10 million liters. All fermentation, ripening,
blending and storage vats would be of stainless steel. The winery
would include the thermo vinification process and a complete
stabilization plant and bottling facilities for 60% of the produc-
tion. A distillation unit would also be included for producing
brandy from "second press" wine.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
(vi) Fruit and Vegetable Cold Store and Packing Line
A new cold store of a capacity of 3,000 tons would be
built near Titograd. It would be designed to hold chilled
produce such as peaches and table grapes and frozen products,
mostly sour cherries, paprika and carrots. The facility would
include a complete washing, grading and packing line in an ad-
jacent building as well as a blast freezer. The facility would
be built to a modular design for ready expansion. All refri-
gerated storage chambers would be at yard level to facilitate
operating lift-trucks, included in the project.
(vii) Collecting Center
The Agrokombinat, under ooperation arrangements with private
farmers, would collect and pack fresh vegetables from farmers in
Zone B for direct marketing in urban centers through the Agrokom-
binat's existing facilities and for processing. Prices for fresh
vegetables paid to farmers are determined on the basis of the
achieved retail prices; prices for vegetables to be processed
are guaranteed. The collecting center would also store and sup-
ply farm inputs to private farmers, such as fertilizers, pesti-
cides, seeds and animal feed.
The following paragraphs describe the different components of the project.
The detailed investment costs, operation and maintenance costs and financial
projections are presented in Annex 5.
PLANTATION ESTABLISHMENT AND OPERATION
18.       General.  Zone C is divided as follows:
(a) 215 ha on which in the recent years a 30 ha trial plot
has been set up and 150 ha of vineyards established.
(b) 3,117 ha initially intended for plantation development
over 3,000 ha net; this area consists of 7 blocks of
uncultivated land.
The project would establish 2,000 ha net (2,220 ha gross) of vineyards, and
peach and cherry orchards covering the whole blocks named A, B, C and D, and
part of the block named E.
19.       Features of plantations are as follows:
(i) Vineyards
Wine grapes varieties both for "high quality wine" (mostly
Varnac) and for blending wine would cover 1,300 ha altogether.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
The table variety Cardinal would be planted on further 200 ha.
This is an early variety for which there is good demand.
The varieties would be grafted on American rootstocks as a
protection against phylloxera. The vines would be trained
in cordons, with trellises at 15 m intervals to be installed
in the second year of plantation.
(ii)  Peaches.  Early and very early varieties which are currently
grown in the Cemovsko Polje would be planted on 200 ha.
(iii)  Sour cherries covering 300 ha for processing.
(iv)  Windbreaks are advisable to increase the uniformity of
distribution in sprinkler irrigation, reduce evapotranspi-
ration and protect against winds. In addition they would
ensure some protection against frost due to cold northerly
winds. Windbreaks would consist of a double row of cypress
trees. Since the windbreaks are expected to moderate the wind
influence on an area up to a width 10 times the height of the
trees, this protection would be consistent with the typical
layout of plantations.
20.       Plantation materials.  Rooted and grafted vine cuttings, peach and
cherry seedlings as well as young cypress plants would be supplied by local
nurseries out of the project area.
21.       Animal manure.  Provision is made for the application of 20-25 tons
of animal manure per hectare, to be mixed with the soil in the planting holes.
In addition to the organic matter, animal manure would provide 100-125 kg of
nitrogen per hectare, thus reducing the dressing of nitrogen fertilizers
during the initial period.
22.       Green manure and intercropping.  Controlled trials would be carried
out in order to establish the benefit, if any, derived from green manuring.
The cost of green manure would not exceed Din 1,200 per annum. This item has
been included in the estimates of operational costs. The soils in the project
area are not suitable for intercropping and the plantations will be clean
cultivated.
23.       Fertilizers.  The initial and annual dressings of fertilizers for
the various plantations is shown in Table 2. The high dressings of phosphoric
acid and potash during the first two years are necessary. The nitrogen dres-
sings for grape production, although slightly on the low side, are reasonable
in view of the adverse effegct of very high dressings of this nutrient on the
quality of wine. It is probable that the proposed dressings will have to be
further reduced for vineyards intended for the production of "high quality"
wine. There is no evidence of minor amount deficiencies. Fertilizer expe-
riments would be conducted with a view to determining the optimum fertilizer
dressings.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
24.       Deep ploughing and levelling.  Before planting, deep ploughing or
subsoiling to a depth of 70-80 cm is necessary for most of the area in order
to loosen the soft, cemented horizon occurring at various depths in the profile.
Levelling is not needed for sprinkler irrigation but some smoothing may be
necessary to facilitate the use of machinery and farm operations in general
and for the establishment of unpaved service roads.
25.       The vineyards would receive regular treatments against mites and
fungus diseases (mildew, oidium). Pesticides would be applied mechanically.
Picking of grapes would be done by hand. Intensive treatment (up to 8 spray-
ings) would be carried out against fungus diseases and insect pests for fruit
orchards also. Fruit would be picked by hand.
26.       Plantation schedule.  The project plantations would be implemented
in 4 years, according to the following time schedule:
Project year                 1      2      3       4      Total
ha
Wine grapes                200    300    400    400        1,300
Table grapes                -       -      100    100        200
Peaches                     -      100    100      -        200
Cherries                    -      100    100    100         300
Total                    200    500    700    600       2,000
Windbreaks (3%)               6     15      21     18         60
27.       Yields.  The average yields at full production would be:
Wine grapes         14 m ton/ha (in the 7th year)
Table grapes        16 m ton/ha (in the 6th year)
Peaches             16 m ton/ha (in the 6th year)
Sour cherries       13 m ton/ha (in the 7th year)
IRRIGATION FACILITIES
28.       Design Background.  To date several preliminary designs have been
outlined for the irrigation of the Zone C 1/. These studies examined alter-
native irrigation schemes supplied either from the River Moraca or from Lake
Skadar, and also combined solutions with the area partially served by ground-
water. The main conclusions may be summarized as follows:
1/   Namely:
- Avant-projet d'irrigation de la Plaine de Cemovsko,
March 1971, by SATEC-AGRAR & HYDROTECHNIK (OECD Project)
- Investment and Technical Documentation for the Construction
of the Irrigation System "Cemovsko Polje", January 1975, by
Hidrozavod Novi Sad, Volumes I, II and III
- Revised solution of the above, January 1976, Volume IV



ANNEX 4
Page 10
(a) Sprinkler irrigation is the only method suitable for
the local soil conditions.
(b) Irrigation systems using groundwater are considerably
cheaper than those served by surface waters; nevertheless,
the present incertitudes on the actual potential of the
aquifer prevent the whole reclamation area of Zone C from
being served by this source, as the water table in Zone B
could drop, thus creating problems with the present users
and making further irrigation development in the zone dif-
ficult.
(c) Schemes using water diverted from the River Moraca are some-
what more economical than those taking water from Lake Skadar;
preference, however, should be given to the latter source be-
cause of the higher temperature of the water and the risk that
the River Moraca might be polluted in the future.
29.       The proposed general layout of the irrigation scheme over 2,000 ha
net would consist of two separate systems taking water from wells and from
Lake Skadar. The proposed irrigation system, would be comprised of:
(i) The Groundwater Sub-System for 700 ha (i.e. the area to be
planted in the first two years) in the upper parts of the
project area on both banks of the River Cijevna since this
zone is the most prom:ising one for aquifer exploitation.
Water would be drawn from 6 wells.
(ii) The Lake Skadar Sub-System for 1,300 ha net (corresponding
to the area to be planted in years 3 and 4) on the lower
parts of the project area, mostly south of the River Cijevna.
Water would be pumped and lifted from Lake Skadar by means
of a 750 m steel pipe up to a basin located on a saddle of the
eastern hill range which separates the Cemovsko plain from the
lake shore.
(iii) A main pressure line to convey the water from the basin by
gravity flow to the command area and then deliver it to the
distribution networks. The distribution networks layout would
basically remain the same as in the HIDROZAVOD design. The
movable irrigation equipment would consist of conventional
aluminum pipes and sprinklers.



ANNEX 4
Page 11
DESCRIPTION OF THE IRRIGATION WORKS
30.       The irrigation system is designed to supply the average water
requirement of 4,230 m3 per net hectare 1/, including a peak demand of 0.73
1/sec x ha for a daily operation of 20 hrs (para 16). The system flexibility,
however, would allow for a water delivery 20% higher, by operating the irriga-
tion system 24 hours a day.
31.       Wells and pumping equipment.  The groundwater system would supply
511 1/sec of water per 20 hours per day by 6 wells, each supplying an average
discharge of about 85 1/sec 2/. The wells would be 600 mm in diameter and
drilled to an average depth of 60 m below the ground level. The casing
diameter would be 450 mm and screens would be protected by a gravel-packed
filter. A submersible multistage electric pump with automatic control would
be installed in each well and a small pumphouse would be constructed to
accommodate the electrical and hydraulic auxiliary equpment, including air
tanks.
32.       By assuming the dynamic water level 35 m below the groundlevel, 12 m
of head friction losses in the irrigation network, and 40 m of residual pres-
sure at the hydrants, the total head of the pumps would be an average of 87 m,
thus requiring about 100 kW of power which would be supplied by an outdoor
10,000/380 V transformer situated near the pumphouse.
33.       Each well would serve a 116 ha independent block, by means of two
main lines branched off from the pumphouse. These main lines would consist
of Class C asbestos-cement buried pipes, 200-250 mm in diameter. Their total
length would be about 5.025 m or 7.2 m per net hectare served. At the final
design stage, possible interconnections between the networks of contiguous
irrigation sectors should be studied to give the systems more flexibility and
a balanced supply of water under unexpected circumstances.
34.       Lake Skadar Pumping Station.  The intake works would be located at
the bottom of an indentation of the lake, a few hundred meters away from the
Titograd to Albania road. The total capacity of the pumps would be 950 I/sec,
with a total head of 111 m. The pumping station would have the following main
features:
(i) Five multistage electrically driven pump units would be
installed, each having a capacity of 190 1/sec. As the
compensating tank would store the flow during the 4 hours
break of daily irrigation, only 4 pump units out of five
would operate 24 hrs a day in the period of peak water
demand, so that normally there would be one stand-by unit.
1/ For 75% overall efficiency.
2/ Experience of the wells operating in the area shows that, in general, one
well could safely supply 100 1/sec with 0.50 m drawdown.



ANNEX 4
Page 12
(ii) Provision would be made for the auxiliary hydro-mechanical
equipment (grills, suction end pressure pipes, gate valves,
non-return valves, air tanks, etc.), transformers, electric
control devices, etc. The required power would be about
1,550 kW.
(iii) A steel lifting pipe, 900 mm diameter and 750 m long, would
connect the pumping station with the compensation tank. The
pipe would be laid over the ground on concrete saddles at
regular intervals. Civil works would also include the align-
ment levelling and concrete anchorage blocks.
(iv) Compensation tank would consist of a geometrically shaped pool,
formed by an earthfill or rockfill embankment with 3 m crest
width. The internal slopes and bottom of the tank would be
concrete lined. The. net capacity would be about 13,700 m3.
Appurtenance structures would include intake works and bottom
outlet with gates, spillway, fencing, electrical lighting and
access road from the pumping station.
35.       Main pressure line by gravity flow would convey an initial discharge
of 950 1/sec from the compensation tank to the irrigation area and then con-
tinue across the area to the farthest sector on the northern bank of River
Cijevna, by progressively feeding all the distribution networks of the system.
The main line would consist of several stretches formed by Class C asbestos-
cement buried pipes, decreasing in diameter from 900 mm to 200 mm. The total
length would be 15,300 m including two secondary branches, i.e. about 11.8 m
per served hectare. The pipeline design capacity would be sized for each
stretch at the rate of 0.73 1/sec per served hectare, but secondary branches
and terminal sections would convey a multiple of the discharge of the typical
sprinkler fly-line (15 1/sec approx.). Besides the construction of the
pipeline, civil works would include chambers, anchorage blocks and crossing
structures. The River Cijevna would be overpassed with a steel pipe sur-
ported by the planned new road bridge. Hydraulic equipment would include
gate valves, air valves and outlets.
36.       Distribution networks.  The layout of distribution networks would
be the same for both the groundwater and Lake Skadar system. They would con-
sist of parallel pipelines branching off at right angles from the mains. Dis-
tribution lines would run straight alongside alternate north-south service
roads, i.e. at 416 m intervals, each line thus serving two rows of plantation
units. The distribution networks would be formed by Class C asbestos-cement
buried pipes ranging from 250 mm to 150 mm diameter. The design capacity of
the various stretches would be about 15 1/sec every 21 ha net, according to
the typical layout of the sprinkler irrigation equipment.
I/    9501,000   x 4 hrs x 3,600 = 13,680 m3



ANNEX 4
Page 13
37.       There would be about 7,200 m of lines in the 2,000 ha irrigation
area, corresponding to an average of 36 m per net hectare. The hydraulic
equipment would include gate valves, air valves, outlets and 100 mm diameter
hydrants at 100 m intervals. There would be about 525 hydrants, each serving
an average of 3.80 ha net area.
38.       Movable equipment and manpower requirements.  Included in the
project would be conventional sprinkler irrigation equipment which might
gradually be replaced in the future with more advanced and labor saving
devices once experience has been gained to make a decision.
39.       The typical set for vineyards consists of 228 m of 100 mm diameter
aluminum pipes (in lengths of 6 m), including the connection line to the
hy drant, No. 11 double nozzle sprinklers with risers 1.50 m high, hydrant
el bow with handle and manometer, fittings, etc. One length of pipe out
of three would be equipped with stabilizing shoe and quick coupling for
the sprinkler riser. Sprinklers would be spaced 18 m along the line, which
would be placed parallel to the cordons. The interval between two adjacent
settings would be 9 row interspacings, i.e. 25 m approximately.
40.       The following operation features would apply:
-    discharge of one double nozzle sprinkler
5 mm + 7 mm at the working pressure of
3.5 kg/cm2                                         1.41 1/sec
-    total discharge of 11 sprinklers                  15.50 1/sec
-    volume delivered in 1 hour                        55.75 m3/hr
-    area covered by one setting                       5,000 m2
-    precipitation rate                                11.15 mm/hr
-    setting time for an application of 53 mm           4.75 hrs
-    No. of settings in 10 days with 20 hours
operation per day                                    42
-    area covered by one set in the 19 day rotation    24 ha
The same typical set would be adopted in the peach and cherry orchards, where
the diversity of layouts and crops might require some adjustment (height of
risers, spacing and interval of sprinklers, diameter of nozzles, etc.).
41.       For the whole 2,000 ha irrigation area 95 typical sets would be
necessary. Taking into account the time required for displacing the equip-
ment to the next sitting and a reasonable operation flexibility, 2 stand-by
sets for every 5 would be provided. The total number of sets would therefore
be 133.



ANNEX 4
Page 14
42.       At full water development there would be up to 8 water applications
per year for the average of crops. Each shift of the typical set requiring
6 men/hr, the manpower requirement would be:
6 men/hr x 2 settings/ha x 8 water applications = 96 men/hr
The labor need could be somewhat reduced by using pipe trailers towed by
small tractors.
43.       Power lines and annual electricity consumption.  A 10 kV overhead
network 3 x 50 mm2 would be erected to supply electric power to the 6 well
pumping stations. The network length would be about 5.5 km. Another 10 kV
overhead power line 3 x 95 mm2 and 11.0 km long would supply the Lake Skadar
pumping station.  Both power lines would be derived from the existing 100
kV line bordering the northeastern edge of the project area, through a
100/10 kV substation to be erected by the Titograd Electricity Board near
the road bridge on the River Cijevna.
44.       The energy consumption for pressure water delivered by the distri-
bution networks would be as follows:
Groundwater system (700 ha)                            0.35 kWh/m3
Lake Skadar system (1,300 ha)                           0.44 kWh/m3
Weighted average (2,000 ha)                            0.41 kWh/m3
Based on the expected water development schedule the annual energy consump-
tion at full development would be 3,468,600 kWh.
INFRASTRUCTURE
45.       Farm Centers.  Three farm centers would be constructed in the
plantation area for storing equipment and inputs and providing facilities
for the field staff and workers. The four centers would consist of:
Area m2
-    Garages and workshop                                 600
-    Stores for spare parts and offices                   300
-    Canteen, first aid, lavatories, etc.                 300
-    Fences, yards, water and electricity supply
-    3 sheds (galvanized steel structure)              3,000
46.       Roads.  In addition to the unpaved service roads alongside the
plantation units, 1/ a main road network would be constructed to provide
1/   The establishment of these tracks would be carried out in connection
with the land levelling.



ANNEX 4
Page 15
access to the farm centers and make possible easy communications and trans-
ports within the plantation area. The communication system would consist of
14 kms of macadam roads and a bridge on the River Cijevna (400 m2).
47.       Infrastructure implementation would be sub-contracted in the same
context as the construction works of the irrigation scheme. A first set of
buildings and access roads would have to be ready for operation in the 3rd
year, in connection with the first wine grape harvest. The remaining works
would be completed within the 4th year.
AGRICULTURAL EQUIPMENT
48.       The establishment and exploitation of the plantations would be
mechanized except the fruit picking which would be done by hand. The
machinery to be provided under the project would include heavy, medium and
small tractors, trailers and a wide range of implements and minor equipment
and spare parts as well. A part of the small tractors and trailers would
be used to displace the sprinkler irrigation equipment. Medium and small
tractors also would be used for road maintenance. The proposed equipment
would not include standby tractors and implements needed in case of break-
down. These would have to be supplied from the pool already possessed by
the Agrokombinat. The 2 heavy tractors are needed for the heavy initial
operations of land preparation (deep ploughing and levelling of 2,000 ha,
plus 60 ha of windbreaks). The heavy wear on equipment in deep ploughing
and subsoiling has been taken into account in the estimated operating,
maintenance and replacement costs.
49.       The project would also need plastic boxes for transport of table
grapes and fruits to the packing or processing facilities. The number of
boxes needed is 60 per hectare; 24,000 boxes would be required, therefore,
for the 700 ha of table grapes, peaches and cherries under harvest at the
same time.
C. AGRO-INDUSTRIAL SUB-PROJECTS
Titograd-Winery
50.       To process the wine grape production from the 1,300 ha planted
under the project in the Zone C of the Cemovsko Polje plain, the project,
would finance a new winery of a total capacity of about 10 million liters.
The new facility would be located near Titograd and would be owned and
operated by the 13 July Agroindustrial Kombinat. This Kombinat already owns
a small winery (Vinarski Podrum) of 2.6 million liters for processing the
Kombinat's production of grapes from about 400 ha of its presently owned
vineyards.
51.       The 1,300 ha of Zone C will only come into full production of wine-
grapes in year 10, however, to avoid the expense of idle capacity for several
years, the new winery financed under the project, would be designed to absorb



ANNEX 4
Page 16
the winegrape production corresponding to year 7 or about 80% of the total at
full development. By year 9 the winery would have to be expanded or alterna-
tively, the surplus winegrape production would have to be sold fresh. The new
plant would include thermo-vinification to obtain an optimum color of the wine
with a minimum of tanning residues, a biological stabilizing plant and bottling
facilities for about 60% of the production, and a distillery for producing
brandy from the wine obtained after second pressing. Production of other by-
products would not be economically justified for a winery of this capacity.
All tanks would be made of stainless steel.
52.       About 10% of the wine would be sold for export in 0.7 lt bottles
under the category "Appelation Controllee", 50%, also in bottles, would be
sold locally and the remaining 40% of the volume would be sold in bulk to
other wineries, mostly located in the Republic of Serbia, for blending. See
Annex 2 for further details on the marketing of the wine to be produced under
the project. After integrating all investments and income and operating costs
from all the three components involved in this sub-project, namely: (a) the
irrigation and infrastructure corresponding to the 1,300 ha; (b) the planting
and development of the 1,300 ha of vineyards; (c) the winery the overall
combined financial rate of return of winegrape growing and processing is
estimated to be 13%. The winery would offer employment on a permanent basis
to 71 persons and on a seasonal basis to 100 persons.
Titograd - Cold Store and Packing Plant
53.       The Project would finance the construction of a new cold store of
3,000 tons capacity. It would include fruit and vegetable washing, grading
and packing facilities placed in a separate building. The cold store would
include a blast freezer. The plant would be owned and operated by the 13
July Agro-Industrial Kombinat and would be located near the city of Titograd,
adjacent to an existing cold-store, of 2,000 tons capacity, also owned by
the Kombinat, but used mostly for storing meat, butter and fresh fruits. The
new facilities would be provided essentially to serve the incremental pro-
duction of peaches, tablegrapes and sour cherries from the new plantations
in Zone C of the Cemovsko Polje field, financed under the project. However
increased production of vegetables such as paprika and carrots, from the
existing private farms in Zone B of the Cemovsko Polje field, would also be
channelled through the new complex.
54.       Obviously, only part of the incremental production would be kept
in cold store and be graded and packed, as a large share is expected to be
sold fresh directly at the markets. Peaches decay promptly and would only
be kept in storage for a maximum 2 weeks, tablegrapes about 3 to 4 months
and sour cherries, paprika and carrots would be frozen and carried in freezer
store for about 2 to 4 months. The income and operating cost projections
indicate the estimated proportions of produce that would be sold fresh and
that would pass through the cold or freezer store and be sold graded and



ANNEX 4
Page 17
packed. Also in this case the investment projections shown in Annex 5, Table
1 as well as the income and operating cost table, integrate the three compo-
nents involved in this sub-project, namely:
(a) the irrigation and infrastructure corresponding to 200 ha
of peaches, 300 ha of tablegrapes and 200 ha of sour cher-
ries;
(b) the planting and development of these total 700 ha;
(c) the cold store, blast freezer and grading and packing plant.
A collecting center of about 500 m2 for the increase production of vegetables
from Zone B (private sector), has also been included. The resulting combined
financial rate of return for the production and cold storing and packing of
the aforementioned produce would be about 17.65%. The size of the new cold
store has been determined taking into account two factors: (i) lessen the
cost of idle capacity during the development years when production is still
comparatively low, and (ii) the demand in the domestic market for the more
expensive cold stored and packed produce is not very well known and must be
tested cautiously.
55.       Trade in frozen vegetables in Montenegro as well as the rest of
Yugoslavia is limited by inadequate storage and transport facilities. Shop
storage is also lacking. The export market fQr the frozen cherries, however,
would appear to be promising as can be seen in the following table:
Frozen Fruits (tons '000)
Importing Countries
1970     1972      1975     1980
West Germany                      30       48        51       60
Netherlands                        9       12        10       11
France                             7.3      8         9       11
Austria                            2.9      6.3       7        8
United Kingdom                     4.5      5.6      10       12
Sweden                             6.3      4.1       4.6      5.6
Switzerland                        3.4      6.1       6.3      7.0
95% of West Germany's imports are frozen fruits without sugar which includes
frozen sour cherries.  Prospects for Yugoslavian export of frozen vegetables
have been reviewed in Annex 2.  The cold store would be built following a
modular pattern so it could be readily expanded if and when required. The
cost of any such expansion has not been included and would not be financed
under the project. The new facility would give permanent employment to about
68 persons and seasonal employment to an additional 80 persons.



ANNEX 4
Page 18
D. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
56.       A Project Unit, organized as a Basic Organization of Associated
Labor (BOAL), would be created within the 13 July Kombinat for the imple-
mentation of the project in Montenegro. The Unit would have the following
responsibilities:
(a) Preparation of final designs and tender documents for all
engineering works to be subcontracted (irrigation systems,
infrastructure and processing facilities).
(b) Preparation of list and specifications of all equipment
to be procured through ICB (pumps and electro-mechanical
equipment, movable equipment for sprinkler irrigation,
agricultural equipment, processing plant equipment, etc.)
or through local tenders (seedlings, fertilizers, etc.).
(c) Award of subcontracts and procurement contracts.
(d) Management, supervision and administration of the project
implementation, and operation during the first 6 years.
57.       The Project Unit will have four Division:  Engineering, Plantations,
Processing Plants and General. Under the overall control of the General
Manager of the AK, there will be a Project Director heading the Project Unit
and each of the Divisions will be headed by a Manager who will be responsible
to the Project Director. The Manager (Engineering Division) would be respon-
sible for implementation, operation and maintenance of all irrigation and
infrastructure works. The Manager (Plantations) would be in charge of im-
plementation, operation and maintenance of the vineyard and fruit plantations
and farm machinery and agricultural equipment. The Processing Plants Division
will carry out the erection of winery and cold and packing plant and will
operate these facilities. The General Division will provide the accounting,
administrative, and specialist services for procurement. The staff of the
Unit would include qualified irrigation and agricultural machinery engineers,
agronomists and horticulturists, economists, administrative staff and procure-
ment specialists. Final designs of engineering works would be prepared by
Yugoslav consultants, assisted by outside experts. Also, part-time consul-
tants would assist the permanent technical staff of the operational units for
the supervision of special works and supplies.
58.       The Unit would have an Executive Board consisting of:  General
Manager of the Agrokombinat, who will also be the President of the executive
board; Director and Division Chiefs of the Project Unit; Director of the
Internal Bank of the Agrokombinat; Representative of the Secretary of Agricul-
ture; a representative of IBT; and the Director of the Agro-Economic Institute



ANNEX 4
Page 19
of the Agrokombinat. The executive board would approve the Unit's operating
policies and procedures and supervise the project implementation. It would
also play a coordinating role between the Agrokombinat, the Secretariat of
Agriculture, IBT and IBRD. After the Project's implementation, the facili-
ties would be transferred to the Agrokombinat's respective operating units.
These units are adequately organized and staffed, and follow satisfactory
policies to warrant efficient operation of the Project facilities.
January 1977

ANNEX 6
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURE AND AGRO-INDUSTRIES II PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
MACEDONIA PROJECT
Project Description
A. Project Components
Social Sector
(i) Dairy Plant
A new milk processing plant of a capacity of 40,000 liters/
day would be built in Prilep in replacement of an existing
obsolete plant of 10,000 liters/day. The facility would
produce cream and butter and pasteurized milk from cows.
The milk would be packed in one liter plastic bags. Equip-
ment would also be provided to produce yoghurt and cheese,
mostly from sheep milk. Milk collection would be organized
and chilling centers and insulated tanker-trucks would be
provided under the project. The lack of organized milk col-
lection is the main disincentive to increased milk production
in the Prilep valley.
(ii) Slaughtering Plant
A new slaughtering plant for an annual capacity of 300,000
lamb and sheep, and 12,000 cattle would be built in Gostivar
in replacement of an existing obsolete abattoir. The new
plant would represent an increase in capacity of about 30%
over the existing facility which cannot provide the required
standard of hygiene for slaughtering and chilling of the ap-
proximately 80,000 lambs exported annually. The new plant
would also include chillrooms and facilities for processing
edible and inedible by-products. It would be designed to
allow slaughtering according to Moslem regulations.
(iii) Potato Cold Store
A new potato cold store of a capacity of 5,000 tons would be
built in Delcevo. The facility would be built above ground,
of the "door-per-bin" design, and would includeltemperature
and humidity controls, and ventilation. An adjacent building
with basic facilities for washing, grading, sizing and bagging
the potatoes would also be provided. Included would be trucks
for harvesting and marketing distribution, pilers for storing
and scoop-trucks for transferring from storage to packing.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
(iv) Fruit and Vegetable Processing Plant
The project would finance major improvements and expansion
of the existing fruit and vegetable processing plant in
Gevgelija. Present processing operations would be upgraded
by reconditioning buildings to stricter sanitary standards
and replacing obsolete and insanitary processing equipment.
The present annual capacity of about 12,000 m tons would be
expanded to a total 20,000 m tons. This expansion would
involve the construction of a new building with a new line
for processing tomatoes, and also increasing the capacity of
the existing lines for processing fruits.
(v) Dehydrated Vegetable Plant
The project would finance a new plant for producing tomato
paste and dehydrating such vegetables as onions, peppers,
carrots, leeks and parsley. The capacity of the plant would
be about 50,000 m tons of vegetables annually, of which 30,000 m
tons would be tomatoes. The other vegetables would be processed
on conventional lines; including vegetable preparation, conveyorized
drying tunnels, packing and storage. The tomato processing facili-
ties would allow for the installation at a later date of a tomato
spray drying cone to produce tomato powder.
(vi) Apple Processing Plant
A new processing plant for apples of an annual capacity of
10,000 m tons would be built near Resen. The plant would have
the necessary facilities to process reject grade apples into such
products as apple juice concentrate, pure nectar, concentrated
aroma and distilled alcohol. No cold store facilities are en-
visaged as a new unit of 6,000 m ton capacity would be available
to the plant for storing raw material.
Private Sector
(vii) Fruit Production
Subloans would be provided to about 220 private farmers for
on-farm development of about 1 ha each of orchards and vineyards.
Included would be such fruits for which the domestic or export
demand is strong, e.g. peaches, apples, apricots and sour cherries.
Investments would include land preparation, planting materials,
fertilizer and labor.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
(viii) Vegetable Production
About 20 farmers would receive subloans for the construc-
tion of plastic greenhouses to develop labor intensive,
early vegetable production, mainly tomatoes, peppers and
cucumbers. Investments would include tubing, plastic for
covering and other construction items. Increased production
would predominantly supply the domestic market.
(ix) On-Farm Mechanization
About 120 subloans would be made to private farmers to fi-
nance tractors and implements such as trailors, ploughs,
tillers, rollers and harrows, as illustrated in the
mechanization model, Annex 7, Table 29. Farmers are ex-
pected to cultivate, apart from their own land, neighboring
farms against service charges. Mechanization combined with
increased use of fertilizer and protective agents are one of
the most effective measure to improve productivity in the
private sector.
(x) Livestock Production
The project would provide for the purchase of about 300 in-
calf heifers and dairy cows, primarily Simmenthal, Montafon
and Friesian breeds, and about 20,000 ewes of Zcabatsha,
Pcowidizka and Merino breeds. The private farmer subborrowers
would purchase the animals from social sector enterprises.
Investments would also include construction of winter housing,
feeding facilities and pasture improvement to increase fodder
production.
(xi) Tobacco Drying Facilities
Credit would be provided for the construction of four tobacco
drying sheds of 200 m ton drying capacity each of Virginia to-
bacco. The single story building would be divided into a
receiving area, drying chambers and humidifying room. Hot air
would be forced through sub-floor ducts from the furnaces to the
drying chambers. Investments would be phased over two years.
Market Center Feasibility Study
The marketing study undertaken under the First Agroindustries
Project-Macedonia (894-YU) recommended the establishment of a wholesale
market center for agricultural products. The Government of Macedonia has
accepted the proposal and intends to build a marketing center in or near
Skopje. The center would collect from various parts of Macedonia mostly
fruits, vegetables, meat and dairy products, process the produce and market



ANNEX 6
Page 4
it in Yugoslavia and abroad. Under the project US$100,000 would be provided
to finance the foreign exchange cost of the feasibility study for the con-
struction and operation of the market center.
B. Detailed Description of Agroindustries Sub-Projects
Prilep - Dairy Plant
1.        With some fluctuations, milk production, in Yugoslavia and Macedonia,
has been increasing gradually as can be seen in the following table:
Milk Production (lts '000)
Yugoslavia        Macedonia
1960                                 2,434                 91
1965                                 2,400                 75
1970                                 2,655                 95
1971                                 2,650                 93
1972                                 2,879                 97
1973                                   -                  105
1974                                                      112
Source: Statistical Yearbooks of Yugoslavia and Macedonia,
1975.
The share of sheep milk has dropped from about 8% in 1960 to 5% in 1972 for
all of Yugoslavia, however for Macedonia it remains high although it has also
dropped from 43% in 1960 to 39% in 1972.
2.        The Macedonian social sector operates dairy farms mostly with ex-
otic breed (Holstein) yielding 2,100/3,900 lts/lactation and therefore ac-
count for over 30% of the production. The private farmers own mostly cows of
indigenous breed (busha) yielding only about 1,000/1,200 Its per lactation.
Yugoslavia is a net importer of fresh milk, which is acquired mostly from
Bulgaria. Macedonia is also a deficit milk producer, situation which has
been aggravated by a growing demand of milk and dairy products. In fact the
demand is projected to increase from 75 lts annually per capita in 1970 to
110 in 1980. Milk prices in Macedonia are comparatively high (Din 3.50/lt
equivalent to US$0.19/lt) by world standards and allow the farmer to buy feed
and concentrate which is available at about Din 2.4/kg. Artificial insemina-
tion is generally available through the extension services of the Ministry of
Agriculture, however the main constraints to increased milk production are the
low yielding cows in the private sector and the lack of organized collection
of the milk from the numerous small producers in this sector.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
3.        In the valley of the city of Prilep in Macedonia, there are about
12,000 cows and 120,000 ewes. The total average daily milk production can
be estimated at about 35/40,000 lts. At present, the Prilep Agro-Industrial
Kombinat operates only one small dairy plant of a capacity of about 8,000
lts a day. This plant is obsolete and unhygienic and too small to be ex-
panded effectively. In view of the foregoing, the project would finance the
construction of a new dairy plant to be operated and owned by the Prilep
Kombinat. The plant would have a capacity of 40,000 lts a day and facilities
to produce pasteurized milk, butter, cream, yoghurt and cheese. The financial
rate of return would be about 17%. The new plant would give permanent employ-
ment to about 57 persons. The existing plant would be closed down.
Gostivar - Cattle and Sheep Slaughtering Plant
4.        Yugoslavia's and Macedonia's cattle population declined in the late
sixties. However since 1971 there has been a gradual increase in this sector
which is over 90% in private hands.
TOTAL CATTLE (Head '000)
Macedonia
as percent
Yugoslavia      Macedonia      of Yugoslavia
1960                           5709            336           (5.9%)
1965                            -              290
1970                            -              282
1971                            -              288
1972                            -              302
1973                           5681            320           (5.6%)
1974                            -              334
5.        The offtake is high:  25%, and close to 50% of the stock slaughtered
are males up to six months of age. Nearly 40% of the cattle are of the Sim-
menthal breed, the balance is a mixture of indigenous breeds, with predo-
minance of the Bosnian "Busha" type. Yugoslavia and Macedonia, both show a
trend toward increasing the proportion of dairy cattle (dual purpose) at the
expense of strictly beef cattle. Lack of feed has been the main constraint to
livestock growth in general. However, with funds being channelled into irriga-
tion and to the private sector, more fodder is expected to be made available
for breeding dairy cows and fattening males. The financial incentives for
this are expected to come from increased demand of milk, dairy products, and
meat, mainly in the local market of Macedonia. Consumption of beef and veal,
in Yugoslavia, increased from 6.7 kg/capita in 1960 to 8.5 kg/ capita in 1972.
Macedonia has no surplus cattle for export, either on-the-hoof or as beef, and
does not share in Yugoslavia's beef and veal exports which in 1972 amounted to
90,000 tons or 37% of the total production. Yugoslavia is also an important
exporter of live cattle, however exports, mostly to the EEC countries, have
declined during the recent years.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
6.        The sheep population in Yugoslavia has declined slightly, however,
in Macedonia it has remained stationary with some fluctuation over the years
as can be seen from the following statistics:
TOTAL SHEEP AND LAMB (Head '000)
Yugoslavica                             Macedonia
1960                  10,842                1960               1,802
1961/64               10,090                1965               1,804
1965/68               10,068                1970               1,863
1969/72                8,444                1971               1,828
1972               1,903
1973                   7,852                1973               1,810
1974                     -                  1974               1,859
The main constraint to increased production has been the decreasing areas
available for grazing. With increased areas under irrigation, flocks have
to move further up on the mountain slopes and trek longer distances to graze.
Increased demand with escalating prices have been strong incentives for the
private sector to keep up production. The private farmers account for over
90% of the total flock. Mutton and lamb retail prices increased from Din
15.40/kg in 1970 to Din 30/kg in 1973, and are currently up to Din 45/kg.
EXPORTS (Tons)
Live sheep and lamb       Mutton and lamb
1960                                 9,324                    2,174
1965                                 1,884                    4,301
1970                                 3,703                    3,001
1971                                 5,329                    2,677
1972                                 2,035                    3,426
1973                                    44                    2,923
7.        Most exports from Macedonia are in the form of frozen spring lambs
slaughtered at the plant owned and operated by the GORNI POLOG Agro-Industrial
Kombinat in the city of Gostivar. The plant at Gostovar is obsolete and un-
hygienic by modern sanitary standards. In fact under the present unhygienic
working conditions it risks loosing its export markets. The main markets for
mutton and lamb in the world are as follows:



ANNEX 6
Page 7
MUTTON AND LAMB MARKETS (Tons '000)
1970      1980
United Kingdom                                       333        359
Japan                                                111       220
Greece                                                24         80
USA                                                   38         75
Canada                                                32         40
France                                                32         70
Italy                                                  7        20
Source: Conrad Jameson and Associates, Marketing Study of
Agricultural Products.
From this table it may be concluded that the present markets for the
Macedonian lamb will probably remain open.
8.        In view of the foregoing, the project would finance the construc-
tion of a new cattle and sheep slaughtering plant with a moderate increase
in capacity by means of an extended second shift, to slaughter annually up
to 12,000 head of cattle and 300,000 sheep and lamb. This would allow to
continue yearly exports valued at about US$1.7 million and retain employment
of about 138 persons. The financial rate of return is estimated to be 17%.
Dolcevo - Potato Cold Store and Packing Plant
9.        Dolcevo is in the extreme eastern part of Macedonia near the
Bulgarian border. It is linked with the rest of Macedonia by a paved road,
however, the region has no significant industries and job opportunities
are scarce. Comparatively it is one of the least developed regions in the
Republic. The region has good potential for growing potato, however, lack
of storage and marketing facilities have been a strong disincentive. Most
farmers grow, therefore, wheat as best alternative crop. With a yield of
5 tons/ha and at Din 2,300/ton the gross income from wheat is about Din 1,500/
ha, whereas for potato if would be Din 26,000/ha, on the basis of a yield
of 20 ton/ha at a price of Din 1,300/ton.
10.       The Ovce Pole Kombinat which has a branch in Delcevo has also 300
ha under wheat which would be switched to potato, on a normal 4 year rotation
basis, if the marketing facilities were available. The whole region has
about 14,000 ha of arable land and currently only 250 ha are under potato.
Yugoslavia's production of potatoes has dropped over the last decade: with
Macedonia's share slowly growing to over 3%.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
PRODUCTION OF POTATO (Tons)
Yugoslavia      Share of Macedonia
1960                             3,272,000              2.5%
1965                             2,383,000              2.3%
1970                             2,963,000              2.8%
1972                             2,406,000              3.2%
Source: Marketing Study of Agricultural Products, loc. cit.
In Yugoslavia, 90% of the potato production is retained by the producers and
used as follows: for the producers own consumption: 50%; for cattle feed:
20%; and to be used as seed: 20%. Only about 10% reaches the market.
Yugoslavia has basically been self sufficient in potato production.  Imports
have been negligible but in 1970 exports reached 70,000 tons and 33,200 tons
in 1972. Exports are expected to reach 50,000 tons in 1980, mostly to
Czechoslovakia, Greece and Italy.
11.       By providing a 6,000 ton potato cold store at Delcevo with a potato
washing, grading and bagging line and trucks to deliver the produce to the
Macedonian markets on a year-round basis, an additional 350/400 ha could be
grown under potato which could benefit about 700/800 farmers. Assuming that
each farmer would grow an average 1/2 ha on a 4 year rotational basis. The
plant itself would employ about 85 persons including seasonal and permanent
staff. The cold store would be of conventional design, so other produce
could also be stored. Movable pilers and scoop trucks, for transferring
potato from storage to packing, would be included. The cold store would be
equipped with temperature and humidity controls and ventilation. As a com-
merical enterprise the cold store would be owned and operated by the Agro-
Industrial Kombinat Ovce Pole. The financial rate of return would be about
13%.
Gevgelija - Expansion of Fruit and Vegetable Processing Plant
12.       With increased areas under irrigation, and other incentives, pro-
duction of vegetables increased considerably during the last years, as has
been indicated in Annex 2. Increased production of vegetables also warrants
increased capacity for processing. There are only eight fruit and vegetable
processing plants in Macedonia and most of them process only fruit. One of
these plants is owned and operated by the Agroindustrial Kombinat Interpirex
Gora and is located in the city oE Gevgelija. Gevgelija is located in the
south eastern part of Macedonia and is a rich fruit and vegetable producing
area, particularly of melons, tomatoes, paprika, onions and carrots. The
total current production is estimated at about 65,000 tons annually. The
plant in Gevgelija has a capacity for processing annually up to 12,000 tons
of fruits and vegetables. Tomato concentrate accounts for more than 50% of
the production and the balance are mostly preserved and frozen vegetables
and processed fruits.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
13.       The plant is 20 years old and shows evident signs of neglected
maintenance. In fact unless some major repairs are carried out to both
buildings and equipment, production would suffer. On the other hand the do-
mestic and export market for processed fruits and vegetables appear promising
for Macedonia. The natural export market for Yugoslavia are the industrial
countries of Western Europe with the German Federal Republic as the foremost
importer of most types of processed and frozen fruits and vegetables. Follow-
ing is a brief analysis of the imports of the Western European nations during
the recent years and the forecast up to 1980.
14.       Processed vegetables:  In spite of strong competition from frozen
and dehydrated products, the consumption of canned and bottled vegetables
has tended to increase and is expected to grow in the forseeable future.
CANNED VEGETABLES (Tons '000)

In 1972, processed tomato represented 34% of all imports followed by beans:
15% and peas: 10%, vinegar-pickled vegetables in cans and jars represented
8.5% or a total 91,000 tons.
15.       West European imports of brine-pickled vegetables have also grown
from 15,900 tons in 1960 to 33,000 tons in 1972 and are expected to continue
such a trend. The main importing countries are listed below:

Pickled vegetables are mostly cucumbers and onions but include also other
vegetables.
16.       Frozen vegetables:  trade in frozen vegetables in Macedonia and
Yugoslavia is limited by inadequate storage and transport facilities. Shop
storage in particular is lacking, and retail sales are almost entirely res-
tricted to self-service food stores. Frozen vegetable consumption is there-
fore very low in Yugoslavia, it is estimated at between 1,000 and 2,000 tons/
year.  However by 1980 this could well reach about 5,000 tons/year.  Exports
markets offer great potential however.

Source:  Marketing Study of Agricultural Products, loc. cit.
The European trade in frozen vegetables has been increasing at a fast rate.
Total annual imports doubled between 1965 to 1970. Most of the industrial
nations in Western Europe are expected to offer market potential for exports



ANNEX 6
Page 11
of Macedonian frozen vegetables. The frozen vegetables consumed in Western
Europe are mostly peas, spinach, beans, brussel sprouts, however other types
such as carrots, leeks, paprika, sweetcorn, mushroom and asparagus are becom-
ing increasingly popular, particularly in the German Federal Republic.
17.       In view of the foregoing, the project would finance the expansion
of the fruit and vegetable plant in Gevgelija to an annual capacity of 20,000
tons of raw fruits and vegetable, including major repairs to the buildings
and equipment of the existing plant. The construction of a new plant is not
considered justified. The financial rate of return would be about 17%. The
expansion of the plant would give employment to 65 persons on a permanent
basis and 20 seasonally.
Sveti Nikole - Vegetable Drying Plant
18.       The Agro-Industrial Kombinat Ovce Pole owns 28,000 ha near the city
of Sveti Nikole in Central Macedonia. About 18,000 ha are arable land of
which 10,000 ha are under irrigation mostly for production of such vegetable
as tomato, onion, leek, paprika, carrot, and parseley. Other crops are peas,
late sunflower, barley and corn for silage. The potential for vegetable pro-
duction is considerable. The objective of this sub-project is to process
annually about 40,000 tons of vegetables into tomato paste and produce dehy-
drated onion, leek, paprika, carrot and parsley, all strongly oriented to the
export market.
19.       Although there is strong competition for the market of tomato con-
centrate, the demand by the main importing countries is expected to increase
substantially as shown in the following table, which would assure a market
for Macedconia's production of tomato concentrate:
IMPORTING COUNTRIES OF PROCESSED TOMATO (Tons '000)

70*       In the last few years there has been a growing demand for dehydrated
Teq ctables, mainly from the institutional trade and the soup and ready made
meal industries.  As stated the Macedonian production would be oriented mainly
to the export market and here also the industriai--Mountries of Western Europe
would offer the best marketing prospects. Following are the past and pro-
jected demand of dehydrated vegetables of the main importing nations:

In 1972, the main product was dehydrated onion which accounted for 40% of all
imports.
21.       In view of the foregoing, the project would finance the construction
of a new plant for producing tomato concentrate and dehydrated vegetables with
a total annual capacity of 45,000 tons of raw material. The layout would
allow installation of spray drying facilities for production of tomato powder
in the future. The plant would be located near the city of Sveti Nikole and
would be owned and operated by the Agro-Industrial Kombinat Ovce Pole. The
permanent staff would be about 201 persons and the seasonally employed, ap-
proximately 60 persons. The financial rate of return would be about 17%.
Resen - Apple Processing Plant
22.       Production of apples has been increasing in Yugoslavia and also
in Macedonia according to the following statistics:
PRODUCTION OF APPLES (Tons)
Year                        Yugoslavia                       Macedonia
1960                          158,000                        22,700 ton
1965                          135,000                        25,200
1970                          277,000                        49,800
1972                          309,000                        54,300
1973                             -                           76,200
1974                                                         53,800
Source: Marketing of Agricu:Ltural Products, loc. cit.



ANNEX 6
Page 13
Total production is predicted to reach 450,000 tons in 1980. Exports of fresh
apples have been insignificant reaching only 9,500 tons in 1972, however the
market for processed apples and apple products is good with the German Federal
Republic appearing as the main potential importer. Relevant statistics are as
follows:
GERMAN FEDERAL REPUBLIC
Total Imports of Unconcentrated Apple and Pear Juice (Tons)
1970                        8,600
1971                       11,300
1972                       14,100
Total Imports of Unconcentrated Apple and Pear Juice (Tons)
1970                       14,500
1971                       16,500
1972                       17,600
Source:  Jabolko Agro-Industrial Kombinat.
23.       The south western area of Macedonia is the main apple producing
region of the Republic. There are a total 3,000 ha under apples in the re-
gion including 600 ha of the Prespansko Jabolko Agro-Industrial Kombinat
(social Sector). The yield in the social sector averages 30 tons/ha and in
the private sector 15 tons/ha, the overall annual production can therefore be
estimated around 54,000 tons. The Kombinat owns a 9,000 ton cold store and
has just completed an additional unit of 6,000 tons. There are also four more
refrigerated depots in the nearby cities of Struga, Ohrid, Bitola and Kicev
with a total capacity of 17,000 tons. The region has however no facilities
for processing the industrial grade of apples that come off the packing lines
of the cold stores and constitutes about 20/25% of the volume. These apples
are generally sold in bulk and command a low price due to lack of a market.
In fact the nearest important apple processing plant is located in Serbia
and this plant is well supplied for apples within that Republic and seldom
requires to pay for the extra freight in procuring raw material all the way
from Resen.
24.       In view of the foregoing, the project would finance the construction
of a new apple processing plant to be built near the city of Resen owned and
operated by the Prespansko Jabolko Kombinat and located near their existing
cold stores. The plant would have a capacity to process 12,000 tons annually
into apple sauce, juice and concentrate, obtaining also alcohol and aromatic
concentrate as by products. The production would be predominantly for export
to the German Federal Republic which is a growing market for processed fruits
and vegetables in general. The financial rate of return would be about 14%.
The plant is expected to employ 71 persons permanently and 60 in a seasonal
basis.
January 1977

gency of 5% has been included in the investment costs. All rate of return calcu-
lations are based on projections in real terms. Particular assumptions made
for each model are listed in the footnotes of the respective Tables.
All cash-flow projections have-been made on the basis of 11% on IBRD funds
and 7.25% on local funds. Interest during construction is financed under the loan
loans and the loans are amortizied by annuities.

ANNEX 11
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AGRICULTURE AND AGROINDUSTRIES II PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Economic Rate of Return of the Project
1.        The economic rates of return for the Macedonian and Montenegrin
projects were calculated on the basis of quantifiable economic costs and bene-
fits generated. Costs and benefits are based on the representative investment
models as described in Annexes 5 and 7, and were evaluated at shadow prices as
discussed below and shown in Table 1 of this Annex. Training and administra-
tive expenses were also included. Results derived from the analysis are con-
sidered conservative since some benefits attributable to the project, such as
improved resource allocation in the agricultural and agroindustries sector and
the health benefits to consumers of hygienically processed food, were deemed
non-quantifiable and, therefore, were excluded from the economic rate of re-
turn determination.
2.        The economic rate of return calculations are based on the follow-
ing assumptions:
(a) Output Prices. For wheat and maize the Bank's commodity
world price projections (in constant 1976 prices) were
used as basic data, thus eliminating the subsidy on wheat
in Yugoslavia. The price of milk was reduced by the sub-
sidy of Din. 0.5 per kg. The remaining products were ad-
justed in accordance with the consumption conversion fac-
tor (see Table I of this Annex).
(b) Input Prices. Investment items were priced at constant
1976 prices. Import duties levied on imported components
were deducted. Other equipment items were adjusted in
accordance with the conversion factor for investments.
The prevailing subsidy for fertilizer was added. Other
input prices were converted at the conversion factor for
intermediates (Table 1 of this Annex).
(c) Labor. Hired labor has been valued at the shadow wage
rate for skilled and unskilled labor respectively (Table I
of this Annex).



ANNEX 11
Page 2
(d) Processing Investments. Benefits were calculated on the
basis of value added through processing, adjusted with the
consumption conversion factor. Benefits due to reduced
losses were added.
(e)  Administrative and Training Costs.  Salaries and other
costs incurred by the borrowing banks and implementing
kombinats were added, valued at shadow wage rates.
3.        Based on the assumptions enumerated above, the economic rates of
return were calculated for the respective components (see Table 2 of this
Annex). The Montenegrin project would generate a 17.3% rate of return; and
the Macedonian project 25.6%. These rates of return do not take account
of the non-quantifiable benefits resulting from the contribution of the
project to improved resource allocation in the agricultural and agroindustries
sectors through training and institution building. Sensitivity tests were
conducted to assess the stability of the respective rates of return to varia-
tion in project cost and benefit streams. The variations are not expected to
exceed 10% of the base estimate. The results are:
Montenegrin Component Macedonian Component
Investment Costs    +10%                  16.0%                     23.2%
Operating  Costs    +10%                  15.9%                     19.8%
Benefits             -10%                 14.4%                     17.0%
Both projects' rates of return are most sensitive to variations of benefits.
However, even under a pessimistic assumption of 10% lower benefits, the rates
of return remain satisfactory.
January 1977

ANNEX 12
